A GRAIN
OF SAND
The Story of
One Man and
an Island
BRENDON
RIMSHAW

PAGE_002  (source: IMG_1352.heic  confidence: 100%  words: 293)
============================================================

Millions dream of owning a desert island
and getting away from it all. Brendon
Grimshaw is one of the rare few to make the
dream a reality. For more than quarter of a
century he has lived alone on the tiny, palm-
covered tropical island of Moyenne.
There, helped by his own 'Man Friday',
this latter-day Robinson Crusoe has created
a unique way of life a thousand miles from
anywhere in the middle of the Indian Ocean.
Stripped to the waist, amid a mass of lush
green vegetation, surrounded by white
sandy beaches and a protecting coral reef,
Brendon spends his days under the equato-
rial sun in search of a fabulous fortune - a
buried hoard of pirate's treasure said to be
More than 200 years ago the grey, granite
rocks of his tropical paradise, in the calm,
azure waters of Seychelles St Anne National
Marine Park, were the secret hideaway of
one of the legendary pirates who plundered
wealthy trading vessels plying between the
Indies and Europe and raided the coasts of
Arabia and Asia.
But when Brendon, a former newspaper
editor, first 'discovered' Moyenne it was a
waterless land overgrown with scrub. In the
course of thirty years he has transformed it
into a paradise on earth'.
How he did it, and the adventure, excite-
ment, drama and comedy of his encounters
with black magic, ghosts, sharks and other
strange creatures of the deep in the offshore
coral gardens, a coup, a mercenary raid,
army-mutiny and the never-ending search
for treasure, make compelling and fascinat-
ing reading.

Author's Note
Personally, I don't know of anyone else who owns and lives on a tropical island,
often totally alone. In fact, I have never met anyone who has done so either. But
most visitors to my island home consider it their ultimate dream and usually
ask if I have written a book about it all. So, what better way to mark my 30th
anniversary with Moyenne than to share my way of life, answering the many
questions visitors ask and providing a permanent record of what for me is still
a unique experience?
Brendon Grimshaw
16 May 1995, St Brendan's Day
Moyenne Island, Seychelles.
First published 1996 by
Camerapix Publishers International,
PO Box 45048,
Nairobi,
Kenya

PART ONE:
Robinson Crusoe
and Man Friday

============================================================
PAGE_009  (source: IMG_1359.heic  confidence: 100%  words: 349)
============================================================

The Island
Moyenne is one of the forty granitic islands of the Seychelles archipelago, the
only oceanic granitic islands in the world.
Set in the middle of the Indian Ocean and some 600 million years old, the is-
lands are believed to be the tops of mountains of Gondwanaland which sank
when that land mass split to shape the world as it is today. South America drifted
away from Africa, and India moved north to push the highest mountains, the
Himalayas, even higher. The other eighty islands that make up Seychelles are
coraline and stand only a few feet above sea level.
Moyenne is small - just 22.5 acres, little over half a mile in length and half
that in width. But this smallness is deceptive as the island has two peaks, the
highest rising to 201 feet, with a broad saddle joining them across the middle. It
is conveniently close to the main island of Mahe (4.5 miles) but far enough away
to ensure the privacy I so much enjoy.
Moyenne is indeed beautiful, a mass of lush green vegetation, grey granite
rocks with white sandy beaches, a protecting coral reef and turquoise seas lap-
ping its shores.
But what you see today bears little resemblance to the overgrown bush and
waterless terrain which met me when I first stepped ashore thirty years ago. It
is undoubtedly one of Seychelles major conservation success stories.
Geographically, Moyenne is 4.36° south of the Equator at a latitude of 55.30°
east. It has an average seven hours of sunshine daily, and an annual rainfall of
approximately forty inches. Temperatures range between 24°C and 32°C and hu-
midity is in the eighties.
Moyenne, which means simply middle island, was named by the leader of a
French expedition, Captain Marion du Fresne, when he landed on it in 1768.
Officially, I am the only resident, the only Moyennois, as an inhabitant of the
island would be called, in the world.
The Seychelles tourist slogan is 'Unique by 1,000 miles'.
is that bit more special as, like me, you will discover.
. Obviously, Moyenne

PAGE_010  (source: IMG_1360.heic  confidence: 99%  words: 543)

Early days
When I was born it was 3 am during a torrential rainstorm in the industrial town
of Dewsbury in the West Riding of Yorkshire, England, on 27 July, 1925.
I mention the rainstorm because it was one of the earliest remembered anec-
dotes of my father, Raymond, as his trouser turnups had to be emptied of water
before he was allowed into the hospital to see his first and only child. My mother,
Kitty, had entered hospital only a few hours before after somewhat astonish-
ingly eating three pounds of whiteheart cherries. Perhaps that is why this
particular fruit could be included in what I miss most in Seychelles, but then there
are tropical gems like sabre and maison rouge mangoes to compensate.
We lived in a terrace house in a quiet street with my grandparents and other
relatives of my father as neighbours. Mother, having met my father while he was
in the army in the First World War, came from Leigh-on-Sea in Essex, noted for
its cockle boats in which I spent many happy hours as a boy. I recall quite clearly
sitting at a trestle table outside The Bell and tucking into plates of cockles and
thinly sliced brown bread to be washed down with tankards of ale — not too
bad for a ten-year-old.
I suppose it was my mother's influence which resulted in my not acquiring a
Yorkshire accent or a southern one, for that matter. In the north I was often re-
ferred to as that London boy, while on visits to my grandparents in Essex I was
always called their Yorkshire lad.
My father, who was wounded twice and gassed in France, was clever with
figures but later turned his hobbies of radio and television into a successful busi-
ness. Mother was a superb cook and brewed potent wines from elderberries,
raspberries, oranges and grapes (separately).
She also had a great love of the theatre and books which undoubtedly she
passed on to me. They say fathers and daughters, mothers and sons, and so it
was that my character, tastes, likes and dislikes, my leaning to the arts rather
than the sciences, were a clear reflection of my mother.
While my father was liberal in his politics with a strong chapel upbringing,
mother was decidedly conservative with High Church beliefs. But she was also
a bit of a rebel at heart and, when I ran away from school at the age of fifteen,
never to return, she fully understood. And, realizing that I had a flair for writ-
ing, she arranged with my headmaster's son, who worked on the local
newspaper, for me to have an interview with the editor.
The paper was the Batley News, with three editions covering Dewsbury,
Mirfield and Birstall. The editor was Rayner Roberts, whose first question was
whether I wished to be a reporter or a newspaperman. Happily, I didn't waffle
and asked him what he meant. Well, laddie, a reporter knows how to report, a
newspaperman knows all about newspapers.! Not being too dim, I replied: Well,
a newspaperman then', and maybe that is why to this day I find references to
my having been a journalist not to my liking and really not quite correct.
1

============================================================
PAGE_011  (source: IMG_1361.heic  confidence: 100%  words: 1089)
============================================================

I had been thirteen at the start of the Second World War and now, two years
later, most firms, including the Batley News, were short of staff. I found myself
in the front office wrapping the newspapers for posting, operating the rather de.
crepit telephone exchange and totalling endless columns of figures, not at all to
my liking. But. looking back, I suppose I was very lucky for, as much as I hated
school, I came to love my work which took up thirty years of my life and pro-
vided the opportunity for me to see the world and eventually find Moyenne.
progressed from that front office to the printing works where 1 became pro.
ficient not only on the 'stone' (in those days this was where the pages of metal
type were puttogether) but also with the linotype typese ling machine and other
printing machines. A hard day's work washool and then, couple of hours study-
ing printing techniques at the local night school and then, after a bus ride, private
shorthand tuition from 9.30pm to 10.30pm. I remember walking home those cold
nights and thoroughly enjoying my well-earned supper of fish and chips
wrapped, appropriately, in an old newspaper.
Reporting taught me not to judge people by what they say and do but by what
they don't say and don't do, and to get the answers I really wanted by indirect
questioning. I know when the technique is being employed on me today!
In fact, I did not start my writing career until eighteen months after joining
the paper, when I was given a couple of districts to cover. I recall that my oppo-
site number on the rival Reporter series of newspapers had all week to gather his
news while I had two afternoons only and my own bicycle as transport. And
woe to me if I was beaten to any story.
At this time I found an interest in local wrestling and with trilby hat and rain-
coat —a teenage Bogart, no less — I became known as Matman' to that sporting
community, led by 'The Farmer's Boy' who trained by lifting full milk churns
for his father.
And then, by chance, as so often happens in life, I found myself landed with
two columns of cinema criticism to produce every week. Here I discovered my
first love and quickly branched out into theatre and even music criticism. It was
a couple of years later that I was called to the Forces and I still have the cutting
from my old newspaper which, with a photograph of a remarkably young look-
ing guy, announced: 'Celluloid Joins the Army'.
Thus ended my training as a 'newspaperman', for on demobilization, had I
even touched a slug of type as a reporter' there would have been an immediate
strike, such was the change in labour relations. But, hard taskmaster that my old
editor had been, it resulted quite successfully in making a newspaperman out
of me, knowledge which was to prove valuable in the years ahead.
At twenty-three, I became the youngest chief reporter at the time in Britain,
switching after two years to The Star in neighbouring Sheffield for my evening
sub-editorial training. This was exciting and high tension work, and I became
'splash sub' handling the main story of the day, updating the news and chang-
ing the banner headline for each of the five editions. It was writing sentences to
12
her coronation were personal highlights, the latter story coming down the chute
from the news service a line at a time with finally just one word: 'Crowned.
It was over a beer at the local pub, The Dove and Rainbow, that a former splash
sub advised me to quit before I ended like him with a heart attack from the ten-
sion and thence relegation to a downtable sub for the rest of my life. I took his
advice and landed a job with the Anglo Iranian Oil company on their newspa-
per in Abadan. Strangely, my mother was adamant in asking me not to go and
for once I did what she wished. Two weeks later Mossadeq nationalized the com-
pany and I would not have had a job anyway. Intuition? Could be.
A few weeks later, she made no objection to my taking up an appointment as
senior sub-editor with the East African Standard in Nairobi, although it meant
eight years of Mau Mau problems and the inconvenience of carrying a gun in a
shoulder holster and sleeping with it under my pillow.
I was relieved not to have fired the gun in anger or hurt anyone when I handed
it in to the armoury in the Kenyan capital before I left finally for my first visit to
Seychelles.
My eight years in Kenya had been great and I ended as features editor of The
Standard as well as drama and book critic, still my favourite 'hobbies'.
First sight
Even today I could not tell you why I chose to start what was to be a round-the-
world trip with a visit to Seychelles.
Certainly, it was a convenient first port of call with the British India Steam-
ship Line operating a once-monthly passenger and cargo service each way from
Karachi and Bombay to Durban taking in the East African ports and providing
a twelve-hour stopover at Port Victoria, the tiny capital of Seychelles.
Friends had told me how beautiful the islands were. They used the same words
that I hear on Moyenne today: Paradise on Earth'. But that, and the fact that the
islands were never touched by cyclones, was all I knew about Seychelles.
Two white ships with black funnels, the Kampala and the Karanja, operated
on the route, taking two-and-a-half days to voyage from Mombasa on the Ken-
yan coast to Mahe. There was roughly one month between ships and the onward
journey to Bombay took five days. So the minimum stay in the islands was a
month, which caused very few grumbles indeed.
The first of many sea trips to the islands from East Africa in 1962 was memo-
rable for two travelling companions: Lord Oxford and Asquith, who was to
become governor of Seychelles (the brass band waiting on the docks was for him,
not me) and a young politician named Guy Sinon, who was a senior minister
both after independence and the coup which followed almost a year later.
Guy became a good friend and remained so until his death a couple of years

First sight
Even today I could not tell you why I chose to start what was to be a round-the-
world trip with a visit to Seychelles.

ship Line operating a once-monthly passenger and cargo service each way from

Of course, the Seychelles which I was approaching was vastly different from
that of today. Not only was there no airport but not even a port, shipping being
rough and ready plying boats.
obliged to anchor in the bay and offload cargo into lighters and passengers into
My first sight of the islands was soon after dawn from the upper deck of the
Kampala, passing the large and mountainous island of Silhouette before swing.
ing round Mahe's north-east point to discover a necklet of lush green islands
to the whole scene. It was at once beautiful and breathtaking.
circling the bay, with the mountains of Mahe forming a magnificent backdron
The capital was tucked away behind a seafront esplanade which today is in
the city centre, usurped by half a mile or so of reclaimed land on which fine new
buildings, a sports stadium and a modern port and industrial zone have mush-
But more of the changes later. It was the Seychelles of 1962 which caught my
imagination and captured my heart.
The rumble of the anchor chain coincided with the gong for breakfast which
Ishared with the other twenty fellow passengers about to disembark. There was
little else to do having dutifully placed our luggage in the passageway outside
our cabins and knowing that gangplanks had to be lowered, pontoons and light.
ers brought alongside.
Our first meeting with the islands officials was impressive. A man with close-
cropped, iron-grey hair, sharply starched khaki shirt and shorts and a row of
medal ribbons, stamped our passports with grave courtesy and I learned later
that he was not only chief immigration officer but also the commissioner of po-
lice. Strangely, perhaps, we were not to meet again for several years, until I
required a permit to live in Seychelles. But certainly he was a credit to the is-
lands.
However, the same could not be said of the two hotel owners who, once I was
through the official formalities, physically grabbed me by each arm and engaged
in a tug of war with me in the middle, both claiming: 'He's mine!' Now, I under-
stand that with a limited number of tourists it was important for any hotelier to
try and capture anyone who had not pre-booked, such as myself.
Managing to shake free, I decided neither would receive my custom and that
was how, eventually, I came to stay at the Pirates Arms - an ancient looking,
two-storey wooden building where every floorboard creaked and the proprie-
tor, a tall thin Englishwoman, Mrs Whiteright (better known as Mrs Quiteright)
frowned upon any out of wedlock sex in her establishment. Such was my intro-
duction to what became known as the 'islands of love'.
One of the nicest aspects of staying in Seychelles in what I can term the 'old
days'— before the advent of the international airport in 1971 and the subsequent
development of the tourist industry — was that visitors mixed far more freely
14
with the Seychellois. They used their restaurants, bars and small hotels and, as
in my own case, made real friendships which have lasted over three decades.
A chance meeting over a drink or a simple introduction by a Seychellois to
another friend would result, more often than not, in an invitation home. A typi-
cal example of this was an invitation to 'come fishing with us tomorrow... if
you'd like ... we'll pick you up at seven in the morning'. The trip was fantastic,
sailing out to a fishing bank and hauling in 'four-footers' until the boat could
take no more and returning to a picnic house to have supper with the family
before being dropped back at my hotel. They wanted nothing in return, happy
with the satisfaction of giving a stranger a great day to remember. A 'thank you'
dinner at my small hotel was not expected but obviously appreciated and recip-
rocated both ways over the years. Three families in particular come to mind: Yvon
and Zita Savy, Sadec and Ruth Rasool and Raoul and Doris Nageon. But there
were many, many more.
In 1962 Seychelles was still very colonial. First colonized by the French in 1770,
what surprisingly had been totally uninhabited islands were ceded to Britain at
the Treaty of Paris. While the French had been settlers, the British came and went
as administrators and this accounts for the fact that a century-and-a-half later
the Seychellois are more French in their 'ambience' than British, and the national
language remains a French-based Creole.
Over the years other races arrived to provide a unique population (30,000 in
1962, 72,000 today) and a welcome example of racial harmony to the outside
world.
With the abolition of slavery, British warships had intercepted slaving dhows
plying their sad trade from Zanzibar to the Gulf and perforce had to offload their
human cargo in the islands of the Indian Ocean. Thus Seychelles became home
to some 3,500 young men and women and formed the main element of its black
population while traders from the Indian subcontinent and Chinese from Can-
ton and Hong Kong made up the third 'partner'.
Perhaps the first visible difference for someone like myself coming from
Kenya, a country where black and white had not mixed, was the amazing vari-
ety of colours even in the same families. Certainly a considerable amount of
integration had taken place. Even today if a handful of old families maintain their
French or European bloodlines, and on religious grounds, many Asians follow
a similar pattern, the mixing continues from generation to generation. Doubt-
less this is assisted by seventy-seven per cent of children today being born out
the main reason.
of wedlock, although living en ménage, rather than being 'churched', is obviously
So, although a colonial governor with a plumed helmet ruled with white ad-
ministrators, the people mixed in the streets, in the restaurants and in the bars.
Naturally, there were social barriers as there are in most countries. But to a tourist
they were of little importance. It was the general friendliness and hospitality of
the islanders that made an immediate and lasting impression.

my visits to the islands and their tone was very much set by the governor of the
I recall turning up at Government House on one occasion to find, with some
the then governor, Sir Hugh Norman-Walker, to appear, monocle and all, at the
amusement, all the guests standing by their vehicles in the car park waiting for
top of the entrance steps.
Eventually, we walked in single file to shake hands and, knowing that he had
no idea who I was, I told him my name, adding that I hoped to come and live in
Seychelles. To which he, rather surprisingly, replied: 'And die here, I trust?'
My spontaneous response was: Well, not too soon, I hope! This appeared to
upset the old boy who spluttered, let his monocle fall and, turning to the guest
behind me, said in a loud voice, 'Next'.
Unfortunately, I made matters worse by asking the young white-coated waiter
for a brandy, not noticing the already poured drinks which were limited to
whisky, gin, beer and orange juice. When the waiter returned with a silver sal-
ver, brandy goblet and what was obviously the governor's personal decanter of
cognac.... well, as a fellow guest commented: 'My, you'll never be invited again.!
I was one of the first to leave politely when Sir Hugh stomped heavily across
the wooden floor to stand by the door and let his guests know it was time to go.
The easiest governor was Sir Bruce Greatbatch who met me by the swimming
pool, a towel wrapped around his waist and a pink gin in hand. There was a big
black dog which he used to take for an evening walk down what was known as
Long Pier.
I mention the Government House parties because, as a visitor to the islands,
I was in a privileged position those days. For a local resident such an invitation
was regarded as very important. Equally important was not to be seen in some
of the livelier spots in town, like Sharky's Bar.
As a tourist, one was expected to sample the local life. And, to be fair, I much
16
preferred the company of Sharky and his motley bar companions to the small
talk of the cocktail parties. But then I was young and, in retrospect, understand
that both were essential parts of the scene which endeared me to Seychelles from
the start.
Sharky was what some writers would describe as a 'rough diamond'. He had
left his wife back in Southend, Essex (pier and all), and ran a friendly bar in the
centre of Victoria, a Victoria, I might add, that was considerably brighter at night
with its live bands, bars and restaurants than the dead city of closed shops and
offices which it is today, with only two discos and a revived Pirates Arms to cater
for a vastly bigger tourist and local industry.
Sharky had the happy knack of making friends with all who dropped by his
bar for the odd drink and who subsequently returned most nights, if not for a
pre-dinner drink, then maybe for egg and chips lashed up by his helpmate, Daisy,
at one o'clock in the morning. It was never too early or too late for Sharky and,
bearing in mind the strict licensing hours operating in the UK at the time, sur-
prisingly there were no licensing hours at all in Seychelles.
I well remember chatting with Sharky over a Carlsberg beer — there was no
local brewery then — when he bemoaned the fact that he was always bank-
rupted' by free drinks on his birthday, which was the following week.
As he talked he suddenly got a bright idea. He would make money out of his
birthday. How? By chartering the Lady Esme, the small, iron-built, inter-island
ferry boat, using his own staff and providing drinks and snacks for a cruise in
the bay for guests whom he would charge £2.
It was a wonderful, balmy starlit night with a huge moon reflecting on the
calm waters that made the encircling islands (including Moyenne, although I
didn't know it at the time) glow in the soft light. There was even a small band
playing quietly for a change. The atmosphere was magic, the drinks flowed, the
bar was drunk dry and Sharky ended up, as usual after his birthday, bankrupt'.
The last time I met him he took me to a shed on the Long Pier where he was
building a catamaran in which he planned to sail round the world. Sadly, his
dream did not come true. It appears that his wife materialized from Southend
and Sharky ended his days in Hastings or thereabouts. He was one of many 'char-
affection.
acters I met during that first visit to the Seychelles whom I remember with real
My next trip on board the Lady Esme was to Praslin, the second largest island
in the group, situated twenty-two miles and three hours' sail from Mahe. In those
days there was no electricity or reasonable roads, let alone an airport or the score
of hotels that welcome today's visitors.
It was a twice-weekly sailing so it meant that I had to stop over at least two
nights. Luckily, I had been given permission to stay in the third of three govern-
ment guest houses situated at Grand Anse. The first, named Mickey Mouse (from
its murals of the Disney characters) was for the governor and his family, and
the second for the matron on her official visits.

I say lucky because there was only one alternative in those davs: the house of
a lady who, on opening the door, either let you in if she liked what she saw or
slammed the door shut in your face if she didn't, which meant a night on the
beach unless someone took pity on you.
I was lucky also because the guest houses were looked after by a tall widow
in black who cooked magnificently. On that first Praslin night I had turtle steak.
breadfruit chips and coco de mer jlly made from the unique double coconut origi.-
nating in the nearby Vallée de Mai and resembling the female pelvis. No wonder,
during his stay in Seychelles, General Gordon (later of Khartoum fame) decided
that the valley was the site of the original Garden of Eden with its exotic looking
nuts, the jelly of which is claimed to be an aphrodisiac.
I must admit it never appeared to do anything for me on the few occasions I
tried it, usually with a sauce made from Grand Marnier or Contreau. Today the
nuts are prized as souvenirs and their sale strictly controlled by the government
at roughly £50 each. But the visitor is not missing a culinary delight as the jelly,
without the liqueur, is literally tasteless.
Most visitors to Moyenne ask what made me decide to settle in Seychelles,
how did I come to buy an island and many more questions.
Well, frankly, I am not too sure. Certainly, I was not, as suggested by some
people, farsighted enough to buy the island as a future investment or yet again
to 'opt out'", to escape from the 'rat race'. All I do know is that after one week in
the islands my instincts told me to find a place to which I could return. I knew
that I would have to put down my anchor some day and why not in Seychelles
so that, wherever I travelled, I would always return to a beautiful and warmer
home.
I suppose the idea of an island came to mind because I was surrounded by
them and at no time can I recall wishing to find a 'parcel' of land, as it is still
quaintly called today, on 'mainland' Mahe. In fact, I spent a fruitless second and
third week of my holiday trying to make what had become a dream a reality.
Golden rocks
The visit to Praslin was to include a quick look at two other islands: Curieuse
and Round. Curieuse, which is now a marine national park and home to a colony
of giant tortoises brought from Aldabra, was 'available'. The government was
keen to have someone take a long lease on the island and arrangements had been
made for a forest ranger to take me from where I was staying by Land-Rover to
the far side of Praslin and then, by boat, to neighbouring Curieuse.
The ranger was Willi André, whom I liked at first sight and thirty years later
I am happy to meet as a director in the Department of Environment. We drove
to a small property owned by another 'character' called Tyndal-Biscoe. He let
us use his boat to row to the other island. I don't remember what state Willis
hands were in by the time we reached the far shore but mine were red and blis-
18
tered. I had found out how difficult it is to row when the south-east monsoon is
blowing. We were welcomed by another forestry officer, Raoul Lafontaine, and
his young son, Pierre, who chopped open a couple of coco tans (green coconuts
large enough to contain a lot of milk but not old enough for the milk to have
solidified into the white coconut meat). Having quenched our thirsts we took a
look at Curieuse and my instincts told me a definite No'. The row back was fast
and easy with the wind and currents behind us.
As we were climbing into the Land-Rover for our return journey, Tyndal-
Biscoe called out to ask why we didn't buy his property. It was for sale at £5,000.
I don't know whether it was Willi or myself who said quietly that he must be
mad, thinking his rocks were made of gold. This time my instincts let me down.
The property had a deep water anchorage and, within a few years, changed hands
for £75,000.
Next came Round Island. There are two islands of that name in Seychelles,
one neighbouring Moyenne and the other just off Baie St. Anne, Praslin. Both
were homes for lepers, the former for women and the latter for men. Later the
remaining few were transferred to Curieuse. There is no leprosy in the islands
Warned that the man living on the Round Island I was to visit shot at people
who tried to land there, I had been given a letter of introduction and had Willi
help organise a pirogue (canoe) to take me across the narrow channel.
My scepticism about the possibility of getting shot was quickly dispelled when
the local fishermen refused to make the trip. Eventually, after agreeing to dou-
ble the price and guarantee the cost of the boat if it was seized by the man on the
island, I was paddled across and to my dismay found that our shotgun friend
was not at the house on the main landing but at another dwelling in the interior.
Calling out Hello, hello. Friend, friend', and waving the letter of introduction
like a white flag, I walked along a track through thick vegetation to a wide clear-
ing where I met a small, thick-set, sun-browned man who eyed me suspiciously
and demanded what I wanted. He was decidedly unfriendly but not hostile and
Asking if he was Ken Theobald and receiving a curt nod, I handed him the
letter which, he read slowly and then, without a word, went into the house and
came back, happily, with his pipe and tin of tobacco. Telling me to sit down on
a nearby log, he filled his pipe, got it going and then said: 'I don't want to sell
I guess my instincts were on the ball this time. Explaining that I thought the
island was his and not on a lease (it had only fifteen years to go), I said that I
would not wish to share with anyone. As I got up to go, he suddenly asked how
I had come to his island. Not with one of those thieving fishermen, I hope..!
I said 'cheerio' as confidently as I could and made for the beach. Ken Theobald
didn't follow and he didn't fire at the boatman who had waited for me in mid-
stream.

Some Veare later I received a letter from Ken Theobald in Australia offering
to S bad parmised to let him have the interest during hi
lifetime. However, he changed his mind after a reconciliation with his daugh
ter. We were to meet once again in Victoria Hospital where he was having
medical treatment before returning to Australia. Presumably the breadfruit had
brought him back in true Seychelles tradition.
Will, with his two-year-old son, Placide (now a grown man with a fast car
and even faster speedboat), took me back to the guest house. I also visited sev-
eral grand madames sitting on their long cool verandahs, overlooking acres of land.
They offered me coffee, orange juice or a sherry if I was lucky but were not at all
Serious in their plans to sell. I got the impression that they merely enjoyed the
social contact. Incidentally, I never met a grand monsieur and came to the quite
unjustified conclusion that the ladies with the acres had probably poisoned their
husbands (there were plenty of strychnine trees and other poisonous plants
around).
Ispent my spare time on Praslin clambering through Gordon's Garden of Eden
and, like all present-day tourists, marvelled at the strange coco de mer palms
which, as with pawpaw and nutmeg, are male and female. To match the sug-
gestive shape of the female nut, the male tree produces giant, phallic-like catkins
studded with small yellow flowers. Only black parrots were to be found in that
valley, eating cucumber-shaped fruits called bilimbe, the pleasant sound of a
waterfall and always the varied and lush vegetation.
Although I did not know it at the time, I was not to return to Praslin or visit
its smaller neighbour, La Digue, for at least another thirty years, an excursion
taken by most tourists to Seychelles. It only goes to show how insular an islander
can become.
Returning to Mahe in the Lady Esme I became friendly with a man who, as we
approached Victoria, pointed to a building on the hillside to the right of the city
and told me: You will always be welcome at my hotel. Afterwards I learned
that he was the chief magistrate and his 'hotel' was the King George Prison. Later
it was to be the scene of a brief army mutiny and provide shelter for a number of
families. Of course, I have my own prison... neighbouring Long Island whose
guards and prisoners give me a friendly wave and a bark for my dogs Bernadette
and Flambo, as they pass through the channel between Moyenne and Round in
their dark-green painted boats. I couldn't have friendlier neighbours.
Back on Mahe, I decided that much as I loved Seychelles, the islands were not
for me. I would make up for lost time and relax and enjoy the beaches and reefs
by day and the bars and restaurants at night, during what would be my final
week of glorious bright sunshine, warm seas and not a drop of rain in
sight.
I tried snorkelling around Souris (Mouse) Island near Anse Royal and had an
uncomfortable ten minutes accompanied by a fair-sized barracuda which fol-
lowed a few feet away from me wherever I swam. It is the only time that I have
20
chickened out and left the water in such circumstances. Since then I have often
found myself swimming with sharks, and not small ones at that, but in Seychelles
there is no known record of a shark attack on man. Why these superb creatures
should be so dangerous off, say, Durban or Sydney, and not in Seychelles no
one really knows. But I suppose the waters are warmer and the sharks not so
voracious. There is an abundance of fish around and man has fished shark and
been his known enemy since he arrived in the islands. I would assume that it is
a combination of all these factors that makes our swimming much safer than else-
where.
On a lighter note, I met one of the most beautiful young girls in the islands
who insisted on calling me 'Captain Bren' and much to my embarrassment (hypo-
crite that I must have been) paraded in front of the hotel until I had to chase or
take her away. She had an angelic-sounding name, and the rest is best unsaid.
I water ski'd and hired a car for £1 a day from a man called Gilbert who had
among his many jobs the upkeep of the four-faced clock in the city centre, a rep-
lica of the one on Vauxhall Bridge Road in London, which was unveiled in 1903
to mark Seychelles becoming a crown colony and is today a useful landmark
particularly for visitors. If a Seychellois wants to describe someone as an idiot
he will say: 'Oh, he has not seen the 'orlage!'
While on the subject of clocks, there is a bell-tower close to the Roman Catho-
lic Cathedral of the Immaculate Conception which chimes twice, once on the hour
and then about four minutes later. Local people will tell you that the double chim-
ing is for those hard of hearing or to remind some of their more leisurely friends
that time is passing. Of course, many clocks of that era and before were made to
chime twice and it gave Alec Waugh a neat title for his book, Where the Clock
Chimes Twice, published in the early 1950s. His descriptions of Seychelles coin-
cide very much with my own earliest recollections. And he refers to many
Seychellois whom I came to know intimately, his comments shrewd but kindly.
Touch of hidden magic
After a couple of weeks I had changed my hotel for a beachside establishment,
the Hotel de Seychelles, which provided morning tea at 6 (whether you wished
for it or not), a full English breakfast, mid-morning coffee and biscuits, a three-
course lunch, afternoon tea and fruitcake and a four-course dinner, all for the
astonishing sum of £4 a day.
The only snag was sandflies on the beach, infinitely nastier than mosquitoes
(still no malaria, yellow fever, cholera or rabies in the islands) and which required
a visit to the pharmacy in Victoria for citronelle, or lemon grass oil, a smelly but
totally effective repellent. Today the beaches are sprayed and tourists can even
sunbathe topless without any problem of bites or stings.
With four days to go before I was due to sail for Bombay, the hotel owner, a
small foxy man called Gerry Legrande, suggested I join a couple of Canadian

The day turned out better than I could have imagined.
The Alice was a comfortable, wooden-hulled vessel which we boarded at the
Yacht Club for the half-hour trip from the inner harbour and up the passage be-
tween Mahe and Cerf. It was necessary to anchor outside the reef which protects
Cerf and Cachée and go ashore by dinghy.
We landed on the southern tip of Cerf where a man was busy digging a large
hole just above the beach. He said he was digging he had asured alo offered to
sell us some superb triton and spider shells which he had found along the reef
and cleaned and polished. Of course, this was quite legal then as the St Anne
Marine National Park, taking in all the inner islands, had yet to be established
(1973).
Carrying picnic boxes shoulder-high, we waded across the channel to ile
Cachée and made our base' in the ruins of an old house still there today.
Cachée is just what one would imagine a tropical island to be, in miniature. It
has two small hills with a grassy saddle sloping down to a pretty hitle beach
facing Anonyme and South-East islands (and today the International Airport)
There are five graves, two of which had been excavated by treasure seekers,
and a clump of rocks with a fun, squeeze-through passage between two of them.
There was a large hole in the middle of the island, roughly ten feet deep, with a
self-seeded palm growing from the bottom, its leaves just touching ground level.
And there were a number of other smaller holes, including one which had be-
come grassed over, a 'trap' into which I stepped and dropped eight feet, happily
without harm. It was a funny situation as I had been walking with the Canadian
and his wife on either side of me and they carried on normally as I 'disappeared'.
My calls for 'help' resulted in the appearance of two surprised faces peering down
and, assured that I was uninjured, roars of laughter until I was unceremoniously
hauled back to civilization.
I suppose the beauty of the island, the atmosphere created by the treasure seek-
ers (past and present), the wonderful weather and the magnificent swimming
among hundreds of brightly-coloured coral fish on the reef worked their magic
and I decided that Cachée was what I'd been looking for all the time.
Over the picnic lunch I learned that the island was owned by Sam Calais, who
lived on another property on the far side of Cerf. The hotel owner said that he
had tried to lease the island without success but that I might prove luckier. It
was arranged for me to visit the Calais family the next day using the Alice for
transport.
Perhaps you can imagine my feelings as I returned to Mahe with the sun set-
ting behind the mountains and the sky all turquoise and gold. Cachée could prove
to be my 'island in the sun.
22
I met Sam Calais, his wife and sons on the verandah of their house with
brightly-coloured birds flying in and out all the time and the view of the garden
dominated by takamaka trees and a large kapok, the first I had seen, with its burst-
ing pods of white fluff.
It did not take long. Obviously, we liked each other and it was agreed for me
to take a sixty-year lease on Cachée. The documents were to be drawn up and
signed in the office of our mutual lawyer friend, Raoul Nageon, the next morn-
ing and my last day in Seychelles. The rent was fixed at what today would be a
ridiculously low figure of £50 annually.
There was a clause forbidding the cutting of trees but this was no problem
and, in fact, over the next few years I revitalized the coconut palm population
and also introduced guavas, mangoes, custard apples and flowering shrubs. Ile
Cachée was, and still is, rather magical for me.
Fate takes a hand
While I followed my instincts in my island search I must accept that fate, karma,
destiny or whatever name you care to give it, finally decided everything.
The lawyer had his office on the first floor of a very old wooden building in
central Victoria. Dry and dusty, with black deed boxes bearing their owners'
names packed from floor to ceiling, the office was approached by a long flight
of wooden stairs and was spartan, to say the least. I found Raoul behind his small
desk piled high with files tied with pink ribbons.
By the time I arrived, the Calais family had already signed the lease and de-
parted. All that remained was my own signature. Signed, sealed and delivered,
so to speak, I was talking with the lawyer, who had become a friend during the
first days of my holiday, when his telephone rang. To be polite and not appear
to be listening to the conversation (which as it was in Creole I could not under-
stand anyway) I went to look out of the window on to the street below. Of course,
there was no glass in those days, just wooden shutters which were closed and
barred at night.
A young man was coming along the street and, to my surprise, called up: 'Hey,
do you want to buy an island?'
I replied: I've just done so
'No, not Cachée, but a real island, Moyenne, he shouted back, adding, 'T'1l
come up!
It appeared that the young man, whose name was Norman Gardner, had over-
heard the owner of Moyenne (Philippe George) say the night before in a local
hardware merchants store that he would have to sell his island.
Raoul confirmed that Moyenne was a beautiful island and, in fact, would be
more habitable than Cachée.
So, with Norman taking me across the bay in his fast speedboat and hair-
raisingly slipping between the rocks off Round Island, I found myself stepping

ashore on Moyenne for the first of many times to come. There was no hesita-
tion, no doubts in my mind, as I clambered up the rocky track to a small plateau
This was it. This was the island of my dreams. As I gazed around, dwarfed by
Sian wango trees, barred from explorine much chine or the ungle-like bush,
i knew that Moyenne was what I had been searching for all the time.
Pushing through a tangle of shrubs and weeds, I found an old weather-beaten
wooden picnic house lonely in its isolation, but its long open verandah must have
seen some happy parties in years gone by. The island was silent, there was no
guardian, not even brightly-coloured birds fitting through the trees to welcome
me. But, there was no sadness here, just happiness and beauty all around. My
instincts were sounding loud and clear: Moyenne is for you. It is everything you
wanted. Make it your home! I was elated, highly excited and I could not get back
to Mahe quickly enough to meet the owner.
I had spent a mere ten minutes on the island but it was enough for me to re-
alize that I was willing to spend a lifetime there. Of course, I wanted to buy
Moyenne for myself. I was no entrepreneur, as has been suggested by a number
of visitors after I opened the island and shared its beauty and tranquillity.
On that sunny Saturday morning, as I stood on the white sandy beach to leave
Moyenne for the first time, not in my wildest dreams could have imagined al-
lowing a situation where thousands of visitors would make it one of the most
popular tourist destinations — if still unspoilt and very special. It just, luckily,
happened kind of spontaneously. Indeed most things concerning Moyenne have
evolved in that way over the years.
As we sped across the bay, taking quick backward glances towards my 'is-
land in the sun', I did not realize that the purchase would be much more difficult
than I expected. Norman generously refused any payment for the trip or even
the fuel and, although I did not know it then, he was not to return to Moyenne
for nineteen years. Eventually, he came with his brother Wills from Cerf Island
and I was able to raise a toast to the man who started it all.
Back on Mahe, I traced Moyenne's owner, Philippe George, to the post office
and, it being Saturday lunchtime, ready to go home for the weekend. Far more
distressing, however, was the fact that he did not want to sell the island. Laugh-
ing, he told me that what young Norman had overheard was a joke. He was
building a house for his wife, Vera, and himself and had merely commented that
if prices increased any more he would have to sell Moyenne.
You can guess how shattered I was at the news. Already I had begun to think
of what I would do when I came to live on Moyenne. I had even forgotten about
Cachée in the excitement. Perhaps with little else to say, I invited Philippe and
his wife to dinner at my hotel. It was my last night in Seychelles and I would
enjoy some company. There would be no embarrassment as, unless Philippe
brought up the subject, I would not mention Moyenne. Philippe accepted the
invitation and I asked Raoul and his girlfriend (now his wife) Doris to join us.
They came on from a cocktail party, arriving about 9 pm. We had drinks at
24
the thatched bar and dinner in the open-air restaurant. What we had to eat I don't
recall but there was a pleasant wine and we sat under the palm trees near the
beach for coffee and liqueurs. It had been a fine evening and created bonds of
friendship which are still strong after thirty years.
Around 11 pm, Philippe turned to me and said that I was not too bad a guy.
I said, 'Thanks. And he continued: 'You know I inherited Moyenne from my
godfather and I would not want it to go to anyone who would not care for it.
And I believe you would!
As I nodded my head to say, 'of course I would, Philippe continued: It's
Vera's birthday today! And there we all raised our glasses and said: 'Cheers to
Vera! Philippe then added: 'It's really strange, it's true that I had no thought to
sell Moyenne, that my comment last night was a joke... but here we are on the
last day of your holiday, and Vera's birthday as well, and Ibelieve that you would
care for Moyenne. Let's try and come to terms today!
We sent Vera and Doris for a walk on the beach and it was exactly four min-
utes to midnight on the last day of my holiday that Philippe and I shook hands
and Moyenne had a new owner. For the record it was 24 February 1962.
I was to discover later that I was the eleventh owner of the island since it was
first settled in 1850 and only the third to actually live on it.
So, to those who ask, 'How did you come to own an island?' I must answer
'fate'. There was no estate agency, no advertisement. It just happened that way.
Had it not been for one telephone call in Raoul's office, my politeness in going
to the window, a young man misunderstanding a conversation, Vera's birthday,
and Philippe and I liking each other on that last day of my holiday, well, I would
not be living on Moyenne today or, for that matter, writing this book.
The morning after our handshake, I discovered that the ship was a day late
and would be arriving on the Monday so arrangements were made for Philippe,
Vera and myself to sign the papers and have the sale registered with the gov-
ernment the next morning. The agreed figure was £8,000 but with the usual bits
and pieces' the island cost me roughly £10,000.
All went according to plan and I boarded the Karanja in the afternoon and sat
on deck looking at Moyenne a couple of miles away. Over the years friends have
said that I must have had an 'all mine' sort of feeling. But not at all. There had
been no time for reaction to set in and, as I drank a large gin and tonic in a beer
glass with lots of ice, a couple of slices of lemon and a few dashes of bitters, I
recall asking myself: 'What have you done?'
At five I sailed away from Seychelles never to regret acquiring the island. If
there was an 'all mine' feeling at any time, certainly it faded with the years as I
came to realize that compared to 600-million-year-old Moyenne I am indeed just
a grain of sand on one of its beaches. And I know, as a fact, that no one can truly
own an island, just be fortunate to be its guardian for a brief spell. Since coming
to live on Moyenne, almost daily I have found myself opening my eyes and say-
ing 'thank you'. To whom? God, of course. Thank you for my life, for the island

and all the beauty that surrounds me wherever 1 look by day or by night. One
does not need to be particularly religious to understand that.
I had planned to return to Seychelles within a year. I made it in three. As they
say, Man proposes, God disposes.
The five-day voyage to Bombay (2,000 miles from Mahe) provided a welcome
opportunity to relax and try and put in perspective the likely repercussions of
my final hectic weekend in Seychelles in which Thad successfully concluded my
island search and acquired not one but two — Moyenne, a totally freehold prop-
erty, and Cachée, with a lease which would see out my lifetime.
The voyage also gave me a chance to sample five memorable steamed pud-
dings (sultana, ginger, lemon, chocolate and treacle), a speciality of the Karanja's
galley. It may seem a trifle odd to partake of puddings in such a hot climate but
even today I miss them. They are a rarity in Seychelles and I have been too lazy
to cook one for myself on Moyenne.
Lying with a book and a cool drink to hand, I found infinite pleasure in merely
watching the waves slip by as the ship ploughed on, the flying fish flushed by
the bows skimming over the water for amazing distances and, eventually, see-
ing the setting sun, a great golden ball sinking so quickly at the horizon's edge.
But I was never lucky enough to catch a glimpse of that elusive 'green flash' at
the moment the sun set. Crossing the South Atlantic twenty years later, I recall
a similar failure as, night after night, I watched with fellow passengers on board
the Polaris. I have to ask: Is there really such a thing as the green flash? Person-
ally, I have grave doubts.
During that voyage 1 do not remember coming to any definite decision on my
future, whether to carry on with the world trip or what. As often happens, events
decided things for me. While approaching Bombay on those flat, still waters, a
telegram arrived. My parents were unwell and it was clear they needed me 'at
home' meaning Seaford, Sussex, where they had retired for the latter part of what
turned out to be a sixty-one-year marriage.
On arrival in Bombay I booked into the Taj Hotel on the waterfront, a hotel
reputedly built the wrong way round so that it faced the hot town rather than
catching any breezes from the sea. The architect apparently committed suicide
after the project was completed. Nevertheless, it was a fine hotel and I was a bit
unhappy to be flying out the next day via Aden. I took an Air India flight which
was remarkable for serving a splendid lobster cardinale and providing truly
memorable service, one of the best flights I have made.
It was from a waiter at the Taj that I learned never to drink anything if I found
a curry too hot. A slice of cool mango, bread or rice would absorb the 'heat' in
one's mouth, whereas water, beer or wine rapidly spread it.
There was little I could do on that one evening in Bombay other than go for a
stroll to the nearby Gateway to India where I saw my first cobra swaying in its
wicker basket, its master playing a mournful tune on a reed pipe. I did not know
it then but my next visit to Bombay would be for two days and never again.
26
Out in the cold
March is not my first choice of a month to visit England. In 1962, March was cold,
wet and windy, especially in that small seaside town of Seaford where the beach
was lashed by great waves which on occasion completely smashed the prom-
enade, piling great chunks of concrete on top of one another. Indeed, my mother
enjoyed telling the story of how the elderly waiter at the then Esplanade Hotel
had clung to a lamppost for dear life until rescued by the police. Had he let go,
undoubtedly he would have been swept out to sea. Others told me of pebbles
hurled from the beach into the town centre.
It was all very different from Seychelles.
Happily, both my parents recovered, although I could hardly claim it was the
result of my nursing. But they did not like the idea of my going overseas again
too quickly and, what was to prove a major problem for me, mother did not wish
to talk about Seychelles and particularly Moyenne. Over the following twenty
years or so she was to refuse every offer of a holiday in the islands, commenting
that 'England is good enough for me. Not nasty, hot, smelly foreign places!
Bearing in mind that she was an intelligent woman, I can only assume that
travel was her blind spot, although, surprisingly, after her death, I found an up-
to-date passport among her papers. At this time my father did not possess one.
So it was with some reluctance that I went up to London and, visiting an old
newspaper friend of the Sheffield days, landed a job with Reuters News Agency
and started shift work for the first and only time in my life.
There was one consolation: I took a bedsitter just off Sloane Square to enable
me to catch the District Line to Temple rather than commute from Seaford to
Victoria and then travel on to Fleet Street. Commuting would have meant four
hours travel every day with a mile walk at each end.
As it was, I became a creature of no world. The shifts I worked prevented me
from joining any clubs or from making any friendships. My colleagues commuted
to their homes in the suburbs. What to do? I discovered London and became the
total tourist. One day I visited the Tower of London, the next Westminster Ab-
bey, then St Paul's, the National Gallery, and so on. I enjoyed returning to my
bedsitter, always stopping for a hotdog (with English mustard) and a mug of
coffee at a night stall in Sloane Square. In a way, I suppose, shades of those cold
night walks with a packet of fish and chips after shorthand lessons in my early
days in Yorkshire.
Whenever I had a couple of days off, or five after a long night stint, I went
down to Seaford and helped with the home and garden. Mother had named the
house, with its tall sloping Sussex tiled roof, 'Clover Patch' because that was what
it was when my parents bought the land. With green fingers she had created a
fine garden and there were masses of roses, honeysuckle and wisteria as well as
long flower beds and cherry and almond trees.
At the back were six pine trees grown from a cone which I had picked up on

Table Mountain in South Africa on a round-Africa voyage in the Kenya Castle.
Three years later, wanting to establish a windbreak, she had remembered the
cone and the six pines were the result. Throughout my life I have been a seed
collector and some of the unusual plants and trees to be found in that Sussex
garden and on Moyenne are living proof that even in a valtan afferent climate
seeds will flourish. Mark you, I always see that they are clean and bug-free.

nese restaurant on Piccadilly.
Over the food, he asked how I was getting along and when I replied, Not so
good', suggested I meet the then editor of the two national newspapers in Tan-
ganyika — part of the group for which I had worked in Kenya — when he came
to London in a couple of weeks time. The editor was Bill Ottewill, who later be-
came Barclays Bank's first public relations manager.
It was a repeat performance, Regent Palace and the Chinese restaurant. This
time over the food Bill told me that if I would accept any job — I had held a sen-
ior post in Nairobi - then the next one available would be mine. I agreed and,
true to his word, a few weeks later I received a telegram offering me a sub-edi-
torial post with the Tanganyika Standard and its sister paper, the Sunday News,
which would fall vacant the following December.
Of course, there were mixed feelings. I was elated at the prospect of return-
ing to a warmer climate to work among old friends. I knew my parents would
not be too happy but they had each other and were comfortable in their retire-
ment. I had my own life to live, at least that was what I told myself, knowing it
to be a thoroughly selfish attitude, if the right one in the long run.
And then there was Reuters for whom I was just becoming useful. However,
when I said I would be based in Dar es Salaam, the news editor was delighted.
'We have a staff man with a flat there. But he's always away on stories in other
parts of Africa. You could file for us and take care of the flat when unoccupied!
Thus when arrived in Dar es Salaam, I had two jobs lined up, neither of which
would affect the other, and was guaranteed a good income from the start.
I planned my departure so that I could spend a month with my parents in
Seaford and then sail from London in the Braemar Castle to East Africa via the
Mediterranean and the Suez Canal. The voyage took in new ports of call, such
as Gibraltar and Genoa, and brought back happy memories of army days spent
in Egypt, particularly Port Said and Port Fouad.
Shipboard friendships are always fun, albeit, brief encounters. And the album
photographs show grinning faces among the apes on the Rock, somewhat fro-
zen looks behind glasses of Ruffino and smiles in Arab garb bought for the
28
fancy-dress ball just before the ship crossed the Equator. The most pleasant part
of the voyage was arriving again at Mombasa, where I had worked for a year
helping to run the now defunct Mombasa Times. On deck, in brilliant sunshine,
memories tripped over themselves as we passed Fort Jesus, and steamed through
the narrow Likoni Channel which opens out into Kilindi, the fine deepwater
harbour that makes Mombasa such an important port.
Quite a number of passengers disembarked at Mombasa, many working or
farming in Kenya or further up the line in Uganda. Friends gave a honking wel-
come from their cars parked on the road overlooking the channel. It was
altogether a happy homecoming for many people, including myself. In a couple
of days we sailed for Dar es Salaam, via the northern Tanganyikan port of Tanga
and Zanzibar Island.
By strange coincidence the couple with whom I left my black and white cat,
Toffee, in Nairobi, joined the ship at Mombasa on their way back to the UK and
retirement. Always good friends, the Niemyers assured me that Toffee was fit
and comfortable in already-paid-for kennels in Nairobi and waiting for me to
send for her once I had settled in my new home in Dares Salaam. Toffee later
travelled there by an East African Airways Comet 4 jet and when I opened her
box in the car on my way back from the airport she gave one very long meow
which I took as a telling off for my having been away a full year. For the record
she was boss' for another eight years before she died 'a grand old lady'.
My arrival in Dar es Salaam was also a happy one. With cabin trunk and a
great number of suitcases, I lined up at the Customs House where a tall good-
looking Sikh in an immaculate white turban and shirt and shorts said, to my
surprise, Welcome back', adding, 'Is this your luggage?' He chalked me through
without any problems at all.
Then I remembered. Eight years before, in Mombasa, I had edited a half-page
every two weeks for the Sikh community and he had been the young fellow who
had brought me the stories and given me a tea party when I left Mombasa to
return to Nairobi in late 1956. It was a tea party with a difference. After the wives
had served halved, boiled eggs garnished with paprika and other snacks (or
toasties or bitings as they were called), they disappeared, which was the sign
for bottles of Johnnie Walker Black Label whisky to appear on each table. My
half-hearted protest that I did not take spirits was brushed aside and half a tum-
bler of whisky was thrust into my hand. Fortunately, there was a carafe of iced
water to help things along.
Thus it was the Sikhs who introduced me to Johnnie Walker and I've been a
whisky drinker (in moderation) ever since. I might add that I count a number of
Sikhs, notably Tara Singh Dogra of Dar and Nairobi, among my dearest friends.
Perhaps, at this point, it would be opportune to 'take stock' of my situation. I
was now thirty-seven, a reasonably good newspaperman with my parents far
away in Sussex and my future home, Moyenne, 1,000 miles distant in an almost
straight line from Dar across the Indian Ocean.

If they had done nothing else, the months spent in i randhad provided me
with an opportunity to plan a little for the life ahead. Irealized that I was still
too young to cut away from my career and settle in Seychelles. In any case, I knew
that a good deal more money than I had at that time would be required to build
a house, reservoir, sea and retaining walls and generally develop what was a
waterless, if beautiful, hunk of bush.
As I had no time schedule I set to work with the two newspapers and found
the local people a deal more friendly and hospitable than those in Kenya. Put
simply, while the Kenyans were very much all head, the Tanganyikans were very
much all heart.
As with most companies employing expatriates, the staff arrivals and depar-
tures at The Standard were fairly rapid. Within a short sequence of time I found
myself chief sub-editor, news editor, assistant editor and finally editor and a di-
rector of both newspapers. Not surprisingly, I continued my book reviewing but
only the occasional play at the Little Theatre Club gave me a chance to dabble in
drama criticism. Also my work for Reuters was supplemented with demands
for stories from the Financial Times, The Times, The Daily and The Sunday Telegraph,
The Daily Express, The Argus group in South Africa and even the San Francisco
Chronicle.
Diplomatic days
The newspapers had a staff of 300, mainly Africans and Asians. Local Goans pre-
dominated in the printing works while the editorial staff was almost exclusively
expatriate or African. We did have two first-class Asian photographers, with
Adarsh Nayar still 'on the ball' today when it comes to catching a scoop.
Over the seven years I was with the group, I saw the expatriates reduced to
three, including myself, and the newspapers produced efficiently by enthusias-
tic, on-the-job trained young Tanganyikans. On one occasion, a year or so before
I left the post of editor, I recall asking President Nyerere affectionately known
as Mwalimu, the teacher), if he had noticed anything different about that morn-
ing's edition of The Standard.
We were having coffee and macaroons at what was one of our fairly regular
meetings on the verandah of State House, overlooking the Indian Ocean, with
its cool Arab style architecture providing a pleasant palatial atmosphere. When
Mwalimu replied, 'No', I said that was as it should be. The edition had been pro-
duced entirely by Tanganyikans, with my merely keeping a fatherly eye on
affairs.
He was delighted and I knew he had no intention of touching The Standard,
although his Tanu Party newspaper almost daily demanded that we be banned
or nationalized. Inevitably, the purchase of the company by Lonrho from the
Anderson family, who had owned it from the start, eventually led to its acquisi-
tion by the government, and my losing the editorship but staying on in the
30
country as public relations consultant to the government and its president.
The diplomatic round was very much part of the editor's job and Dar es Sa-
laam, with almost fifty embassies and high commissions, produced an average
of two cocktail parties and a dinner every night (except Sunday when I shared a
magnificent 'potluck' with a Greek friend, Dimmy, and his English wife, Joan).
A good example of how wearying it all became can be gauged from one
evening of many: cocktails at the United Arab Republic's ambassador's residence
in lounge suit and tie; back home to change into jeans and a sports shirt for pre-
dinner drinks and barbecue snacks at the American chargé d'affaires beach
cottage some six miles up the coast; and, lastly, a mad dash back home to switch
to black tie and tuxedo for dinner with the German ambassador and his wife,
arriving about 9 pm for one drink and then straight to the table for what was
always a superb meal. After coffee and cognac, it was home by midnight, hav-
ing called at the newspaper office to catch an early copy to check for mistakes.
Happily, it was a rare event to 'stop the press'.
It is little wonder that since arriving in Seychelles I have successfully resisted
such invitations. Not because I'm a hermit, as some would believe, but having
done it many times before it was not really necessary to repeat the performance.
In any case, crossing the bay to change at the Yacht Club and then return to
Moyenne late at night, often in roughish seas, can be troublesome.
Business luncheons, although cutting into the daily work routine and, there-
fore, kept to a minimum, usually proved more productive, intimate and lively
affairs. I enjoyed such meetings with the Countess of Huntingdon, better known,
perhaps, as the authoress Margaret Lane. Certainly she needed a 'civilized' break
during her trips to Tanzania to see lonedes, the internationally recognized 'snake
man'.
For her book, Life with Ionedes, she spent several weeks in his snake-infested
hut. On her visit she told me of using a box as a chair at suppertime only to dis-
cover later that it was full of reptiles. I was saddened to learn of her death while
writing this chapter.
Dar es Salaam in my day was also a hotbed of political intrigue and the home
of many exiled politicians, two of whom I remember particularly.
There was Sam Nujoma, a nice young fellow whose articles on what was
South-West Africa I published in The Standard, and who today is the much-
bearded President of Namibia. Certainly he deserved his success.
And there was Eduardo Mondlane who, as leader of Frelimo, often went into
Mozambique with his freedom fighters and kept himself fit by jogging on the
beach and swimming in Oyster Bay. He had a good sense of humour, always
laughing when I dived under him and surfaced as a Portuguese submarine...
bang, bang! Eduardo also had a great love of books and so it was one sunny
morning in a small beach cottage he opened a parcel of them and was blown to
pieces. I lost a friend and Mozambique a man who would have made a fine
president. Throughout my days as editor I was often saddened by the attitude

of many British diplomats who felt strongly that, having a British passport, I
should follow the London line; particularly with regard to what was then South-
ern Rhodesia. With one possible exception
Horace Phillips, later made
Ambassador to Turkey and knighted —
• they did not seem to understand that 1
was running national newspapers in Tanzania, whose approach to the Rhode-
sian issue was clearly emphasized when President Nyerere broke off relations
with Britain.
A few hours before the break came I was with the then British Deputy High
Commissioner in his recently converted Arab residence in Zanzibar. It was a
Sunday morning and noon was the deadline. Watching a dhow glide past with
her billowing white sail, I heard the diplomat say: But Brendon, you don't re-
ally think that he will carry out his threat? I was astonished that the diplomat
and his wife, who were an intelligent, delightful couple, could be so naive.
Mwalimu was a man of principle and he gave his answer, as I expected, at noon.
I was sorry to see the diplomats leave but then it was inevitable.
Of all the official visits, maybe the one which had a touch of magic about it
was that by Bobby Kennedy not long before his assasination. Mwalimu held a
comparatively small party for him on the front lawns of State House with
fairylights in the trees and a band playing in the background.
It was an evening of soft breezes and there was a definite festive air to the
occasion, a total absence of the usual 'politics in the bushes'. When Nyerere made
his entrance with an arm around Kennedy's shoulder the atmosphere became
electric. Some leaders have that charismatic touch and that night it was tangi-
ble.
Emperor Haile Selassie was capable of evoking similar emotions in spite of
his diminutive figure. He had been kind to me on a private visit to Addis Ababa
and told me that I would always be welcome in Ethiopia. At the end of an audi-
ence, he put his hand on my shoulder and asked how old I was. When I replied,
'Twenty-seven, sir', he smiled and I asked if he would like to be that age again.
'Oh, very much, he answered. Taking leave was a tricky business as you had to
bow three times, once on shaking hands, then halfway across the large room and
finally at the door, which was not set centrally, and the whole business had to
be done walking backwards. I made it and saw the Emperor again when he
waved as he passed me on the way to a small crater lake resort, Bishoftu, just
outside the city. He did not deserve to see his family's destruction or his own
ignominious end.
Feel of cold steel
My most hair-raising experience, apart from travelling in a plane with a suspected
bomb on board, was at the time of the army mutiny in Dar es Salaam in January
1964. Coming exactly one week after the Zanzibar Revolution, most people be-
lieved that the soldiers were staging a coup. I was awakened by a phone call
32
giving the news and decided to try and drive to the office. Half-a-mile from my
home I encountered a machine-gun emplacement but managed to talk my way
through, saying I was going home from work. The next barrier was outside the
Central Police Station, where the same story got me past a dozen soldiers with
machine-guns and rifles and, oddly, when I came to think about it later, camou-
flaged with bits of trees, hardly right for the city.
In the centre of Dar I met my match — two soldiers, one armed with a glint-
ing panga and the other with a rifle which he thrust through the car window on
to my bare chest. The cold steel did not feel good and the fact that the soldier
was sweating and obviously very nervous made matters worse. I told my re-
turning from work' story again but was ordered sharply to go back the way I
had come.
Making a U-turn as quickly as possible, I then faced the hazardous drive past
those earlier checkpoints. It is at such times that luck, coupled with a little inspi-
ration, is useful. Seeing an unmanned one-way street, I turned down it and drove
the wrong way as fast as the car would go, ending up at my office where I parked
the car out of sight in the printing yard.
The mutineers had taken control of the radio station, telephone and cable of-
fices among key points, but could not have known about my newspaper tele-
printer link with our Mombasa, Nairobi and Kampala offices. Thus I was able
to let the outside world know there was a happening in Dar.
As the morning wore on, quite a few of the staff, including a colleague, David
Martin, who represented the BBC among other media, found their way to work.
No soldiers appeared and word had it they were returning to the barracks.
I suppose foolhardy is the right word, but David and I decided to check the
story and see what was the true position. Sure enough, many of the checkpoints
were no longer manned, there were no camouflaged 'trees' in the street. But when
we arrived at the Central Police Station three soldiers with rifles surrounded
David's Volkswagen and made us get out. They then clambered inside and told
us to drive them to Colito Barracks outside the city along the north coast. With
a rifle pointing out of each window and the third straight ahead to shoot through
the windscreen we set off to be told: 'Faster, faster.'
And faster we went. I doubt whether that little car had ever moved so quickly.
The worst part of the drive was having to ignore the red lights at intersections
with traffic starting to return to normal. We had some narrow misses but made
it to the barracks, where about fifty soldiers were milling outside the main gates
with their rifles at the ready. David and I were hauled out of the car and at that
point, at the very least, I really expected to be beaten up. Luck was with us again
as one of the soldiers called out: 'They are OK. They gave us a lift. Let them go!
Never more smartly, David and I jumped into the little car and sinking as far
down in the seats as possible took off like a bomb.
There was a good end to the story. On our way to the barracks I had talked
with one of the soldiers and asked if they were staging a coup. He was genu-

inely astonished by this and said that the mutiny was only over more pay as they
received less than house servants and they also wanted to have their British of.
ficers replaced by Tanganyikans.
It was a world scoop and David's byline and mine were on the front pages of
most of the world's newspapers the next day, including our own.
Of course, the government could not allow the troops to continue to hold the
city to ransom and a few days later British marines from a Royal Navy aircraft
carrier captured the barracks. Elsewhere in the country, the soldiers surrendered
peacefully. They did get a pay rise and their own officers — and they also had a
spell under the discipline of crack Nigerian troops.
A friend for many years, Alex Nyirenda, had the misfortune to have been
Sandhurst-trained and promoted to half colonel at the time of the mutiny. Af.
terwards he was sent on long leave, continued to have a chequered life and with
his wife Hilda, became a popular figure in the business community. Today Alex
runs his own haulage business in Zambia where he settled with his wife and
family. In Tanzania, Alex is best known for having been the young lieutenant
who climbed Mount Kilimanjaro (19,340 feet) in 1961 to place the new flag and
torch of freedom that marked the country's independence on its summit.
Cars and cigars
Looking back on my life I realize how little time there was for anything personal
With two newspapers to run, an editorial to write every day, representing so
many overseas media, playing a role in the business community, as well as so-
cializing with the diplomatic corps, it filled all but an average of six hours sleep
in twenty-four.
I was never short of a hostess for the return cocktail and dinner parties or for
the many functions involving my presidency of the capital's Rotary Club and
other institutions. By any standards, it was a full life and a good one. There was
fun, excitement, danger and that touch of power.
Apart from moving into what was a fine hotel, the New Africa, in the centre
of Dar es Salaam, my life-style changed very little when I stepped down as edi-
tor. New management — the government — required a new boss to make the
change meaningful to the people but it was all carried out in the most pleasant
of ways.
Naturally it hurt to leave my staff, most of whom I had trained, and walking
through the open access office in total silence with all heads down I telt very sad
indeed. The staff were happy to be government-run but all came individually
to see me and there were tears on both sides. I was always thankful that my sec-
retary, Yasmin, was understanding enough to occasionally stop the interviews
and let me compose myself over a cup of coffee. They were a great staff, all of
whom I call friends to this day.
There was one funny story which lightened the day. Having ceremoniously
34
left my office I discovered that I had left my car keys on my desk. No way could
I return but, remembering there was a fallpipe and an overhanging ledge out-
side my window, I ran into the printing yard, shinned up the fallpipe and tapped
on my ex-secretary's window.
Surprised, she waved me to go away and then opened the window, handed
me the keys and laughing said: You don't work here any more, see you tonight
at the party! She was a good secretary and on her twenty-first birthday flew to
Washington to take up a post with the World Bank. Later she married a United
Nations diplomat.
There is one other tale concerning Yasmin. Standing in her office one day I
saw, through the glass partition, a State House driver in white uniform and
peaked cap coming towards us bearing a large, ripe pineapple. My comment:
Well, I never expected the president to send me a pineapple,' was totally
squashed when the driver handed the fruit to Yasmin with a note, 'From your
father. Puzzled, Lasked her: 'Who is your highly-placed father?' To my surprise
I discovered that her mother had remarried and was the wife of Thabit Kombo,
whom I regarded as the eminence gris of Zanzibar. He was wounded at the time
of the assassination of the island's president, Abeid Karume.
Zanzibar remains something of a mystery to me. All my newspaper colleagues
were detained or deported, at one time or another, in particular Mohamed Amin
whose first photographs I was happy to have published and who is still the best
photographer I have known. Mo, as he is affectionately called, lost twenty-eight
pounds in weight during the twenty-seven days he was detained by the
Zanzibaris.
Yet on no occasion was 1 troubled in the least on my regular visits to the is-
land hoth before and after what was a bloody revolution. My most satisfying
story was one which led to the halting of the introduction of people's courts and
the revenge killings which undoubtedly would have followed at the time.
That it was 'hairy' in Zanzibar for many people there is no doubt. Once, hav-
ing slept on board the Karanja in Dar es Salaam harbour in readiness for her
sailing the next morning, I was called to the gangplank where my young Zanzi-
bar reporter was waiting, visibly very nervous. He told me that he had been
tetched late at night and taken before the Revolutionary Council for allegedly
writing objectionable reports on the island's regime. One of the council mem-
bers had demanded: 'Hang him!' But a more sober mind had prevailed and the
reporter had been deported to Dar instead He had come to the ship to ask me to
tell my stand-in as editor not to send him back to Zanzıbar or he would be a dead
man. i had to obtain the captain's permission to leave the Karanja for an hour to
go to my office with the reporter and see that he was given a spot of leave and
then kept in Dar. I made it back to the ship with only a few minutes to spare
before she sailed.
The day before the got ernment took over the newspapers I was summoned
to State House where President Nverere handed me a letter he had just signed.
