The letter asked me to relinquish the editorship and thanked me for 'the
operation which you have given to me and to the Tanzania Government in the
past' and expressed Mwalimu's good wishes for my future. The change in
editorship was in no way an adverse reflection on my ability.
ithe letter and my reply were published on the frontpage of The Standard three
days later when the takeover was completed. (Formerly the Tanganyika Stand-
ard, the newspaper's name was changed following the Union of Tanganyika and
Zanzibar. It was to be renamed afterwards the Daily News.)
In my reply, I said it was with sadness only that eft my staff, most of whom
had grown up in journalism with me over the previous eight years. I am sure
you will appreciate that this is the most difficult break and the one which hurts
the most. Thave considerable affection for them all and indeed for Tanzania and
its people.
On the takeover I was reported as saying that I was particularly happy that
President Nyerere had emphasized that the newspapers would be run on the
basis that a newspaper only keeps the trust of its readers, and only deserves their
trust, if it reports the truth to the best of its ability and without distortion, whether
that truth is pleasant or unpleasant. This declaration would be welcomed by
newspapermen all over the world.
When I had finished reading Mwalim's letter in his office, he told me that
he had no wish for me to leave Tanzania and had a job for me. I remember feel-
ing very mixed-up and commenting that there could be only one editor.
"Ah well, said the president, 'that's so but I want you to become our public
relations consultant and also train some Tanzanians to do the job!
This made me laugh as I remarked that I'd spent most of my working life fight-
ing those people and telling them to advertise. Well, what better man for the
job then. You know all the answers,' he replied.
After a few seconds I said: 'OK I'll do it.'
Just like that?' asked Mwalimu.
'Yes, I said, 'I'm not ready to go to Seychelles and the job sounds like a new
challenge!
What car do you drive?' he asked.
'A Peugeot 404, the editor's car, I answered.
Well, take it and go off for a week; there must be the odd place in Tanzania
you've yet to visit.'
Taking leave of the president, I decided to drive to the Mikumi Game Reserve
and then to Lushoto where there was a small hotel noted for its friendliness and
good food. I did manage to relax thoroughly and unwind from the tension of
the takeover.
When I returned I was to hand in the car to the pool at the National Develop-
ment Corporation Building where I was to find an office four times larger than
my editorial den and a desk similarly bigger.
The point of this story is that when I handed in the car I commented: There
36
goes my freedom! I expected to have to indent for a vehicle in future but I was
astonished to be told by the head driver: 'Oh no, sir, here are the keys for your
new car over there.
What awaited me was a brand-new Peugeot 404 which was for my personal,
as well as official, use until I left Tanzania three-and-a-half years later. I can only
assume that my immediate response to carry on working in Tanzania had in-
spired Mwalimu to make such a kindly gesture. But it was only one of so many
that I came to know of during my time as the first public relations consultant in
Tanzania.
Apart from training young Tanzanians, the job entailed my looking after the
public relations image of some fifty companies worth a total investment of about
€50 million, including four textile mills, a brewery, tobacco company and oth-
ers dealing with a wide range of products from gemstones, steel, fertilizers and
tyres to instant coffee, meerschaum pipes, shoes, plastics and even my old news-
papers.
There were speeches and articles to write and the organization of trade stands
and official openings of companies, seminars and conferences. There was never
a dull moment and we introduced a slogan 'Sema Tena', or 'Say it again', for the
National Development Corporation which proved extremely popular.
In spite of the front-page publicity given me both for relinquishing the post
of editor and, a week later, becoming Tanzania's first public relations consult-
ant, many of my former hosts, particularly in the diplomatic field, appeared
unsure as to my standing with the government and the president. All but one
embassy continued to send along the invitations, but it was easy to sense their
doubts and their care not to offend politically.
President Nyerere, unexpectedly, made everything clear when he opened a
tannery in Moshi. On arrival, with all the guests, including the diplomatic corps
gathered in the building, Mwalimu made straight for me, saying: Welcome, wel-
come! You've come from a small job to a big one!' and shook my hand. He then
called for a drink and a waiter brought over a tray with a couple of beers and we
drank to each other. After that I was persona grata!
I enjoyed especially driving up from Dar to Nairobi — past Kilimanjaro, which
I had climbed in 1956 — to conduct business for Tanzania in the Kenyan capital.
It was a twelve-hour drive with two stops for fuel but always interesting, the
only danger being posed by game leaping from the bush in front of the car on
what was a superb tarmac highway.
It was my good fortune during this period to meet Andy Chande, whose mill
had been nationalized but who became general manager of all the milling com-
panies in the country. He was also chairman of my old newspaper company and,
among others, Air Tanzania. Andy and his wife, Jayli, I hold to be among that
handful of real friends one makes in a lifetime.
My initial two-year contract was extended and, in fact, I was never asked to
leave but, realizing I had completed my job, I knew it was time to go, albeit re-

luctantly. Over a beer at the beachside home of George Kahama, the general
manager of the National Development Corporation and a former minister and
ambassador, I brought up the subject and it was agreed I would say farewell
Tanzania in mid-year 1973.
We forgot completely that the first week in July is the busiest in the country's
calendar, with celebrations including an international trade fair marking Saba
Saba - seven, seven - the seventh day of the seventh month when Mwalimu's
political party was founded. So, not wishing to leave my team at the worst pos
enforced holiday in my old stomping ground, Mombasa.
sible time, I stayed on for a couple of weeks, missed my ship and enjoyed an
Mention of George Kahama, today again a minister, brings to mind an amus-
ing incident at a party in the grounds of the French ambassador's residence. f
was smoking a cigar when I heard a voice demand: A Havana, I trust? It was
the Cuban ambassador, a powerful looking man called Zorilla, and at the time
doyen of the diplomatic corps.
I said: Yes, a Monte Cristo. But, Mr Ambassador, why is it that I have to go to
capitalist Kenya to buy my good Cuban cigars which are unobtainable in social-
ist Tanzania?'
'A good point; he replied and looking towards where George was standing
laughingly added: 'He's to blame! As George was my immediate boss, I sug-
gested that I bring him over and when he arrived the ambassador put forward
the problem of purchasing good Cuban cigars.
'Oh, replied George, 'it's the fault of the tobacco company BAT]
'And who, retorted the ambassador 'is chairman of the company?'
To which George was forced to reply: Me!
The incident had a happy sequel. After George had left us, the ambassador
asked what I was doing at ten o'clock the following morning. Could I call and
have a sherry with him? Of course, I went along and found that the sherry was
accompanied by a box of Havanas.
We met at many more cocktail parties and, occasionally, the ambassador
would invite me for a 'sherry'. Just before I left Tanzania, he told me that he too
was going and proposed we should have a final sherry, one for the road. I made
that box of cigars last me the full year on Moyenne and I doubt whether I will
ever forget my Cuban friend's nice sense of humour and generosity to a fellow
sherry drinker and cigar smoker.
Incidentally, I have within reach at the moment, a pewter tankard bearing the
inscription, 'Cheers! From George and Janet', two more good Tanzanian friends.
My departure from Tanzania earned me many farewell gifts. I tried to dis-
suade my friends from loading me with too many heavy ebony carvings as my
luggage had already been sent by road to Mombasa for shipping. Priding my-
self on having only two suitcases and a briefcase to carry after ten years in the
country, I did not allow for the happy 'malice' of six close chums with whom I
spent that last evening. I left the sad little party with a carving I now call 'Charlie
38
(the Charlie being me!) Crying deeply carved tears, he was carrying a gun to
defend me from any unfriendly natives I would find in Seychelles. And he
weighed twelve kilos.
At the airport for the early morning flight, the checking-in officer, not sur-
prisingly, asked: What's that?' It was then I named him Charlie. Well, said the
officer, 'he can travel complimentary in the seat next to you to Mombasa. Bon
voyage! It was the final friendly gesture of so many during my decade in Tan-
zania.
Another treasured souvenir was my gift from the government: a tanzanite tie-
pin, the gemstone being found only in Tanzania. There were ebony and carved
walking sticks, a game of bao bao, an ivory-handled flywhisk (shades of
Kenyatta) and two sword sticks, sharp steel in leather sheaths. Even without
Charlie, I would have been well armed for the trip!
Mwalimu memories
Memories of my days in Tanzania would hardly be complete without a couple
of anecdotes relating to Mwalimu Nyerere and probably never told before.
They give a quick glimpse into the character of a man for whom I have the
greatest respect and affection. There are many today who criticise his economic
policies, forgetting the tremendous work he did for his people, the poor peas-
ants who made up the vast majority of the country's then population of thirteen
million. He was a man of principle and I guess the only African president to leave
office without a Swiss bank account. He never let down his friends and often
was too forgiving in nature.
The first story concerns an incident shortly after the nationalization of the
banks. I received a call just before I was due to leave the newspaper office and it
was dusk as I drove without security checks through the gates of State House
and parked outside the main entrance. My opposite number on the party news-
paper arrived at the same time. He was Ben Mkapa, later foreign minister — he
became President of Tanzania in 1995 — but then one of a duo nicknamed 'Ben
and Bren, the newspaper men'. (The Flowerpot Men were popular at the time).
We walked through the reception halls to the sea-facing verandah where we
found Mwalimu with his head in his hands. Looking up he motioned us to take
a drink from the trolley and raised a smile when he noticed we were sharing a
beer.
Mwalimu then asked us what was he to do with 'those young men' and at
this point we noticed for the first time a group sitting silently in the shadows
further along the verandah.
The President explained that, having managed to settle amicably the takeo-
ver of the Dutch bank, the youths had now caused a grave problem by entering
the bank, pulling down the framed portrait of Queen Juliana, which was dis-
played alongside that of himself, and demonstrating with it in the streets.

We knew, of course, of Mwalimu's personal friendship with Prince Bernhard,
who had a farm in northern Tanzania. It was abundantly clear that the presi
dent was not only embarrassed but genuinely distressed by the incident.
I recall asking who else knew about the affair and the president replied: 'No
one, I just called you.' The answer was simple. Instead of the world press re-
Porting "Tanzanian vouths insult Dutch Queen' we suggested making the story
a non-starter by putting out Nyerere apologises to Queen'. Clearly relieved,
Mwalimu asked us to do just that and said he would deal with the youths him-
self. It gave me an insight into that very human side of the President's character.
The second instance emphasizes the point. It concerns a man called George
Rockey, with whom I worked in Mombasa in 1956 and whom I was delighted
to find as press secretary to President Nyerere when I landed in Dar es Salaam.
George was a good and loyal friend and a hard worker. Sadly, he was a heavy
smoker and contracted lung cancer.
True to form, after learning of George's illness, Mwalimu decided that Tan-
zania should have a Director of Tourism in London and appointed George to
fil the post. Thus Tanzania had a first-class tourism boss in the UK but, equally
importantly, George was able to obtain the necessary medical care which was
not available in Dar. It was one more example of thoughtfulness and understand-
ing for others.
By coincidence, George and another newspaper colleague, Bill McAteer, both
married Seychellois girls. George's son, Tim, attended Seychelles College and
visited me on Moyenne before settling in London where for some time he ran a
champagne bar in St James.
Bill McAteer, recently editor of the Gulf Times in Qatar, has just published a
book on French and English relations in the early days of Seychelles history called
Rivals in Eden. With property on Mahe, I expect he and his family will become
neighbours fairly soon.
Two other authors have established homes recently on neighbouring Cerf Is-
land: Wilbur Smith, whose novel Wild Justice opens in Seychelles, and Bill Travis
whose books Beyond the Reef and Shark for Sale tell of life in the islands thirty years
ago.
As this book concerns my life on Moyenne it is not possible to dwell too long
on the twenty years I spent in Kenya and Tanzania.
However, each thread of my life has drawn together to meet on Moyenne.
Particularly pertinent to my story was a chance meeting in the early 1970s at the
New Africa Hotel in Dar es Salaam with Guy Sinon, a fellow passenger on that
first voyage to the islands. Guy was a fairly regular visitor to Dar on behalf of
the Seychelles People's Progressive Party (SPUP) meeting with members of the
Organization of African Unity (OAU) Liberation Committee, run incidentally
by an old friend and former press secretary to President Nyerere, now Briga-
dier Hashim Mbita.
Guy had a colleague with him this time whom he introduced as Albert Rene.
40
They asked me when I planned to return to settle on Moyenne and I explained
that it would be as soon as I had completed my job in Tanzania. Little did I real-
ize that I would live to see Albert become president, beat off a mercenary raid,
thwart an army mutiny, help to bring in a multi-party state and be returned as
president and, meanwhile, make me a citizen of Seychelles.
A family ready made
During my spell in Tanzania, I managed to visit Seychelles on four occasions,
staying on Moyenne for a few days each time, first in the old picnic house and
then in one of the two cottages I had built for the guardian and his family.
If you have a property in the Seychelles and do not live there it is essential to
appoint a guardian, otherwise 'visitors' would steal whatever can be carried
away, be it parts of buildings or fruit and timber.
My lawyer friend, Raoul, had put a man on the island as guardian soon after
I sailed away but had to get rid of him when he said that the twenty-two guinea
fowl which Raoul had introduced to Moyenne had all died. Raoul then found a
fisherman with the piratical sounding name of Sasa (François) Lafortune for the
job. Sasa had a wife, whom I always called Madame, and a family of six, three
boys and three girls.
The children, in descending order, are Marie France, Frank, Rene, Paul, Ma-
rina and Eliana. I mention them by name as all had a part to play in my early
days on Moyenne.
So, when I arrived on the island, I had a ready-made family awaiting me, the
mother being a superb cook. However she was very domineering and necessar-
ily so as Sasa tended to drink away his fish money and return with precious little
in the way of food for the family, perhaps a small loaf of bread and a tin of mar-
garine. I watched Madame on many occasions fishing from the rocks and sending
the youngsters up the mango trees for fruit. She collected large bundles of
firewood with which to keep the home fires — a Dover stove - burning and
was a dab hand with needle and thread and an old sewing machine.
No wonder that Seychelles was a matriarchal society. Alcohol was and still is
a major problem. Happily, with strict control drugs are not as big a threat to the
teenagers as they might have been. An obligatory three-year sentence can be a
good deterrent. But with cannabis easily grown in our humid climate, drug con-
trol will continue to be an uphill struggle as the population continues to rise. All
of this, and much more, I was to discover after stepping ashore on Moyenne.
On one holiday visit to Seychelles I stayed at the Continental Hotel, situated
directly across the street from the Anglican Cathedral and the Central Police Sta-
tion. There were two main rooms, each with a small balcony from which a good
view of the bay and Moyenne could be obtained. Premier Building had not ap-
peared and the piece of rough land next to the hotel was a good place to park a
car. The second room was occupied by an elderly man who lived on a liquid

diet in the bar below and was helped by the waitress to his bed each night, where
he slept with a very loud snore.
Mv lunches were taken at a long table in what was a popular dance hall at
weekends. They were enlivened by the rehearsals, on a raised platform at the
far end. by a group still well-known. Mickey and the Buccaneers. Micker
Mancham, whose eldest brother was to become the first president of the Sev-
chelles, and I struck up a friendship and he welcomed my constructive criticisms
of their music and gave me a signed photograph of the group in action.
When I came to pay my bill before boarding the karanja for Bombay, I was
told by the woman in charge that it was only E4. Explaining there must be a mis-
take as I had kept my room even while visiting Moyenne and had had many
meals there, she said: Mr Mickey had to go to Praslin today but sent you his
greetings for a good voyage and instructed me that f4, the normal cost for one
day, is all you are to be charged! He also said, Thank you for your kind help?
When I got back to East Africa, I had his photograph and the Buccaneers pub-
lished in the leading newspapers with a short article. It was all I could do in retum
and they certainly deserved the publicity.
Sadly, Mickey and I were not to meet again as he had a fatal scooter accident.
Seychelles lost a great personality, as talented as he was generous, whose songs
are still heard over the radio today. A favourite of mine, and many other
Seychellois, is Going back to the Seychelles.
On 14 July, 1973, I said kwaheri (goodbye) to Tanzania, leaving behind the hap-
piest of memories of a charming and hospitable people. It would only be fair to
add that, had I not found my 'destiny' already in Seychelles, I doubt whether I
would have left my Tanzanian friends.
Mark you, the majority of them thought me quite mad to go and live on an
island in the middle of the Indian Ocean, however beautiful it might be, espe-
cially when I told them it was without water and overgrown by bush.
President Nyerere was an exception. When I went to his home at Masani to
say farewell, he commented: 'Oh, yes, I think you'll make it. Throughout my
life I've had a reputation for talking too much but that day I found I had little to
say. It was a sad time and I was also taking leave of Joan Wicken, who has helped
Mwalimu with his writing for many years and was a particularly good friend to
me in Dar.
I realized that my going back to the land was the sort of challenge Mwalimu
would have taken up willingly. He was happy always to return to his village.
Of course, I was hardly going back to the land. Indeed, apart from a touch of
printing ink, I had never before got my hands dirty.
Now all that would change, the days, months and years ahead would prove
far more manual than mental. Moyenne was bound to be demanding if it wasto
be developed as I wished and there never was any likelihood of my becoming a
lotus-eater, beachcomber or vegetable, as had been suggested.
42
Return to Eden
My voyage to settle in Seychelles was made in a small vessel of some 500 tons
called the Nordvaer. The agent told me that when I got to the dockside at Mom-
basa I was not to look up for the ship but down.
Well, she was small but so was the cost of my passage, a mere £26. I shared a
cabin with a young English graduate, Tim Willing, now town gardener of
Broome, Australia, who was studying and seeing the world at the same time.
He spent most of his month in the islands on Moyenne and was very helpful in
what proved to be my first frustrating and hectic days as an islander.
We did not travel directly to Mahe but stopped for a day at Aldabra, the larg-
est coral atoll in the world and home to some 160,000 giant land tortoises. I was
able to go ashore through the kind invitation of a fellow passenger, David
Stoddart, who was concerned with Aldabra then as the island was run by the
Royal Society, and today as a member of the board of the Seychelles Island Foun-
dation which takes care of what has become a World Heritage Site and totally
protected conservation area.
Some idea of the size of Aldabra can be gauged from the fact that the whole
of the island of Mahe could be accommodated inside its lagoon. Teeming with
wildlife (ashore and underwater) it is also home to one of the world's last flight-
less birds, the rail. It was a lucky stopover for me and maybe it was seeing all
those tortoises, rather like large pebbles on a beach, that led eventually to my
establishing a colony on Moyenne which today numbers forty and is probably
the largest private sanctuary for them in the world.
And so, on to Mahe and the granitic islands of the archipelago. We arrived in
port mid-afternoon after sighting them in the distance some hours before. The
Nordvaer was not a fast ship. First Silhouette appeared on the horizon and then
North Island over to the portside with the mountainous mass of Mahe gradu-
ally taking shape and seeming to grow out of the sea.
It was a perfect day with a shimmering sun and a deep blue sea. The peaks of
Mahe were wreathed in cloud but as the thick green vegetation became clearer
we were able to pick out buildings and a new hotel along Beau Vallon Bay.
St Anne, with its high peak, hid Moyenne from my gaze until almost the last
minute as we swung round North East Point and made for the new port which
had recently been completed at Victoria, the Nordvaer pulling alongside and sav-
ing the problems of launches and lighters for passengers and cargo.
Once again, Moyenne did not let me down. She was just as I remembered her,
the highest rocks.

The western side of Moyenne faces Victoria so the sun was spotlighting the
island as well as the others in the necklet of bay islands, revealing them in all
their lush tropical splendour. I was home and happy to be so. My 'idyllic'
thoughts were quickly dispelled when the medical officer came on board and I

oculations I had been given before sailing. However, it was all very simply and
found that I needed a cholera jab as well as the smallpox and yellow fever in.
painlessly done on the boat deck by the doctor and his pretty assistant.
Immigration formalities had been completed in the ship's saloon so it left only
customs to be seen ashore. They maintained their previous reputation for speedy
service and passed my personal luggage — crates and motorboat to be cleared
later — with just a warning that spearguns were now illegal in the islands fol
lowing the killing of too many fish along the coral reefs. This I still believe to be
a sensible ban, in keeping with the establishment of the St Anne Marine National
Park which encompasses the islands of St Anne, Round, Long, Cerf, Cachée and
A taxi took Tim and myself to Captain Tregarthen's guest house situated at
St Louis, about half a mile from the city centre, overlooking the harbour and, of
course, Movenne. The captain had been harbourmaster and owner of th
schooner, Iron Duke. He looked and was an irascible old sea dog.
The guest house was above the Marie Antoinette bar and restaurant whích
had a loud and lively disco, the Grass Roots, which thumped away until the early
hours of the morning and probably accounted for the remarkably low prices they
charged for bed and breakfast, the latter consisting of tea and a banana. Today
the Marie Antoinette is one of the leading Creole restaurants in the islands.
It had been two years since I was in Seychelles and there were noticeable
changes. Many new buildings were under construction, a sports stadium and a
modern Pirates Arms had been established and the number of vehicles had in-
creased dramatically.
A traffic jam by the Clocktower, a new system of one-way streets and the re-
port of seven accidents in one day were enough to let me know that the tempo
of life on the 'mainland' had speeded up. It was then I thanked my lucky tropi-
cal stars that in choosing an island in the sun I had got one without any roads,
cars, scooters or even a bicycle.
There is a well-known story that soon after the second car was introduced on
the island of La Digue it turned a corner and had a head-on collision with the
first.
It takes a little time to become used to the fact that one is an islander, that a
car is no longer either a status symbol or an everyday necessity. On the other
hand, a boat ceases to be a luxury item and is essential as the sole means of com-
munication with the outside world, with the supermarkets, merchants, doctors,
dentists and as a means of visiting friends.
And it takes a while to be sure whether an island is just what one wants after
the initial novelty of the situation has worn off. The very remoteness could be a
bore to some people and if, for example, I had to work on Mahe, a twice-daily
trip across a bay which is often far from calm would become a commuter's night-
mare.
Sometimes, I wonder, if I would have managed to settle in so easily on
44
Moyenne had I married. It would have taken a very special sort of woman to
give up an active 'civilized life for a home without piped water, taps, toilets or
showers, without electricity, a telephone or the normal 'shop around the cor-
ner' for the odd, forgotten groceries.
As the months went by, I did acquire a kerosene fridge and a gas cooker, but
those early days were definitely primitive and it was almost twenty years be-
fore the advent of freezers, water heaters and air-conditioners for the storing of
goods away from the fungus-growing humidity.
Children would have created another problem. Depending on the age, over-
seas schooling would have been inevitable, although the holidays would have
been fantastic for any youngsters.
So, my bachelor status was an advantage and I have come to understand that
one cannot expect to have everything in life and the island was a huge bonus at
the end of a successful and enjoyable thirty-year career.
Bag and baggage
It is fairly obvious that the problems of an islander and his freight do not end
until all are safely landed on his property, but not always even then.
My major worry was finding transport from the port to the island. At one time
I had visualized moving bag and baggage in my small motorboat. Apart from
the fact that there was no firm anchorage laid down at Moyenne and the seas
were running high, with a storm blowing itself out from the south-east, such an
exercise would have entailed at least eighty trips of four-and-a-half miles each
way. Soft furnishings, bed linen, books, paintings and other fragile items of per-
sonal and household effects would have got a good soaking from salt water on
the way over, if not during the offloading from a small boat bobbing like a cork
in the choppy waters. Yes, I lived and learned as an islander, often the hard way.
While attempting to sort out freight problems, I had the 'traumatic' experi-
ence of seeing my crisp new motorboat swung by crane and lowered directly
on to the sea. It did not help to have a friend relate how, only a few days before,
an unspecified 'they' had dropped a boat overboard, letting her slip from the
cradle and do a perfect nosedive, with engines and all, to the bottom of the har-
bour. In the event, the Nordvaer's skipper, Guy Savy, saw my Sema Tena lowered
safely and, with me astride her bows, towed to the Yacht Club for servicing be-
fore an official launching.
In conversation at the newly-built Pirates Arms, someone suggested that I
should hire the 'sea-truck' to take my belongings to Moyenne. The 'sea-truck'
turned out to be a bright-yellow landing craft with powerful engines and an en-
ergetic crew of three brothers, David, Mike and Alan Pool. It was agreed, if I
move.
cleared customs, that they would come straight to the dockside and make the

taken part in the 1977 coup and was then minister for defence, Ogilvy Berlouis
When the sea-truck' arrived it was found that the crates were too big for load
ing and we opened them to manhandle the 107 packages into the craft. The
crossing was a bit rough but David managed to come alongside the seawall on
Moyenne and everything was landed safely.
Then came the backbreaking task of carrying them the fifty feet up to the old
picnic house on the plateau. Tim, David, his brothers and I sweated under a blaz
ing sun as we moved the boxes, many of them extremely heavy. There were three
brassbound wooden Zanzibar chests full of books which we left to the end, none
of us feeling quite capable of managing then up the steephil. It was fortunate
that Sasa and a strong young fisherman friend, Jim, appeared and relieved us of
this final burden.
We had been worried also by the rapidly falling tide, so, as soon as the last
box was away, we headed back for Victoria and the welcome prospect of long
cool beers.
It had been the hardest work I had done since leaving the army, more years
ago than I cared to remember, and I realized that an islander has to be fit and
able to tackle tough physical labour.
In the course of the next few weeks, the pounds fell away and my swimming
trunks, shorts and slacks all fitted comfortably. As I took in the odd notch on
my belt I also started to feel much better in health. A sensible diet, based on fresh
fish and fruit, helped, if offset somewhat by a greater increase in liquids, includ-
ing beer.
With all household effects now on Moyenne it was time to go shopping for
my first island supplies. The list was not that long but it was by the carton and
included corned beef, baked beans, powdered milk, tinned butter and cheese.
There were tea, coffee, sugar and rice, orange squash, Coca Cola and beer,
matches, soap, toilet paper, toothpaste and razor blades. On the 'heavy side' were
a 14lb hammer, jacksaw, large and small axes, pangas, spades and secateurs. And,
having seen that lighting on the island was merely the half-shell of a coconut
filled with oil and a smokey wick, top of the list was a kerosene lamp and a good
fuel supply. Hopefully, bread and perishable goods were to be brought over by
Sasa when he returned from selling his fish on Mahe.
Finally, on 17 August, 1973, a day I still celebrate, Tim and I sat atop all the
supplies on the Long Pier slipway waiting for the prison boat to take us to
Moyenne.
Now this may sound a little strange but the prison boat — then under François
Savy's command — still plies regularly between Long Island and Mahe, and over
the years both guards and prisoners have proved to be good neighbours. We
46
crossed the bay comfortably and, the tide being high, the prison boat, an old life-
boat of the ill-fated tanker Ennerdale which some years earlier had hit an
uncharted rock and sank just off Mahe, was able to go alongside the seawall and
land us safely on the island with all the supplies.
At last, I had arrived. But there was not much time to ponder it all. The sup-
plies had to be carried up to the plateau and then along to one of the two cottages
which I had decided to make my home. They were the first of countless cargoes,
from loaves of bread to sacks of cement, to make that uphill journey in the years
ahead. However, the magnificent views were sufficient compensation for the ef-
fort.
It was unfortunate that the old Creole-style picnic house, with its wide cool
verandah, had been ravished by the weather and lack of care to such an extent
that it was uninhabitable. We collected mattresses and sheets from one of the
boxes brought over in the 'sea-truck' and, after a supper of curried fish and rice,
fruit and coffee, decided to have an early night.
Neither Tim nor I had reckoned on the veritable animal farm which we had
inherited. At 3am the cockerels started their dawn chorus, presumably mistak-
ing the full moon for a rising sun. But, never mind, the sun came up eventually
and they were able to crow on and on.... Added to this were sympathetic grunts
from three pigs, a variety of odd sounds from hens, chickens, ducks, cats and
last, but by no means least, the howling of 'IDon't Care', a white, smooth-haired
terrier reminiscent of the dog on the HMV record label.
On first acquaintance, 'I Don't Care' — she was brought to the island and
named by a young fisherman, André Beaufond, who became a good friend —
was the most demoralized animal I had come across for years. Not having met
another of her species since she was a puppy, I'm sure she was uncertain just
what she was. It took weeks of patient handling to gain her confidence and for
her to lose the habit of scurrying away with her stumpy tail between her legs
every time anything moved, even the wind in the palms.
Battle of the ants
Our first morning on Moyenne started perfectly. We breakfasted under sway-
ing palms, but not so close as to be hit by a falling nut or frond, and to the
accompaniment of the gentle murmur of the incoming tide. We ate pawpaw
sprinkled with sugar and lemon, an omelette, bread and butter and home-made
jam, washed down with large mugs of tea. There is no doubt one develops a thirst
on an island and small cups become tedious.
It was just as well that we relaxed in readiness for the work ahead for little
did we know the major exercise that faced us. Tim and I had talked of quietly
slipping into the island way of life, of taking our time in unpacking and moving
the personal and houshold goods we required from the picnic house to the cot-
tage. There was no need to rush matters and we could swim and idle away a

few days after all the nonsense of our free that thshopping expeditions.
So much for plans, First, we discovered that the list of boxes and their con-
tents had been left in the locker at the Yacht Club. It would have to be a game of
guessing by shape and weight what they contained. A good game with 107 case
to choose from.
We walked along the path from the cottage to the old house, discovering ar
almost primeval beauty in one small valley, rich in coconut and oil palm
takamaka, mango and mandarin orange trees. It ended at a delightful sandy cov
sheltered by enormous granite boulders. We looked up and spotted jackfruit
breadfruit and tamarind forming in the giant trees nearer the picnic house, and
we gazed across the channel to little Round Island and the mountains on Mah
be ged in early morning sunlight, quite breathtaking in their cloud-capped splen-
dour.
And then we came down to earth with a vengeance. I won't say that we pan-
icked but it was pretty close. Certainly we started to move and fast like ants -
in fact, like the thousands of white ants, termites, which had attacked the pack-
ing cases left in the house. In the five days since we had deposited the cases from
the 'sea-truck, the ants had managed to eat their way into every case which had
been standing on the old wooden floor and this meant ninety altogether.
It was no joke, and I am sure the author of Claret, Sandwiches and Sin would
not have approved of his book being so popular that it had been half-eaten in so
short a time. My immediate concern was for the safety of the more valuable items,
the oil paintings and Zanzibar chests. Happily only one chest had been 'nibbled.
We dealt swiftly with those particular ants, placing the chest in bright sunshine
to finish off any we might have missed.
The Battle of the Ants was on — a battle which lasted three days. The boxes
containing china, glassware and kitchen utensils were put on one side as hav-
ing inedible contents. We also felt that my collection of ebony Makonde carvings,
although wood, were of such a hard kind that it was unlikely the ants would
make much of an impression on them but go for the more readily crunchable
books, sheets and furnishings.
We worked on as the sun rose and perspiration streamed down our backs.
The packers had done a good job and there was a mountain of paper, mainly
my old newspapers, to be burnt by the time evening came. Every book had to
be leafed through and the spines checked for ants. It was getting dark and eleven
hours after we started we sat down to dinner with the knowledge that all the
more important and useful items, as well as those with the greatest sentimental
value, had been cleaned and moved to the cottage and put in places where they
would be needed — rather than dumped down to provide another major mov-
ing exercise later.
After listening to the radio for a while, Tim and I voted for an extra early night.
Even the island menagerie was unable to prevent us sleeping soundly until dawn.
Over those first days we massacred thousands of white ants. Obviously they
48
had been living under the floorboards of the old house, floorboards which must
have been totally antproof as they were still solid when we removed them dur-
ing rebuilding. My years in East Africa had given me a healthy respect for the
hard-working ant. Its feats of endurance and ability to carry colossal loads never
failed to fascinate me. But, frankly, after my experience on Moyenne I would
not care if I never saw another ant, especially those of the white variety which
are particularly nauseating with their tiny sluglike bodies, unpleasantly
squashable. Unfortunately, the ants are still with me on Moyenne, white, brown
and a large black stinging variety. I guess there are millions of them.
The unpacking revealed a large number of items which I had forgotten, in-
cluding a set of Stuart crystal wine glasses and souvenirs of travels in the Far
East and Russia. It was a tribute to the packers in Dar es Salaam that in all 107
boxes only one small glass dish was broken. I now appreciate the true worth of
the professional packer and the greater uses to be made of a newspaper.
In retrospect, I realized that the ants had done me a good turn. I was very com-
fortably settled in the cottage with one room as a lounge-cum-study housing my
best paintings, chests, radio, record-player and a wide range of books. Surpris-
ingly, the stark grey cement walls set off the paintings, emphasizing the bright
colours of the mainly Maasai and other African scenes depicted. To open the door
on the tiny cottage was like rolling back the stone of Aladdin's cave. Unfortu-
nately, the only treasure to be found there on occasion was 'I Don't Care', the
dog who, to my chagrin, I discovered was sharing her affections for me with that
of a young black pig tethered below Sasa's cottage. Still, to each his taste, as they
say.
Across the open central verandah of my cottage home was the second room
which we fixed up as a bedroom-cum-bathroom-cum-store. With a comfortable
divan in the lounge, I found it to my liking to sleep there so Tim took over the
bedroom proper. I decided also to continue to sleep in the lounge after Tim's
departure. It was pleasant and it gave a good feeling at the start of the day not
only to awaken to the sunlight on the palms but also to have old friends in the
way of paintings and books around me.
Coral Cove
Having completed the move to the cottage, I could now concentrate on the is-
land itself, an island I found far more wildly overgrown than expected, with the
few old paths blocked by shrubs and trees which had firmly established them-
selves in the years since the last owner to live on Moyenne, Miss Emma Best,
had left in 1915. Imagine any garden neglected for a year, let alone an island for
half a century.
To reach the other side of Moyenne, a boat or long swim was necessary. To-
day it is simple to say that it took eight months to cut the path around the island
and three-and-half months to level part of a hillside to accommodate a new house.

In practice it was sheer hard work, starting at 7.30 in the morning and finishin
at dusk. Where no path existed previously I had to hack through the thickl
entwined branches of prune de France (coco plum) with its deep roots a
back-breaking problem.
The path, which turned out to be exactly one mile, had to be 'walkable' and
over to the small cemetery and finally along to the cottages, the plateau and t
this meant taking it round giant boulders, down to the far bay and then up and
it, it seemed like many, many more.
main landing. Visitors tell me it seems more like two miles. When I was cutting
One of my favourite spots on the island is Coral Cove and it was natural that
a track through the bush following the course of an old path to a house built for
I should decide to make it the first accessible point. Tim, Sasa and myself opened
stray dogs by Miss Best who owned Moyenne in the early 1900s. The ruins of
the house and old rainwater catchments are on the southern side of the cove in
a garden dominated by shady tamarind tree lein down to wie
cent-shaped sandy beach. The lagoon, alive with turquoise parrot fish and
friendly twin-tailed 'cocos', is protected by the reef, and the crashing waves make
splendid background'music. Happily, itis the same today as twenty yearsago.
On that first morning a little of my pleasure was tempered by the sight ofa
jungle of driftwood and thick scrub to be cleared, including thousands of young
acacia trees, very much a weed on Moyenne.
The sea had seen to it that the beach was clear and clean to the high-water
mark, but after that nature had other ideas.
No matter, the potential was there and we set to work, sweating under the
sun, having a swim now and again to refresh ourselves, and burning the wood,
branches and leaves on the beach. I built the fire, starting with coconut husks,
near the water's edge so that as the tide came in it would gradually wash away
the embers and prevent any flare-up after we had left.
Everything went well, although the crackling and high-shooting flames of the
coconut and pandanus leaves made me apprehensive in case sparks carried on
the wind might set fire to the dry bush. If that happened I had visions of the whole
island going up in smoke and, as fire chief without a brigade, engine or even
water, I would have been powerless to do anything. Fire, of course, is a hazard
which has increased with the advent of tourists.
On that first day, the only items of any interest which were burnt acciden-
tally were my own feet. Going in a little too close to the fire, I sank in the soft
sand up to my ankles and very smartly was seen by an amazed Tim and Sasa to
be doing a Red Indian war dance on the beach. Three blistered toes and tender
ankles were the penalty I paid. It could have been much worse. After four hours
of fire the sand was dangerously hot. In fact, it was my hottest day in the tropics
so far.
Watching the tide come in always has a certain fascination for me and observ-
ing it gradually creeping up on the red hot embers somehow added to this
50
fascination, rather like that of a youngster seeing the tide slowly demolish his
sandastle. Not every wave reached the fire and those which did touched only
a small part at a time. However, within half an hour, in a succession of hisses
and clouds of smoky steam, the fire vanished but not, unfortunately, the char-
coal embers. These in their thousands swirled up and down and across the beach
with the still incoming tide. The next morning, after another high tide during
the night, the beach resembled a spotted dick pudding.
It took almost a week to finally clear away the charcoal, the final bits joining
old bottles, broken glass and tin cans in a large hole, the only means of dealing
safely with rubbish on the island. Today the rubbish problem has been made
much worse by a wide range of plastic items which appear with every tide but
they are all bagged and taken away weekly for disposal on Mahe.
Coral Cove was transformed with the way clear to a rainwater well and a de-
lightful shady nook for relaxation amid the granite boulders. Whatever state of
the tide, the cove was now accessible, which led, in turn, to the realization that
the cedar forests on the peak were not — and that a good path from the cove to
the top of the island would not only be useful but an essential part of any circu-
lar route around the island.
So Pirates Path' became our second project and it was a tough one, taking a
full month to complete, by which time Tim would be in Sri Lanka on the next
stage of his journey to Australia and a new life 'Down Under', which, come to
think of it, applied equally to Moyenne, being four degrees below the Equator
ourselves.
Visitors often ask me why I did the manual work myself. Why didn't I em-
ploy some husky men and act the foreman? Mainly, I suppose, because I
thoroughly enjoyed the total change of work. I had time to ponder, to think things
out carefully and I also came to know the island more intimately and kept fit.
Within two months, my mind and body were attuned to Moyenne. I had worked
out a rough development plan for the island and I had become used to the sun
and the humidity.
When I first arrived on Moyenne I had to stop a third of the way up to the
peak to rest. Although fairly active, my previous deskbound life had not pre-
pared me for scrambling over rocks, climbing 200 feet at a go, for living on an
island.
After those first two months my body, tanned a deep brown, gave no more
sunburn problems, my feet and hands had passed through the blister stage and
I was sufficiently acclimatized, if not to run up to the peak, at least to no longer
resemble a puffing billy.
There were other useful lessons, such as how to climb the granite boulders
and slither safely down leaf-covered slopes. Cuts and bruises often prove diffi-
cult in the tropics, even with a well-stocked medical chest.
With Tim gone, I worked on alone, setting specific tasks for Sasa, who con-
centrated on cleaning up the main landing below the old picnic house. Bright

green and brown lizards, wasps, hornets and large black-and-yellow spiders
were my companions on the bush-clearing expeditions, apart from I Don't Cars
who always left me with my liquid lunch for something more substantial at the
I also began to get to know the different types of vegetation on Moyenne. Here
the leaves and flowers which I showed her. Then a cross-check with a useful lo
Madame was helpful, providing the Creole name for the trees and shrubs from
cal publication - D Bailey — gave me both the botanical and the family nam
For example, a fine timber tree with flowers like orange blossom was the popu-
lar takamaka, or Alexandrian laurel with the botanical name Calophyllum
inophyllum.
Movenne proved to be an education in itself. I soon recognised more than 100
different palms, trees and shrubs found on the island and discovered it was dif
ficult to stand at any point without being able to identify at least a dozen species.
There was something very special also about the granite rocks and boulder
with their unusual and distinctive shapes, some to which I quickly gave names:
elephant, tortoise, stallion, sleepy chicken and the crown. I still find it particu-
larly pleasant, in the cool of early evening, to sit on a giant boulder and feel th
retained warmth of the sun against my skin. If the boulder is at the water's edg
and facing a setting sun, so much the better.
Working alone did not mean that I was at all lonely. There was much to oc
cupy my mind as well as improving my skills as a manual labourer. I had started
to keep an island log and decided to make a once-weekly trip to Mahe for shop.
ping and to attend the Rotary Club. Even today, if l am seen in the city on another
day, I will be asked jokingly: 'Hey, it's not Tuesday, is it?'
With my new fishing rod and snorkelling gear untouched, I had to rethink
also about the speedboat anchored at the Yacht Club. It was obvious that what
the island required was a work boat, capable of carrying cement and blocks, tim-
ber and pipes, one that Sasa could handle.
Luckily, I found a man looking for a speedboat, sold it to him and with the
proceeds bought an 18ft, clinker-built, open boat — all takamaka wood and cop-
per nails. Sasa was delighted and we no longer had to catch the prison boat,
although they were always willing to give us a lift when needed. Strong and sea-
worthy, we named our new boat the Amy Jane after my Essex grandmother.
Looking back, it was a period of fairly rapid adjustment, a time in which I truly
became an islander for life.
Man Friday
On my visits to Seychelles from Tanzania, Sasa, accompanied by one of his sons,
had met me in his pirogue, a local canoe-shaped boat. And, on each occasion I
failed to remember that youngsters grow rapidly. Thus on my second and third
visits I greeted Sasa and added Hello, Frank' to which the boys in successive
52
turns replied: 'Non, non, monsieur, me Rene and 'Non, non monsieur, me Paul.
It had been my good fortune to see the island on those all-too brief visits
through the eyes of the boys, who, despite speaking only Creole, managed, with
all the enthusiasm of youth, to show me their favourite spots, the tiny coves and
caves, and to take me fishing and octopus hunting with two sharpened sticks to
lever them from their holes.
When I arrived on Moyenne, Frank, then twenty-one, was on Praslin and Rene,
nineteen, and Paul, seventeen, on Mahe. The eldest daughter, Marie France, had
married a policeman in Victoria and only Marina and Eliana were at home help-
ing mother.
Frank and Paul came over and worked for a day or so with me but I guess it
was a bit too hard to be away from the bars, football, cinema, dance halls and
girls. I was resigned to bush-bashing on alone, with help from Sasa when he was
not putting down or lifting his fishtraps and going over to Mahe to sell and drink
his catch.
But on 5 December, 1973, Rene appeared and started to work alongside me. I
did not know it then but he would be dubbed my Man Friday' by newspapers
all over the world and twenty years later would still be with me as manager and
friend.
I do recall telling him that I wanted someone to work with rather than for me.
He now understands what I meant but I doubt whether he did at the time. There
was just a grin and he got on with the job.
Although he had been a young man about town, Rene settled into island life
easily enough. Some years later, watching Peter O'Toole and Richard Roundtree
in the film Man Friday, I realized how similar our life had been in those early
days.
We worked hard and we made our own fun. We would clear an area of bush
and then go swimming and catch a couple of fish to barbecue on the beach; break
rocks and play football or race each other along the sandspit. We also enjoyed
what we called 'La Guerre', a game something like the antics practised by In-
spector Clouseau and Cato in the Pink Panther films, almost as rough but nowhere
as damaging to our sparse furnishings.
Playing tricks on each other was, I suppose, one way of easing the boredom
of chopping through some tough stretches of prune de France. The first to see a
hornet nest would never tell the other in the hope that he would be unwary
enough to hit the nest with his panga and have to throw himself flat on the
ground, the only way to escape multiple and very painful stings. A bit childish,
perhaps, but people do pay to see actors slip on banana skins. In the evening
there was always time for a game of cards, dominoes, chess or a local form of
draughts called dam.
Madame made breakfast for us after a mug of coffee at 6 and we would be
away by 7.30 until it was too dark to see to work any more.
Saturdays were different. At noon we took the Amy Jane to Mahe where we

showered and changed into slacks and sports shirts at the Yacht Club. The pat-
ema irrespective of what the film might be.
tern was much the same: a soccer match in the stadium (Rene backing Rangers
if I supported Rovers), chicken and chips at the Pirates Arms and then the cin
A couple of drinks and then back to Moyenne well after midnight, easy when
there was a full moon, rather more difficult getting through the pass in the reef
if it was cloudy. Fortunately, we made Sunday a day of rest when I wrote letter.
and Rene would possibly make a fishtrap. Working in our brief swimming cos.
tumes kept laundry to a minimum and Madame washed our sheets and towels
better than any Creole food I ever had in restaurants on Mahe.
She also provided our evening meal, usually grilled fish or a curry, but it was
Looking back, Robinson Crusoe and Man Friday were appropriate tags in
those days and even todav, bearing in mind that after the visitors have left du
ing the week there are only the two of us on the island, although we do have a
mod cons.
With Rene on the island, I decided to devote all the time possible to cutting
the main path. It was a long and arduous task but not without surprises. Reach-
ing the top of a hill, some 100 feet above Coral Cove, it was necessary, because
of the increasingly steep incline and large rocks, to make a right-angle tum
through the bush and trust that we would succeed in reaching a convenient point
to descend to Pirate Cove. There was no shade at all along this stretch and at the
end of each day we were exhausted with the cutting and by hours of exposure
to the sun.
Imagine our relief and delight when chopping through the last of the prune
de France we found a small copse of tall cedars and the remains of what clearly
had been a house. A little detective work and we discovered that it was where
Julie and Melidor, the first owners of Moyenne, had lived for forty-two years.
They had been given the island when they married in 1850. Melidor Louange
was seventeen and a carpenter, and Julie Chiffon sixteen and a seamstress. The
children of freed slaves, they had lived with their mothers, Suzie and Marie, on
neighbouring St Anne Island.
The siting of the house was good. It caught the cool winds from both mon-
soons and stood just above Pirate Cove, the nearest landing from St Anne. As
there was no water on Moyenne it would have been necessary to ferry it across
to the cove for drinking, cooking and washing and, with three children to care
for, it must have been a daily trip.
In spite of close scrutiny of all possible records in the archives, my detective
work never disclosed who were the fathers of either Julie or Melidor or which
one, with a conscience no doubt, made them the handsome present of the island.
A reasonable guess would be the father of Julie, as the island was put in both
their names and in those days had it been the father of Melidor the likelihood
was it would have been in his name only.
Four of the leading landowners at the time witnessed the marriage certificate;
54
none, however, can be connected in any way with Moyenne. A small mystery
which must remain unsolved.
The descent to Pirate Cove was well-shaded and much easier to clear, with
the bush mainly white cedars and an occasional takamaka, cashewnut and drag-
on's blood tree, the latter is so called because, on scraping the bark, the sap which
oozes out looks like blood. The trees, and there are many on Moyenne, flower
on one day every year, usually 27 November, and the sight of masses of cascad-
ing laburnum-like flowers is matched only by the heavy scent which drifts across
the water to neighbouring islands. Returning to Moyenne late one afternoon I
commented on a wonderful smell and Rene pointed out the bright yellow flow-
ers which had blossomed during the day and added: 'It's the island dragons.'
Two-thirds of the way down to Pirate Cove we came across a tiny plateau and
two very old graves topped by almost rusted-away iron crosses. Referred to as
pirate graves, they had been mentioned in the deeds when Julie and Melidor
sold the island in 1892 and had been just two piles of rocks until Miss Best had
them dressed with coral cement and the crosses put in place in the early 1900s.
On one of my holiday visits to Moyenne, Madame told me: Monsieur, there
is a treasure on Moyenne! I had laughed and said I knew about the reputed £30
million somewhere on the peak, a story I did not take very seriously as it would
have been a very foolish pirate to have hacked his way to the highest point on
the island, through some of the toughest bush, when there were many other more
accessible and suitable sites for burying his hoard.
Madame told me then that recently on Mahe she had met a six-year-old girl
who had a dream about Moyenne and in it she had seen two graves, a mango
tree at the foot of the graves and a treasure nearby. The story had surprised her
as the girl had not been to the island and there were indeed just two graves there.
On returning home, Madame checked the small cemetery and found a mango
tree where the girl said it would be.
Clearing the cemetery, I had discovered a square of coral concrete only a few
anything was buried underneath.
feet from the graves and so one evening decided to take a spade and find out if
While I admit to being a bit sheepish about the whole affair, I chickened out
completely when, at the precise moment my spade touched the coral concrete,
two large coconuts fell with a thud and rolled close to where I was standing.
Over a number of years I told many visitors about the girl's dream and my
encounter with the coconuts and suggested that if there was a treasure it was
likely to be at the foot of the graves. Someone must have had more faith in the
stories than I because, one weekend, a large hole had been dug at the spot and
filled in again with leaves scattered over to hide the activities. However, my feet

and wondered who, all those years ago, had lit a fire by the graves.

Leaving the cutting of a track down to Pirate Cove for later, we carried n
from the cemetery to the two cottages. The path ran parallel to St Anne's Chan
nel and, in turn, we found stands of white cedar, cashewnut, bois noir (blackwood
after which we named the cove below the cottages.
or woman's tongue because the seedpods rattle), mangoes and finally takamaka.
We discovered many new trees and shrubs, including bigaradier, a cross be
tween an orange and a lime, bois table, the table or looking-glass tree the voung
leaves very shiny and flashing in the sunlight like a mirror, bois cuillere, spon

The path had to pass over a wide and slow-sloping granite face known asa
glacis, from which a district on Mahe takes its name. Obviously, some cement
work would be needed to make this spot safer, especially in wet weather wher
it became very slippery.
The final section from the cottages to the plateau and main landing, about fifty
feet up from the lagoon, was lined with dragon trees and there were large clumps
of oil palms and Chinese guavas.
It was quite a celebration as we arrived at the old picnic house, although both
Rene and I were aware that the path would require widening and constant at-
tention, otherwise it would close in and revert to bush. Also, while a circular
route was essential, it did not solve the problems of reaching the peak and many
other parts of the interior. In fact, it was only in 1994 that we completed the net-
work of paths and wide nature trails.
The task of keeping them clean and clear must be a continuing one for us, and
whoever comes after. At least it helps to keep us reasonably fit and trims the
tummy line.
Homes and gardens
During the path-cutting operations, we had also been busy on the housing front.
In May 1974, nine months after my arrival, a team of carpenters, led by Joseph
Bertie Marie, came to live on Moyenne during the week and completely rebuilt
the picnic house, cutting timber on the island and bringing over other wood and
breadfruit planking for the floors from Cascade on Mahe. The carpenters took
56
six weeks to finish the job and left Rene and me the task of giving the building
its first coats of paint. We moved from the cottage to our new home after three
weeks of painting the corrugated sheeting and asbestos panels white, all the
woodwork dark green and the windows splashes of orange. The original design,
with its wide front verandah, two main rooms and kitchen, had been maintained
but modern style doors and windows introduced and a back verandah enclosed
to be used as a store.
When I say 'we' moved it meant not only Rene and myself but also I Don't
Care and a black hen called Jojo, who not only produced hundreds of chicks
and outlived them all but became a star turn with visitors. Naturally, a cockerel
followed with the apt name of Napoléon. But the remainder of Rene's family
and their noisy menagerie were well left behind.
We used one room as a lounge and the second as a bedroom. The kitchen al-
lowed us to cook for ourselves when we wished and Rene turned out to be a
first-class Creole chef. Indeed, his sauces today are always tasty and never too
spicy, but he does produce a very special mango salad using the unripe fruit,
onions, chillies (all thinly sliced) and dressed with a mixture of oil, vinegar, sugar
and a pinch of salt. It is guaranteed to make perspiration stand out on your fore-
head and the evil-looking juice remaining to be battled over.
In front of the picnic house, which Madame had nicknamed Maison Blanche,
was a small square area of levelled ground. Enlarging and extending it to take a
new house would require cutting into the hillside and moving very large boul-
ders.
This was to be our next job and took three-and-a-half months of hard and hot
work, making greater use of the 14lb hammer and crowbar. Every day saw the
firing of boulders dug from the hillside. It was necessary to collect old coconut
husks, which burn with an intense heat, and palm fronds to start the fires and
we became experts at hearing the 'crack' which told us they were ready for hit-
ting with the hammer.
Cold water thrown on hot rocks will help them crack, but, as we still had to
fetch our supplies from Mahe and Cerf, we could not spare any fresh water for
the exercise and sea water meant a fifty-foot haul from the beach. Incidentally,
we were warned to allow a cooling off period from the sun's heat and the hot
rocks before washing in the sea.
Of course, at this time we were still without toilet facilities, other than a long
drop', and even a shower. After our evening swim we soaped down and poured
a couple of buckets of freshwater over each other. It was all very simple but I do
not recall having been bothered about it in the least. We were busy all day long
and making visible progress on our slowly evolving 'development plan.
Living standards would improve greatly when the new house became a real-
ity and an underground reservoir was built to hold rainwater from its roof. A
generator would provide the power for a pump to feed a tank on the hillside for
gravity drop to taps, showers and toilets. All this seemed a long way ahead,

That was the easy bit. From there on it was uphill — some of it literally. Fora

the view from Maison Blanche resembled a military cemetery for many months.
It was unfortunate that the men who came to dig the holes for the reinforced
pillars did not follow the pegging out by Sunny, Rene and myself. On checking
the work, Rene discovered that the final pillars would not fit on the plateau and
the house would jut out over the drop to the beach, totally blocking the only path
round the island.
The foreman, who had sat on a rock like a guru facing out to sea while the
men worked, suggested steps on both sides of the verandah to connect the path.
He offered no solution to the 'overhang'. I could well imagine a barrowload of
cement or fertilizer providing an unwelcome diversion at either tea or beer time.
Sunny came over and it was agreed that if we provided the rocks — earmarked
for garden walls — and the cement, he would pay for the masons to build a
twenty-four-foot extension wall to take in the house and allow space for the
pathway.
It was an unnecessary hiccup but the delay in the arrival of the materials from
Penang allowed ample time for the wall to be extended.
Again, unfortunately, although warned, the builders did not backfill the area
and with some torrential rainstorms the wall formed a fine dam which broke,
the water taking a giant boulder with it from just below the house to the beach,
which we had named 'I Don't Care' after the dog. Happily, no one was injured
and little damage done.
58
There were a number of silly incidents during the actual building; but one
that was not so funny concerned a young electrician caught cutting a large ob-
long hole in the middle of the verandah wall.
He was spoiling a very nice piece of woodwork and when I asked him, not
too politely, what he was doing the lad replied: It's for the meter!
What meter?' I asked.
The electricity meter, he said. 'Every house has one and it must be on the
front verandah in case you are out when the meter man calls!
More patiently this time, I asked him where he thought the power was com-
ing from and when he looked towards Mahe, I said: 'No! A generator!
But the electrician had the last laugh. While agreeing to take the wires to join
a cable out of sight at the back of the house, he said that all the wiring had been
brought to the verandah point and any change would require complete rewir-
ing. We compromised. He closed the hole on the verandah and cut a similar one
at the same spot but inside the lounge to take the fuse box. Now there would
not be an unsightly red box for all to see and the view from the house was suf-
ficiently eye-catching for the visitor not to notice it inside. But we still have to
live with it to this day.
Excavation of the soil and rocks for the reservoir produced different problems.
It was important to have the reservoir completed in the dry season so that the
cement would set properly and be ready to take the water when the rains came.
This meant an immediate start as it was already May and the dry, salty winds
were blowing.
The builders sent over a team of three diggers who worked well after mark-
ing out a seventeen-foot square directly in front of our present home. However,
soon the earth and rocks started to pile up on all four sides and tumble back into
the hole. Rene went over to ask them where they were going to put it, to which
they replied: Nowhere! We are diggers, not earth movers!
The situation did not resolve itself as we hoped and, eventually, wanting the
reservoir finished on time, I asked the diggers if they would mind if we moved
the debris. 'Oh, no! Go ahead,' was their reply.
So, buying a second wheelbarrow, Rene and I spent the next three and a half
what today is a very good path to the cottages.
weeks loading up and trundling our barrows along past the old house to make
Ilost fifteen and a half pounds during the haul and we were both more than
happy to see the last load away. We were astonished then to discover that the
diggers were the master masons employed to build the reservoir. This time there
were no problems and a fine, covered reservoir, reinforced with steel and ag-
gregate, still functions today, so strong that it also forms the base of one of the
Jolly Roger thatched restaurants.
It seems that they did not mind being diggers, but earth movers was beneath
their dignity. They went on to build and rebuild our retaining and sea walls and
the generator house and block for the machine. All turned out well eventually.

Most people consider the coconut palm part of any tropical island scene as
coconut meat and oil to thatching, coir for (hard) mattresses and sweeping
It was obvious, therefore, that we should plant a new generation of palms on
Moyenne. As with almost anything concerning an island, saying is infinitely
and still attached to the nuts from which roots were sprouting) we had to dig
easier than doing. Before ferrying over 1,000 young palms (about three-foot high
three-foot square and three-foot-deep holes for them, backfilling the earth with
a layer of coconut husks at the bottom.
Digging batches of fifty or 100 holes, where possible on the hilly terrain, we
then collected the young palms by truck from the nursery on Mahe and brought
them over to Moyenne in the Amy Jane. As the trips were often wet, the palms
had to be washed down with freshwater and carried up to the plateau in read-
ness for planting. This was the easiest part, merely requiring the nuts to be
pressed into the soft soil. In the old slavery days the palms were ringed with
large rocks but we did not consider this necessary and the palms today are well
established and starting to bear fruit.
Visitors sometimes ask why coconut palms grow outwards from an island no
matter in which direction they are planted. The answer is a logical one: they seek
the most light and this comes from the sea rather than the land. It all adds to the
beauty of the beach scene, anyway.
Clearing the bush not only allowed us to introduce a wide variety of new
palms and trees, it gave us the added bonus of permitting coconuts to fall and
germinate on the ground. Hundreds of baby palms are popping up all over the
island and it is no longer necessary for the long haul from Mahe.
Over the first twenty years we bought, begged and even stole cuttings which
60
has
Yorkshire-born Brendor
are now beautiful shrubs providing a touch of colour to the previously all green,
if in differing shades, vista that Moyenne presented. Our main concern, how-
- other than the Cocos nuciferus — and trees, especially
ever, had been with palms -
rare Seychelles species. We had our successes and total failures.
Of the 3,500 cedars, more correctly casuarinas, we lost only 500, which was
fair enough as they were planted on top of the island, and in other less hospita-
ble sites subject to the sun and salty winds, where no other vegetation, apart from
the inevitable prune de France, could grow. Once established, the cedars not only
provide cool, shady areas but their acidic needles form a carpet which keeps
many weeds at bay.
Happily, we were not so successful with the 300 eucalyptus trees. Only one
survived, and that could hardly rub against itself and ignite, a dangerous habit
which we only learned of after bringing them over from the nursery and plant-
ing them on the hillside facing Victoria. And, while on failures, the saddest was
the loss of all 250 orange, lime and grapefruit trees, and the funniest was the sole
pineapple produced by 300 suckers for which we had carefully terraced the land.
It was eaten by one of Sasa's pigs before it was ripe enough for harvesting.
Mahogany was a different story. Advised that they would never grow on
Moyenne, I took fifty little trees as they looked so strong and healthy. Only three
died and the next year I collected 150, all of which today are shooting up, some
more than fifty-feet tall and five inches in girth.
Always on the lookout for rare trees, I was pleased to welcome my old friend
Willi André — of Praslin adventures — Lindsay Chong Seng, a leading conser-
vationist and, with his wife Kate, author of some excellent books on Seychelles
trees and plants, and John Hook, a British forestry adviser, when they brought
with them twelve bois de fer, better known as ironwood trees. Before man came
to the islands, Mahe had thousands of these 100ft hardwood giants, which were
cut for building boats and houses and making furniture. The abolition of slav-
ery and the need for a less labour-intensive crop encouraged the establishment
of coconut plantations and sounded the death knell for the ironwoods. Today
only twelve mature trees survive on Mahe and, of these, only one produces seeds
for propagation, which itself is tricky, the young trees having a danger period
of between five and seven years.
So, to have twelve planted on Moyenne was quite an occasion, and we 'wa-
tered' them with a drop of beer for luck, each of us deciding where three trees
should be planted. A few months later, a young park ranger, visiting the island,
noticed the ironwoods which had been specially labelled, and commented: But
they are not ironwoods. They are bois de nattes [a rare tree also]! He was right.
Lindsay came over to investigate and agreed. When young, the two trees are
difficult to distinguish. The story had a happy ending as some ironwoods ar-
rived and we now have two rare varieties rather than one.
As time passed, we managed to purchase eleven germinated nuts of the coco
de mer, or double coconut, which I had first seen in the valley where they origi-

During the period between the completion of the reservoir and the arrival of
what we were to call 'Maison Moyenne, Rene and I decided to cut into the hill.
side at the back of the renovated picnic house, 'Maison Blanché, to make a long
store and cage for Madame's constantly straying hens and chicks. This proved
to be the hardest task so far as it was mainly soft rock and solid terre rouge which
was trundled away to extend the new path to the cottages.
A track, to be widened later, was cut from the plateau to the peak and the
magnificent rocks, including one which we named 'The Crown', were made ac-
cessible after a massive attack on really jungle-like bush. A start was made also
to strike a wide path across the saddle between the two peaks where we planted
the first 1,000 cedars.
We still kept Tuesday as our 'big city' day and, taking advantage of a calm
spell, brought over the 1.5 kV Lister generator, another foolhardy activity buta

keel and, eventually, we made it to the sandspit where it was unloaded with the

Yorkshire-borr
Fishing dogs and parrots
Meanwhile, at the lowest tides, the masons had filled in all the holes in the sea
walls, rebuilt sections where necessary and refaced and retopped the entire wall-
ing. With the breakwater and slipway also rebuilt, we found that some 150 bags
of cement had been ferried over in the Amy Jane. She was proving her worth.
On what we called our home front, we had acquired four pigeons, which rap-
idly multiplied to around forty, two guinea pigs, four white floppy-eared rabbits
and a puppy dog named Fiapi (breadfruit) to remind us of our somewhat haz-
ardous and slightly drunken trip through the reefs from Cascade with the
breadfruit planking. Fiapi was a gift from Bertie, the carpenter.
Atfirst, I Don't Care' was jealous of the little dog but as time went by started
to play with him and eventually they were to produce two litters from which
we kept Jolly Roger and 'I Don't Care, Too. Fiapi gained a well-earned reputa-
tion as a fishing dog, managing to chase them into shallow water, holding them
firmly down with his front legs before putting his head underwater and bring-
ing up fine parrot fish to eat raw on the sandspit. Then he would come and lick
us with a fishy tongue to show what a clever dog he was.
With I Don't Care, Too', whom he taught to fish — being totally unsuccess-
ful with the daft but always happy, Jolly Roger - Fiapi caught an octopus. I was
returning along the sandspit after seeing off some friends when I noticed the two
dogs splashing about in three feet of water. As I went over a large ink stain ap-
peared and I realized they were entangled with an octopus... eight tentacles
and eight dogs' legs. Joining the mélée, I managed to let the octopus put a tenta-
cle around my arm and all four of us rolled on to the sandspit. It was a
four-and-a-half foot specimen which Madame cleaned, cooked and served as a
curry to some visitors who refused to believe me when I told them they were
eating an octopus that had been caught by my dogs. But then, Moyenne stories
often seem far-fetched even when I know them to be completely true.
Fiapi appeared in most newspaper articles and films about Moyenne, nota-
bly one made by Omar Sharif called Return to Eden. But he was not so popular
on our fishing trips, always swimming out to the anchored boat, even following
it as far as Cerf Island, and then having to be hauled on board amid fishing lines,
hooks and bait. He was almost banned from joining us the day we took the boat
to the other side of Beacon Island (or ile Seche, Dry Island). When we looked for
the cut bait to start fishing it had gone and Fiapi was licking his lips. By good
chance, one piece had fallen under a bench and Rene, acknowledged to be our
best fisherman, caught a nice-sized fish with it and so we had enough bait to
haul in a catch to satisfy the needs of our small black cat, Gris Gris — Creole for
black magic — and ourselves. I recall Fiapi was forgiven and presented with a
fish to munch on our way back to the island.
Not so welcome 'pets' were the five-inch long, mahogany-coloured centipedes,
two of which managed to give me very painful bites. I encountered the first at
