Miss Best, the last owner to live on Moyenne, had stipulated in her wil that
nothing was to be killed on the island, particularly the small bushrats. Wal 1
washappy to respect her wishes but the bushrats on the island proved to be as
tonishingly intelligent. I had been told that to move an egg one would lie on is
back with the egg held firmly between four paws while the mate would pull it
and the egg away by its tail. They had other tricks-
— for example, eating the top
off a bottle of oil, they would dip in their tails and lick off the oil, repeating the
process until they had taken enough. Their sharp teeth were not only capable of
cutting through the husk and shell of a coconut to get at the white meat and juice
but also of eating through wooden cupboard doors to have a party with their
friends, demolishing bread, cakes, sugar, flour — in fact, anything edible. We
discovered their capabilities very early on and today still battle against their cun-
ning efforts that are often amusing. Certainly I have a conscience when,
occasionally, we have to have a short blitz' and trap and drown a few. I remem-
ber Miss Best and realize that, with the lizards, skinks and spiders, the bushrats
have been on the island as long as man, if not longer.
Obviously well established before the 1900s and Miss Best's era, it is unlikely
the bushrats came ashore in the small boat of Julie and Melidor, but more prob-
ably from a ship anchored off the island, perhaps as a bonus' left behind with
the treasure by pirates in the 1700s.
I discovered that far more dangerous creatures lurked in the shallow waters
around Moyenne which could prove painful and put you out of action for a few
days. I am not referring to any swiftly-moving sea creatures but the more mun-
dane sea urchins and razor or pen shells. The latter is like a flattened ice-cream
cornet, buried up to a curved and extremely sharp edge which lies just above
the surface of the sand. I saw a visitor jump from a speed boat, slice open his
instep and have to return to Mahe immediately for stitches and a dose of anti-
biotics.
The sea urchin presents the unwary with a foot or handful of sharp black nee-
dles which, being chalk and barbed, cannot be dug out without causing a worse
wound, one which could easily turn septic. The local remedy — putting fire
against the spot where the needles have entered -- should be resisted, as I did
when Sasa, with a flaming newspaper, was about to give me second-degree
burns, a cure far worse than the complaint. Glowing coconut husks were another
64
YOu
Spentmore that
Indian Ocean Islands, Tau
avourite with small boys on some of the popular beaches on Mahe, with equally
disastrous results. The best 'cure' is to tap off the spines at skin level and leave
well alone. Within three days most of the black dots will have disappeared, dis-
solved into the body.
Perhaps the application of a cottonwool pad soaked in concentrated vinegar
— acetic acid, in fact — held to the injury for one and a half hours will help to
soften the area and speed up the vanishing act. But, from experience, a longer
period will produce acid burns and a foot with a variety of colours over the week
it takes to heal. An American marine biologist, Tom Ebert, who stayed on
Moyenne for five weeks tagging sea urchins and measuring red starfish, had the
misfortune to step on an urchin on his first day on the island. A double dose of
the vinegar treatment gave him the chance to sit on our verandah for six days
and bring all his files and reports up to date.
My first anniversary on Moyenne saw me settled into a totally rebuilt picnic
house, with a completed path around the island, the hillside levelled and await-
ing the new house, and an 11,000-gallon covered underground water reservoir,
a feeder tank and a long store constructed, a generator installed, sea and retain-
ing walls rebuilt, a considerable area of bush cleared and something like 2,500
palms and trees planted.
It was a formidable list of achievements for Robinson Crusoe and Man Fri-
day, with a little help from their friends. I had learned of the dangers and wonders
ofliving on an increasingly beautiful tropical island. I had a long way to go but
I felt that I was becoming a genuine islander.
Eventually my prefabricated house arrived on board a ship called the Tropic
Queen with a full cargo of similar wooden houses, schools and offices. It was 10
September but it was not until a month later that the pontoon was sighted off
Moyenne bringing over all the planks, beams, corrugated asbestos roofing,
maisonite, pipes, bathroom fittings and, of course, the kitchen sink.
The word prefabricated had conjured up visions of whole sections of the house
arriving ready-made. But this was not so, all the pieces being numbered and
notched for easy assembly by a team of seven Malaysian Chinese who, starting
work two days later — after the materials had been carried up from the beach
—and putting in a twelve-hour day (7 am to 7 pm), completed their task in little
more than a week. It was astonishing to look across to the house site and see a
whole wall of tongued-and-grooved planks nailed in place within half an hour.
The verandah rails, window frames, doors and fitted cupboards did come com-
plete, having been made on Mahe.
Unfortunately, the speed of assembly was not matched by the plumbing and
the fulfilment of other contractual obligations. Many times I had to refuse to ac-
cept the keys and thus make the third and final payment. As I explained to Sunny,
I could hardly accept a shiny new car with all the fittings but without an engine
to make it go. I wanted to see water flowing and taps, showers and toilets work-

pute was not settled until almost four months later, on 14 March, 1975, fourteen
In the meantime, electricians had connected the generator to the recently-wited
old house as well as the new one. The lights went on at Maison Blanche' five days
before Maison Moyenne' and both occasions were something of non-events for
me personally. The kerosene lamps, like a log fire, had provided a friendly focal
point in the evenings for our games or 'cosies', as family get-togethers are called.
The electric lights were totally impersonal and shed their light everywhere. But
we were well rid of the smoky oil lamps and kerosene smell. Incidentally, the
generator house was sited so far away that the thud-thud of the engine could
not be heard in either house. Bruch, Mendelssohn, Neil Diamond and the Beatles
had no noisy rival, only the sound of the sea and the wind rustling in the palms.
One funny incident always comes to mind when I pass the small concrete
boxes at the foot of each fallpipe, providing filters for the rainwater on its way
into the reservoir.
The Malaysian workers had gathered around a box and one was holding a
four-gallon can of water. Rene and I went over to see what was happening and
stayed to witness an historic occasion for Moyenne-
— the first water to be poured
into the reservoir.
With seven faces close to the box listening for the echo, the water was poured.
But it never arrived. In laying the connecting pipe, someone had been too lazy
to remove a large rock and the small hill it made was too great an incline for the
water to pass. Instead it flowed back with a rush and final splash which caught
the team I had dubbed earlier as our Magnificent Seven' totally unawares. As
they started to shout, Rene and I made a hasty retreat to the back of the old house
where we could laugh without causing any embarrassment. Recalling those sur-
prised and very wet faces we have chuckled many times since. The 'Seven'
deserved their nickname. They were a fast, hard-working crew.
I was pleased some years later to find Sunny Khan as a fellow passenger on a
flight to London. Although we had battled', I liked the man who lived up to his
first name. Sunny also had the privilege of taking the first and best photograph
of the house he built, Maison Moyenne.
Tourist trove
With the departure of the builders and masons, it was time to clean up the area
and establish the beginnings of a garden between and around the two houses. We
camouflaged the septic tank and manhole covers, which were an eyesore, with
plastic sheeting and then earth and small shrubs. More rocks had to be broken
with the 14lb hammer to edge the flower beds and a line of palms and trees along
the path to the cottages. The hillside beyond the houses, facing Long Island,

The new house, with its superb view across the bay, taking in Round Island
and the mountains of Mahe, was perfect during the north-west monsoon but
windy and cold enough for slacks and a pullover when the south-easterly was
blowing. A windbreak of cedars was planted but, unfortunately, it was too high
up the hillside and a second copse of trees had to be established. Today both act
as good shields for the house.
Bright yellow allamanda and white ixora shrubs were brought over from Coral
Cove, where they were spreading rapidly, and nurseries on Mahe and the gar-
dens of friends supplied plants and cuttings of a wide variety of species, with
the emphasis on hibiscus, bougainvillea and frangipani, which we knew were
hardy enough to survive in salty and windy conditions.
The collection of palms, trees and shrubs continues to grow. More than 9,000
have been introduced all over the island, especially along the new nature trails.
With the arrival of an adequate freshwater supply we started also a new task:
watering the garden.
We had found that building a house and facilities to make it habitable was
considerably more difficult than in more 'civilized' surroundings, where the con-
nection to power, water, sewage and telephone are comparatively simple. But
once completed we were happy to have all mod cons in our insular way of life.
New furnishings, which had been brought over in the Amy Jane, included cane
chairs and tables for the verandah, which was obviously going to be the most
popular 'room'in the new house. With the paintings, Zanzibar chests and other
items from my East African days, it would quickly become a home — or so I
thought.
At weekends, Moyenne, with its long sandspit, provided a useful picnic spot
for local residents and also during the week for tourists hiring small boats for a
day's trip to what had become the St Anne Marine National Park. Being 'domaine
public, the Seychelles beaches are open to all. But above the high water mark
the island is private and permission is needed to step ashore.
Unfortunately, high tide inevitably meant that picnickers and tourists alike
landed on Moyenne and, once ashore, their curiosity resulted in exploration. Al-
though they were trespassers in law we discovered the most effective way of
persuading them to leave was to request a £1 landing fee. Not a single visitor of
all those who disturbed our work - and on occasion we found some inside the
houses — ever paid up, although such a charge for having an island to them-
selves was hardly excessive. Indeed, at the time it was no more than the price of
a couple of beers.
Only two glass-bottom boats were operating in the park, a converted landing
craft, taking twenty-seven passengers, named Sonja, after the head of the tour
company that owned her, Sonja Lindblad, and a 12-seater 'mini' Babette.
They had one port of call, the Beyond the Reef' bar and restaurant, which

Perhaps fate decided to take a hand again for three chance meetings within

coming several thousand visitors to the island over the years.
In Dar es Salaam I had stayed at the New Africa Hotel and it was a happy
coincidence that the former manager, John Wright, and his wife, Terry, were
given the tough job of opening what was to be the largest modern hotel in fe
islands, originally called the Mahe Beach. When they spent a day on Moyenne
they both said it was ideally placed for a bar and restaurant and the house would
'sell' easily as a tropical island hideaway.
Two days later, Len Rene, who operated Leon's Tours' in Victoria, asked me
if I would allow his clients to land on Moyenne for which privilege he would
pay me. And, finally, at a Rotary Club meeting, the Director of Tourism, Colin
Walsh, said he wanted to see Moyenne and encouraged me take a plunge into
tourism. He was right when he added that it would be the key industry of the
islands in the future.
But that 'plunge' turned out to be a very slow dive. In spite of the tourism
director telling me to go ahead after he visited 'Maison Moyenne', it took five
months for me to overcome a series of obstacles and, occasionally, what is rightly
termed in Seychelles as 'faire malice'. We received our licence to operate the day
after the Liquor Licensing Board had met. This would have meant a further two-
month delay had not a magistrate convened a special hearing with two colleagues
and approved a full licence without any limitations whatsoever.
So on 29 July, two days after my fiftieth birthday, we were legal and ready
for visitors. I think it fair to say that neither Rene nor I had the slightest idea of
what we were starting or what we needed to run a bar and restaurant, let alone
a guesthouse, albeit a small one. Madame had surprised me when I asked if she

It seemed that we were all game to give it a go, although I had some serious
doubts about the enterprise. In fact, Madame was to cook for up to ninety cli-

— with the arrival of more babies — Rene ran the kitchen with a young helper,
Jean Claude. Eventually we granted exclusive rights to the island facilities to a
tour operator who brought in their own staff. My job throughout had been to
run the Jolly Roger Bar and make the visitors welcome.
The business evolved and carried us along with the increasing wave of tour-
ists visiting the island. We made an arrangement with the breweries for beer and
soft drinks, bought a variety of glasses by the carton, provided tables and chairs
and built extra toilet facilities next to the bar. The latter project, including a large
septic tank and soakaway, was completed by Rene and myself in three weeks,
working from dawn until after dark by kerosene lamps.
Some old wine casks were halved for more tables and we managed success-
fully to create a 'piratical' atmosphere for the Jolly Roger.
As more tourists found their way, via the glass-bottom boats, to Moyenne we
got Bertie to build an attractive thatched restaurant on top of the otherwise rather
ugly, grey cement reservoir. Elsewhere in the garden we had tables with para-
sols and small benches, not so good on wet and windy days. A second thatched
restaurant, built by Bertie, overlooking the sea, allowed us to cope daily with
up to eighty visitors at a time.

We worked long hours and learned the tricks of the trade the hard way but

The guesthouse produced some unusual clients but the majority returned ye

the Pyalls from Essex, the Burckschats from Berlin, and the Plewmans from Jo.

The Queen Elizabeth II was a regular visitor on her round-the-world voyages
(missing out during the Falklands War and refitting but bringing the British com-
mander, Brigadier Tony Wilson, and his wife, Janet, as guests on the next trip)
We serviced the Canberra, the largest cruise ship to berth alongside the new port,
and many more in the succeeding years.
At first, I led a long snake of visitors each day on a conducted tour of Moyenne,
following the bright orange 'sun splashes' with which we had marked the main
path. But those at the tail end could not hear my commentary on the islands
history and vegetation and those at the front wanted to move along faster. To
solve the problem we started to label all the palms, trees and shrubs many times
around the island, and place explanatory signs on such spots as the old cem-
etery and Miss Best's House of Dogs.
Visitors were then able to walk at their own speed and discover the island for
themselves, while I was free to run the bar and play 'mine host.
As the years progressed so did we, providing better facilities, illustrated bro-
chures giving the island's history in seven languages — English, French, German,
Italian, Spanish, Danish and Creole — and a map showing the network of na-
ture trails, the main path and points of interest.
Coming from cooler climates, most visitors tended not to believe us when we
told them it was too cold for tropical softies like ourselves to swim when the
south-east monsoon was really blowing from August to October, accepting the
fact only when they saw the goose pimples on our arms.
Of course, no one likes the patches of cloudy and wet weather, often around
Christmas, possibly the 'edge of some cyclone. Happily for Seychelles, cyclones
heading in our direction usually change course about 400 miles away and move
south, which is not so good for our neighbour, Mauritius. The only recorded cy-
70
chelle:
done in Seychelles was way back in the 1800s but it had nothing to do with the
shooting of the last island crocodile in 1830, as some jokers would have us be-
lieve. Incidentally, when the island of Krakatoa blew up in the Pacific the tidal
wave it generated passed through the Seychelles islands, doing considerable
damage to shoreline property and ebbing and flowing against the African coast
seven times before subsiding.
The year of the cardinals
Today tourists produce their video cameras when we feed the birds: the red
(male) and brown (female) Madagascar fodys, more popularly known as 'car-
dinals, the endemic turtle doves with their attractive reddish plumage, the small
ground-barred or peaceful doves and the inevitable but friendly and noisy
Indian mynah birds. It astonishes them when told that, only twenty years be-
fore, Moyenne was waterless and so inhospitable that there were no land birds
at all apart from the odd kestrel who came to hover and plunge for the baby
chicks. Of course, all the usual sea birds were still around, including the green-
backed herons which continued to play a suicidal game of 'tag' with whatever
dogs happened to be on the island sandspit.
Early in 1975, Rene and I caught a dozen 'cardinals' on Cerf and released them
on Moyenne. They promptly flew back home. We returned, caught some more,
fed them on cake, biscuits, bread, sugar, milk and rice for a week and then freed
them. They also returned to Cerf.
We decided that it was not to be the 'Year of the Cardinals' for us after all.
And then, a couple of weeks later, Rene heard their distinctive 'cheeps' and we
spread out rice and bread and waited. Ten settled, told their friends and now
we have more than 500 of the bright little birds as well as some 300 turtle-doves
(from an initial three, 100 ground-doves, the odd colibri and even a couple of
owls.
About the same time, we started to establish a colony of giant tortoises. The
first to arrive was later named Derek, after my godson, and today, at sixty-three,
he remains the oldest, biggest and friendliest of his 'family of forty freely roam-
ing Moyenne. The only place from which they are barred (literally) is the
restaurant area, at the request of the caterers. It's a pity because Derek was the
only giant tortoise in the world to share lunch with visitors, who whipped out
very politely.
their cameras and videos as soon as he appeared to seek bananas and shake hands
All the tortoises have names and move surprisingly quickly, covering the
length of the island in a day. We tell visitors not to be alarmed if, on their walks,
they see a rock move. It will have nothing to do with having had one too many
the night before, just one of our giants.
Most visitors believe that tortoises live to a great age, which is understand-
able as writers these days do not seem interested in a tortoise unless it is said to

Not so popular with us were the giant African land snails which found the
young and tender shoots of our new plants and shrubs much to their liking. We
collected 1,600 and disposed of them in the sea — where else? — in one week
alone. Today the same type of snails are being served in restaurants on Mahe
and Praslin with garlic butter or Burgundy sauce.
At night we were often bombarded with mangoes dropped on to the cort-
gated-iron sheeting by fruit bats or 'flying foxes. The sheets had been used to
re-roof the old house, later 'Maison Blanche', and now the Jolly Roger, where Rene
and Ihad our 'den' when guests were staying in the new house, 'Maison Moyenne.
The bats continue to fly in each evening from Mahe and land directly on trees of
their choice. Unfortunately, they start eating the fruit before it is ready for pick-
ing and they take about eighty per cent of our crop. Without guns we cannot
shoot them and the trees are too big for nets. Served as a curry fruit bats are re-
garded as a delicacy, but I was put off eating them when I discovered their
anatomy was exactly like that of little people.
Once, a friend gave me a fruit bat complete with a cage to keep captive. I can-
not stand seeing an animal imprisoned so I left the cage door open to encourage
it to fly. I was delighted when, after a few weeks, it left the cage, joined the other
fruit bats, began eating the mango fruit — and finally flew away with its fellow
creatures.
Clearing the bush and cutting paths continued at weekends and in our spare
time. With so many tourists, and so much island maintenance and development,
our Saturday jaunts to Mahe for football matches and the cinema came to an end.
During this busy period, however, Rene found time to start a formal court-
ship with Florita Charles, a girl who had visited the island. Within months they
were engaged and then married in the Cathedral of the Immaculate Conception
in Victoria on 1 May, 1976. In true Seychellois tradition it was a grand affair with
the bride in white and a reception at the Coco Rouge at Anse Francois on Mahe
with cake, band and champagne for a large number of guests.

In the proper course of time, a daughter, Gina Renette, was born and three
[??  a Twas given middle name and that  ??]
*his father and I was happy to become the godfather. There were no more chil-
dren and, eventually, Rene and Florita established a home at English River on
the outskirts of Victoria, the daily commuting from Moyenne having proved too
much for the mother, who had continued her teaching career. Now Rene settled
into a pattern of arriving on Monday morning and staying with me until Satur-
day afternoon when he crossed the bay to rejoin his family for the weekend. It
proved a good arrangement, allowing Rene to enjoy some family life and me to
have total privacy on Moyenne, alone but never lonely.
As Thave said to hundreds of visitors, on such a beautiful island, in the com-
pany of the domestic and wild creatures and a very normal belief in God, a man
who is lonely can have little imagination. Incidentally, it really is taboo to visit
Moyenne on Sunday. My friends never come and, indeed, they would no longer
be friends if they did.
Towards the end of 1975 we began a continuing and friendly relationship with
a firm of chartered accountants, B D O Pool, Bruce and Patel. Michael Angas,
the principal partner at the time, advised me over coffee in his office at La Rosière,
Mahe, that I should become a company. We were welcoming a wide range of
visitors to Moyenne, including film stars. He warned me that should they trip
and break an ankle on their way to the Jolly Roger for a drink they could sue for
heavy damages and I might find myself forced to sell Moyenne to pay them.
He suggested that I should be the major shareholder in a company which
would lease the island from me. In the case of any claims, the company would
have limited liability. The company might go bankrupt but the island would be
safe. I saw the sense in this and, with Man Friday' Rene as the other director,
we became Maison Moyenne (Pty) Ltd on 4 November. Two weeks later I leased
Moyenne to the company at a nominal rent. Thus I landed myself with another
job — the preparation of accounts for the annual audit. Of course, I was already
keeping books and sending out statements to various tour operators for serv-
ices rendered in the way of landing fees and luncheons. Usually, visitors paid
for drinks at the bar.
The advent of tourism also saw the beginning of a colossal mailbag with hun-
dreds of letters to answer from past and future visitors. Correspondence was,
perhaps, my biggest and least welcome chore but I believe that if someone takes
the time to write to us then it is only correct that there should be a reply. I con-
tinue to answer a score of letters weekly while our Christmas card list tops the
300 mark. Moyenne has made some thousands of friends all over the world who
now send their married children to Seychelles with messages for Friday',
Robinson and even Derek Tortoise. They do not forget what they describe so
I grow older, gives my life even greater purpose.
often as ' the best day of our holiday'. It makes all the effort worthwhile and, as

pompously, who they were. I then kept clear of them for the rest of their stay on
Lesser nobilities and low court judges occasionally informed me, sometimes
tally unnoticed on the island whereas when Julio Inglesias was spotted by a fan
the island. Julie Covington, the 'Don't Cry for me, Argentina' singer, went to.
I managed to keep his identity secret from about fifty other guests.
Perhaps the saddest meeting was with a man who asked for a vodka and tonic
when we were serving only beers and soft drinks to half-day clients. He appeared
to be in his eighties and 1 relented and gave him a double. I was glad that i did,
for later on the beach he asked if the name Lupino Lane meant anything to me,
When I replied: 'Oh, yes. He was great, all those laughs in the London stage show
Lambeth Walk at the time of King George VI Coronation, the star turn as he bat-
tled so successfully with his robes and coronet..!
'It's me, it's me, ' he cried, his face lit up. I had made him a happy man. Obvi-
ously, he dearly wished to be remembered and I told him that if I'd known who
he was, I would have given him a full bottle of vodka.
Our guest books bear witness to the good times that have resulted from open-
ing the island to the public. We helped others, and the island, at the same time.
Poachers and the park
While we were busy developing Moyenne, things were developing also on Mahe.
The political scene was hotting up as Seychelles became an autonomous colony.
James Mancham became Prime Minister, with a mixed cabinet of ministers from
his own Democratic Party and the Seychelles People's United Party, led by Albert
Rene, who was Minister for Lands.
More hotels were being built on Mahe and Praslin and blocks of offices were
74
on his trop
chelles
going up on reclaimed land in Victoria. Prices, too, were rising and traffic prob-
ems increasing. But the Rotary Club was thriving with the Commissioner of
Police, Tom Booth, and a magistrate, Clive Sutton, to keep fellow members in
The new port, which became operational in 1973, was handling a growing
number of cargo vessels as well as cruise ships, although the airport continued
as the main entry for tourists. Their numbers rose steadily to a peak of almost
79,000 in 1979.
But as they increased, so did the demand for souvenirs, and the quest for tor-
toiseshell, coral and reef shells raised poaching to dangerous levels. Fishing in
the marine park was also taking a heavy toil, particularly on the gentle ray and
fascinating octopus. It was decided that a warden was needed and that he re-
quired a home. So, on neighbouring Round Island, the old house which had been
lit by means of a windmill and batteries was demolished and a well-thatched
wooden structure took its place. Unfortunately, whoever designed what was to
be the park warden's house let the toilet and bathroom with their small high win-
dows face the sea and the glorious view of Moyenne and St Anne. There was no
verandah which would have provided a fine vantage point to observe the inner
park and poaching fraternity.
An American Peace Corps volunteer, Kurt Buchanan, came to live in the house
and soon brought his Japanese American fiancée, Janice, to Seychelles. After they
married, they spent their first two years together on Round Island. Although I
did not know it until just before they left, my dog Fiapi was a regular visitor to
pie or chocolate cake.
their home, especially on Saturdays when he swam over for a slice of pumpkin
tion has doubled since I first came to the islands it would still be unwise to
comment unfavourably about anyone at a party. Likely as not, it will turn out to
be a cousin or close relative of the person to whom you are speaking.
One of Kurt's first tasks was to place buoys to mark the marine park bound-
ary. He did this successfully enough but the buoys gradually disappeared. After
all, without markers, who was to say where the park began or ended? — a use-
ful situation for fishermen-cum-poachers.
In spite of such early setbacks, the park slowly became a reality. Although
not much money was available, a boat with rangers started to patrol the waters
around the inner islands.
Unfortunately, they were not very effective for the fishermen and poachers
were aggressive and armed with spears — for catching rays — pangas, and sharp
knives, whereas the rangers, perforce, were totally unarmed.
The fishermen also had the edge with a thorough knowledge of the reefs, rocks

If it was dangerous to try to apprehend the poachers by day then obviouslyi
was an impossible task at night. In fact, over the years, the same poaching tich
water, glide in quietly to harpoon rays. Sadly, the number of rays has decre
ermen have continued to raid the marine park and, especially after dark at low
dramatically through the activities of these pirates, who are robbing Seychell
of part of its natural heritage.
The octopus is another example. There were always plenty to be found lurk.
ing in the long lagoon between Moyenne and the reef facing St Anne. On on
day alone, a boy, with two girls sitting as 'camouflage' in a small boat, prised no
fewer than twenty-seven octopuses from their holes in the area. He boasted
his exploits over drinks on Mahe later — drinks paid for by the sale of his illegal
haul. Since then it has been difficult to find one octopus to show a visitor.
After Kurt and Janice left Round Island, no warden was ever appointed and
the house, with the assistance of the American Government, became an into.
mation centre for the park but really available only to visitors to Round Island
On many occasions, I tried to reason with the poachers, who ignored my po-
tests as they speared rays during the day in waters by our main landing and the
the long tails on to the sandspit as a gesture of contempt.
One day, after swimming with a large spotted ray for some ten minutes, l
looked up to see a boat coming directly towards me with a man standing in front,
his spear held aloft.
I pleaded with him to stop. But to no avail. The spear hit its mark and, drip-
ping blood, the ray was landed in the boat later to be sold on Mahe and, most
probably, the proceeds drunk away in some bar.
There are few shells to be seen in the park and many fine coral heads have
been broken up for sale by curio dealers on Mahe or smashed by boat anchors.
My suggestions for concrete anchorages with mooring chains for boats have been
ignored by park authorities and the rangers appeared in uniform only after com-
plaints that they had little authority when wearing pop group T-shirts and tom
shorts.
Now, with further American funds, research and training facilities are being
set up on St Anne. But a true love of the marine creatures which they must guard
is equally important if a motivated force for effective conservation is to be es-
tablished.
Turtles, of course, are an even sadder story. When first I came to Moyenne
there were regular sightings of mother turtles coming ashore to lay their egs
during September to December. In two layings, about three weeks apart, one
turtle produces an average of 300 eggs. Compared with a land tortoise's four to
India
on his tropicar
chelles
sixteen eggs, the number is high. But with crabs, birds and other sea creatures,
Induding turtles, gobbling up what in the early stages are soft and vulnerable
baby turtles the mortality rate is high.
Due to poaching, the numbers had already started to fall when I helped Jeanne
Mortimer, a professor of marine biology from the University of Florida, with her
in depth study of the turtles of Seychelle. My role was small but in the 1982-83
season I tagged and kept an accurate record of all hawksbill turtles which came
ashore on Moyenne to lay their eggs. The green turtles, of steak and soup fame,
do not visit Moyenne, although they feed on the marine grasses near Round Is-
land.
The figures showed twelve sightings and of these we knew that at least five
were poached. The situation was worse elsewhere. On South-East Island, where
twenty to forty had landed, most had been poached and of the fifty-five to sev-
enty turtles on St Anne the number poached was at least twenty.
It is all a grim testament to the poachers efficiency and a sad indictment of
the callous ease with which they plied their evil trade. In 1994 only four turtle
tracks were observed on Moyenne, while at night the small boats continued to
seek the few remaining rays.
In her report, Jeanne Mortimer, whom I dubbed 'Turtle Lady', warned: The
most complete protection for the hawksbill would be afforded by a total ban on
the possession, purchase and sale of all hawksbill products. When Seychelles
signed the Cites Treaty in 1977 she essentially agreed to impose such a ban. Ex-
port of raw hawksbill shell is in direct contravention of the Cites agreement. Nor
is Seychelles honouring her obligations to Cites when she condones the sale of
tortoiseshell curios to tourists! Such a ban was imposed eventually at the be-
ginning of 1995.
Nature is producing some new corals but many have died and the numbers
of fish I now see when I go snorkelling nowhere approach the masses which de-
lighted my eye when first I settled on Moyenne. The banning of fishtraps in the
will hardly make much difference.
park, resulting in the islanders having to buy their fish at the markets on Mahe,
When St Anne Park was under consideration I was given a draft copy of the
act for comment. I remember reading one paragraph that said that no animal or
plant life, dead or alive, was to be disturbed or killed. It has a bitter irony today.
Stepping ashore
On Moyenne, we settled into a new pattern for the days when tourists arrived
in the glass-bottom boats. I met them on the beach, welcomed them in a mix of
languages and told them a little about the island. Then it was up to the plateau
tora drink and either snorkelling along the reef or walking along the main path
and increasing number of nature trails. The most energetic clambered with their
cameras up to the peak for the breathtaking panoramic views. Lunch was a happy

cup of tea Sir Colin remembered until his death early in 1993.
Among the many boats and yachts which put in to Moyenne none was more
welcome in the early days than one we nicknamed 'Bateau Rouge' because ofher
red paintwork. The two 'captains', Di and John Tigwell, came from the west of
England and the yacht was their home. Whenever they dropped anchor, usu-
ally in the channel between Round Island and ourselves, we either went on board
for curry and coffee or they came ashore for beers and snacks.
There was one occasion when they invited me for a sail around Beacon Is-
land so that I could look at the back of Moyenne from the sea. I took my, as yet
unused, fishing rod and, while still close to the island, caught a small bonito.
Ten minutes later, with the sea getting rougher, I hauled in a nice-sized barra-
cuda.
And then, I was seasick. It was a quick bout but in rushing to the side I pura
78
foot in the open mouth of the barracuda and received five bright red puncture
marks on the big toe.
We decided to stick to the calm anchorage and Moyenne in the future. But I
did get my first glimpse of the island from a different angle, the rollers crashing
on the rocky point. I also saw an area I had yet to explore because of the thick
bush which blocked the way.
I receive postcards from John and Di as they continue their leisurely sail
through life, now in Antigua in Bateau Noir Sabai, the successor to our old friend
'Bateau Rouse'.
Another, smaller yacht, the Diana, always welcome on the Moyenne horizon,
was skippered by Frank Wood and his wife Di. Frank was a magistrate, and later
high courtjudge, and easily recognizable through my binoculars wearing a bright
orange peaked cap which stood out against the white sails.
It was expected that I join them for lunch — usually a curry and a home-made
pudding — and I took down a bottle of wine and three glasses in a box so that
prying eyes would not know of our tippling. But, as Frank said on one occasion,
You know, you can be drunk in charge of a car, even a bicycle, but there is no
such law concerning boats.'
They stayed about four hours on a Sunday before returning early as Frank,
who was Chancellor of St Paul's Anglican Cathedral on Mahe, liked to attend
evensong.
Mention of St Paul's reminds me of the day I met the Bishop and Archdeacon
in Victoria. Hoping to please them, I said: 'Oh, I've just been to pray in your small
he means our cathedral.'

The third vessel, remembered well, was slightly larger than the first two —

It was no surprise, therefore, when a jollyboat nosed on to our sandspit and a

A new flag
My constant companion since I arrived, 'I Don't Care', had her second litter of
puppies in 1976 but died from a heart attack while nursing them. We buried her
in the pet's cemetery and kept one of her youngsters, naming him 'I Don't Care,
Too' or 'Too' for short. With Jolly Roger from the first litter, and Fiapi, I now
had three shadows wherever I went.
Madame made many batches of bacca from fermented fruits and sugar cane.
One put me out of action for a few hours not because I drank too much but be-
cause she had mistakenly used salt instead of sugar and I took a fairly good swig
when offered a 'tasting'. Rene was wiser — he sipped and took no more.

The only time I knew him to be aggressive was when a tourist thrust his flipper
at him three or four times. The man took off sharply when George emerged,
baring a mouth full of very sharp teeth.
White ants made their reappearance three times but were seen before they
could do any damage and we started to use Rentokil on all buildings twice a
year. No more centipedes were reported and the large black-and-yellow spiders
increased. But with their webs catching unwanted flies and mosquitoes they were
welcome.
Just as we developed our island life, so had the political climate of the main-
land progressed. Now, everyone was talking about independence. The date was
set: 29 June, 1976.
From Moyenne, we watched the jumbo jet carrying the Duke and Duchess of
Gloucester and the Royal Marine Band fly over Victoria to land at the interna-
tonal airport at Pointe La Rue, built over corals and low-lying land rather similar
to Kai Tak in Hong Kong. The handing-over ceremony took place in the capi-
ta's sports stadium. I insisted that Rene go as it was an historic event for the
islands. Just before midnight the duke shook hands with the country's first Presi-
dent, James R. Mancham, and its first Prime Minister, France Albert Rene, and
the Union Jack was lowered and the Seychelles flag - two red and two blue
triangles set in a white St Andrew's Cross — was raised.
To mark the occasion, I had erected a flagpole on reclaimed land on Moyenne,
now known as Flagstaff Point. As the commentator described the flag ceremony
on my small radio, I slowly raised the new flag on Moyenne, maybe reaching
the top a second or two ahead of the one on Mahe. I sipped a large Drambuie,
smoked a cigar and wished our new republic and all Seychellois well.
Unfortunately, while Rene and his family, like the majority of the people in
the islands, became Seychellois, I remained British and was thus a foreigner on
my own island. It would require an official change of citizenship to resolve the
problem.
We had been promised a fireworks display and I sat on the seawall and
watched the rockets and exploding stars that briefly lit up the night sky. Disap-
pointed, I returned to the plateau. But just as I was entering the house, the
fireworks really started and it was a fine show. Afterwards I heard two expla-

Enter the gris gris
Madame, who had not been too well, went into hospital. When she came out,
she stayed on Mahe for her convalescence. So Rene and I took the opportunity
to redecorate her cottages inside and out and to surprise her by laying an elec-
tric cable from the generator giving her more comfort on her return.
We had to work fast as the tourists also had to be looked after, but by bring-
ing over an electrician to do the actual wiring of the cottage we were ready in
time.
Unfortunately, water still had to carried from the main reservoir daily, a chore
shared by all the family.
Some of the paths which we had cut were already in need of attention, espe-
cially after heavy rains and windy weather which brought down cedars as well
as coconut palms and any other trees which were in the way. Saws and axes were
always 'at the ready for such emergencies. New path cutting in the interior of
the island was yielding previously unknown trees, such as santol, bois sandal, bois
sagnie, boi calon, and gayac, another fairly rare species. The Amy Jane often returned
to Moyenne resembling a floating garden, with young palms and trees and
shrubs, usually obtained from government nurseries with the continued help
of our old friend Willi André. One of the most difficult cargoes had been the dry
leaves of the Feuille latanier for thatching. Although tied in bundles of fifty, they
were easily caught and ripped and blown out of the boat by the strong winds
means of ensuring safe transportation.

containers were required until, finally, she was living in a 100-gallon tank. Ser-

tecs — like cockles and the base for a good soup — and raw fish was astonish-
ing. She was well named.
Thad the choice of setting her free in the sea or giving her to a large aquarium
at the Northolme Hotel on Mahe. I decided on the latter as I was afraid Ti Vorace
would go up to any boat and her life would be a short one, bearing in mind the
value of her shell. Sadly, I made the wrong decision. Someone sprayed the build-
ing, cockroaches dropped into the water and Ti Vorace ate them and died.
If you have heard of the man who sat on a branch and sawed it off, well l am
the next best thing. Realizing that the sandragon trees, which we had cut to
stumps so they would not fall on the new house and Jolly Roger, had rapidly
put out arm-thick shoots to a height of twenty feet, I took a saw and set to work.
All dropped as I expected until one, a few feet longer than the others, hit a
neighbouring tree and bounced back... on to my foot. I was in agony and a
lump the size of an egg appeared. At first Rene thought I was joking but when
he saw the 'egg' ran to the bar for ice to ease the pain. The foot swelled so much
that the next day I went over to the hospital to see the doctor in casualty, It was
Friday and the doctor told me that I would have to come back on Monday as the
ray department had started working a five-day week. He said that even if the
foot was broken they would merely strap it up. I returned to Moyenne and hap-
pily never set eyes on that does for again. Putting the foot to the ground every
moming literally brought tears to my eyes but I decided that I would hold out
unti the Tuesday, my normal day to go to Mahe for Rotary and shopping.
I managed to make it to the surgeon's office, but he was in the operating theatre
so / went down the steep hill to the clinic in a wheelchair, helped by a young
Boy who had to use himself as a brake to prevent my shooting at speed across
the main road at the bottom. It was not at all funny. Seeing something like 100
patients waiting, and by now in great pain, I phoned the surgeon's office and,
thankfully, got Sister Patrick on the line. The surgeon, a personal friend who had
stayed with his wife and children on Moyenne, slipped out between operations
and inspected my foot. He was cross, asking:
'When did you do this?' When I
told him he said I was just as bad as a young boy sitting nearby with his arm in
asling, the result of an accident also five days before. I didn't have the heart to
suggest that maybe the boy had encountered the same 'doctor' at casualty.
John Nichol knew my habits and said: 'No Rotary, no food, and come back at
3pm - no, make it 2.30 pm. Your feet are dirty!
Rene appeared and took charge, shopping for pyjamas which I never wore
on Moyenne, a razor, a toothbrush and the rest. He had me back at the hospital
in time and I was put in a small room, which today is part of the refurbished
and 'historically protected old wing. After only five minutes, the bishop put his
head around the door and, when Ijoked that he was a bit premature as I hadn't
had the operation yet, told me he would teach me a lesson and send his arch-
deacon to see me at seven the next morning.
The surgeon did a magnificent job and when I asked how he had managed to
sort out the smashed bones without any cutting he laughed and said: Magic'
The last time I heard of John he was a surgeon at Charing Cross Hospital, Lon-
don. Incidentally, when I protested at the extra large plaster cast on my foot, he
said it was the only way to stop me from hopping around the island.
Later, sitting a prisoner on my own verandah, Frank's young daughter, Sabine,
brought a plastic bag and dumped it on my lap. Inside was a tiny black cat that
een and still a tough old lady.
we named Gris Gris (Black Magic). She is with me now as I write, nearly eight-

By instinct, on reaching a length of about five feet, the sharks left the lagoon

She was Frankie, the vivacious wife of Mike Kettle who did a spell as gynae-
cologist at the Victoria Hospital before returning to Britain to become consula
at St Mary's Hospital in Portsmouth and a pioneer of pregnancy screening techi-
niques. With their children, they stayed on Moyenne and became good friends.
I was saddened to learn of Mike's murder while on holiday in Turkey. Hav-
ing just retired they were walking in woodlands north of the Mediterranean
resort of Marmaris when a fourteen-year-old boy (to whom they had offered wa-
ter) shot Mike in the back and killed him. The police described the boy, who used
a shotgun, as having 'a long history of crime and mental illness'. It was a trag-
edy: what more can be said....
Swimming with my dog Emma outside the main reef one morning I was as-
tonished to find myself suddenly in the middle of a dozen large sharks. They
showed no interest in either Emma or myself but were busy attacking several
hundred jackfish which they had chased and trapped against the reef.
Two flashed past me towards the seabed, but slightly touching each other,
they immediately shot away in different directions. When I looked down to see
what had attracted their attention I saw a small turtle asleep with back flippers
on the seabed and front flippers resting against the reef. The little fellow had no
idea how close he had come to being eaten. I swam quietly away leaving him
still asleep as the sharks continued to dart in and take whole jackfish at their lei-

Having seen film heroes grapple with man-sized sharks and swum with the
real thing. I am very much aware that if they decided to attack, being such su-
pet creatures in their own eleent, men would really have no chance atali.
Often, when swimming outside the reef, I have seen the odd big one but it is
in the lagon in November that visitors can swim with up to twenty young sharks
and live to tell a fishy tale when they return home.
Among other regular visitors to Moyenne are a pair of tropicbirds with their
attactive long tails. They arrivetvery May, circling overhead to let us know they
are back but taking greatc are not to show us where they nest and rear their young
before flying away. They have been breeding on Moyenne since 1989.
The largest migratory bird I have seen on the island was a fish eagle, with
multicoloured plumage and cruel-looking talons and beak. It rested on the peak
for a couple of days before continuing its journey.
The majority of our bird population now nest on Moyenne, the male 'cardi-
nals letting us know that mating time is near when they produce their bright
red chests around February. After successfully rearing their youngsters, most
of the males lose this red plumage and for several months it is difficult to tell the
difference between them and the females until the mating season comes round
again.
Today, with water and an increasing food supply in the way of seeds and ber-
ries, the island is far more hospitable for birds, but we still need to supplement
their diet with at least two sacks of rice a week. Obviously they are matching
the human population boom on Mahe.
Quite a number of visitors ask if we have any snakes on the island. The an-
swer is: No, not at all, but maybe it is a little strange if we are really part of the
Garden of Eden! However, there are three species of snake in Seychelles, none
of which is harmful, producing only the equivalent of a mosquito bite should
you accidentally tread on one and striking only in self-defence. They are wide-
spread throughout the granite islands but rarely seen. The largest reaches a length
of four feet and they are greenish or bright yellow.
One of Moyenne's more knowledgeable friends in flora and fauna from the
earliest days to the present is Betty Beckett. She is a 'character', no doubt about
it. Betty started scuba diving at the age of seventy-six, plunging to seventy feet
and winning the prize for the best underwater photography of that year. With a
tendency to quote Latin tags, which leaves visitors somewhat bemused, she will
also lead them up into the mountains to see the strange pitcher plants which catch
flies and other small insects in their sticky traps. A fully trained vet, Betty re-
tired from the UK to help for two years in a leper colony in India before finding
her'destiny' among the tourists and those young Seychellois whom she contin-
ues to train to follow in her still sprightly, octogenarian footsteps.

Then came the day which was to affect the fundamental political character
Seychelles, the coup. Of course, being four miles offshore, we on Moven.
remote from the action and had no warning of what was to come. The first the
I knew was that on Sunday morning, 5 June, 1977, Rene came along from
cottages and, banging on the wall of the house, called for me to switch
radio.
'At 7 am on a Sunday?' I protested.
'Yes, he said, 'something is happening on Mahe'
The radio was playing music but then came an announcement they calle
Communique Number Three. Obviously I had missed the first two. It gave new
of a coup and a twenty-four-hour curfew which meant no visitors and that
were confined to Moyenne. Though no movement at all was permitted, whi
precluded Sasa from lifting his fishtrap, we had enough food on the island
keep going. So we just stayed put and listened to the radio.
Later on that first day, when James Mancham was in London attending the
Commonwealth conference and the Queen's Silver Jubilee celebrations, Albe
ile for the next fifteen years.
Rene announced that he was the new president. Mancham was to remain in ex-
After three days the curfew was lifted for a few hours and Rene and I wen
over to Mahe in the Amy Jane for supplies. There were men in civilian clothing
with guns in the town centre and on the roof of the Central Police Station. The
men could maintain their position. But the coup was assured when Tanzania-
troops arrived in support to help establish and train our first armed forces
We were glad to return to Moyenne where we lived quietly until the sita-
tion settled down and tourists were 'freed' from their hotels to spend a day.
the island.
And then, it was "back to business' and we entertained more and more i-
tors both for the day and for three-week holidays in the guesthouse. There it
no prospect of my taking a holiday or visiting my parents who were heading
towards their diamond wedding anniversary.
The coup had little immediate effect on Moyenne. Living on an island, we had
the advantage of learning about events after they had happened. We were in-
deed a little bit further 'away from it all'.
Saints and sinners
It is said that faith can move mountains. Certainly, I know that faith has helped
me through the more difficult patches of my life and that any truly spontaneous
good deed which I might do has always been 'rewarded', often from a totally
unexpected quarter. They have occurred too often to be shrugged off as coini-
dences.
I am no churchgoer and say my prayers daily in private, quietly sitting ona
88
rock, in the shade of a tree, on the sandspit or, more usually, in the evening on
my verandah, peace all around me. Somehow the prayers have more meaning
for me in such surroundings rather than being in my Sunday best among a mass
of people. I feel nearer to nature, nearer to God.
(Visitors ask me why I built a chapel on Moyenne. It is a regular question and
faily simple to explain and understand. When I was going through a problem
period alone in London I told myself: 'If ever you make it to Seychelles, build a
chapel to say thank you.!
well, I made it to the islands and during the first five years or so regularly
met the Anglican Bishop, George Briggs, at Rotary of which he was an honorary
member.
Early in 1979, he told me that he was retiring. I remember commenting that I
thought bishops died rather than retired. He laughed and said he would be re-
turning to England as Assistant Bishop of Derby and Rector of Matlock.
In fact, at seventy, he found the weather and the work in the UK too much
and returned to the tropics, first as an assistant to Trevor Huddlestone (then Arch-
bishop of the Indian Ocean, whom I had met in Dar, a tall, gaunt, commanding
figure and later, more intimately, at the bishop's home on Mahe). Finally, George
took up a teaching post at Mtwara, a coastal town in southern Tanzania.
We had first met when he was Archdeacon of Dar es Salaam and now he was
an Englishman' with a Tanzanian passport and Bishop of Seychelles. Of course,
it didn't matter. He was and is just a good man.
Remembering my promise to build a chapel, I told him that I had hoped he
would have been able to consecrate it for me. Well, get it built and I will, but
not at Easter as we are too busy then, he replied. We had three and a half weeks
to complete the task for the bishop was leaving immediately after Easter.
Naturally, we chose a site near the old graves but levelling the rocky hillside
was difficult and torrential rains made it more so. However, Rene and I, with
the gift of a few hours from the glass-bottom boatmen, Rene's brothers and
friends, managed to lay a firm base with a raised altar area and a slightly higher
altar.
With a three-and-a-half-foot wall on three sides and the entrance facing the
graves, we called on our friendly carpenter, Bertie, and asked him to build a
thatched A-frame roof, an altar rail and chapel furnishings. Bertie, with a love
of St Andrew's crosses — which he incorporated in all his other building work
on Moyenne — produced exactly what we wanted. From its consecration the
those who visit or pray there.
chapel has had an air of peace and tranquillity, noticed and commented on by
Building a chapel certainly brought out the best in those concerned with it.

About forty people, including Seychellois friends from the neighbouring is-

The service started with my asking the bishop and the père: Reverend father

Bishop Briggs unveiled a small plaque set in a granite rock at the entrance to
the chapel which had been covered by the Moyenne flag, a St George's Cross
with a green tortoise in the centre.
Later, over wine, cheese and biscuits, we chuckled about a 'behind-the-scenes
caper' when it was discovered there was no Holy Water for the ceremony. While
Iled the congregation to the chapel — the bishop and the père having gone ahead
to change into their vestments - Rene was commissioned to obtain some water
which he put in a Johnnie Walker whisky bottle. Eventually, the water was
poured into the small font which was ready for any baptisms and blessed dur-
ing the service by the clergy — the first time I had seen this done - while sprays
of casuarina were used to splash it around the church and the cemetery.

over the years, the chapel has witnessed three weddings, two baptisms, a
mas, and a holy communion. We also celebrate our saints days, or patronal
festivals as they are called in the islands. The saddest occasions have been lay-
ing my mother' ashes to rest in the altar and my father's funeral, but that comes
Later. The first wedding in the chapel was between Tim Nimigan and Debbie
Danielson on 5 November, 1983. Tim, a Canadian, helped to set up a successful
pottery industry in Seychelles. His bride was American. Both their parents, who
were church ministers, conducted the wedding making it a truly family affair.
But the chapel is not just for Christians. I have always considered it a place of
prayer and tranquility for members of all religions.
To illustrate the point, shortly after the consecration, I was approached by five
Muslims who asked to use the chapel. I said that if it was correct for their reli-
gion they were most welcome.
They replied that it was wholly acceptable to them and so they washed in the
sea at Pirate Cove below, prayed in the chapel and then came along to join us
for lunch at the Jolly Roger.
I was relating this incident to some visitors when a Sudanese Muslim said he
also had used the chapel for prayers.
I was equally pleased half an hour later when a couple told me that they were
Jewish and said they wanted to send me a mazuza to place at the entrance to the
chapel. admitted to being ignorant about mazuzas so they explained that they
contained a prayer that Jews touched when entering their homes or a synagogue.
moon came to the island carrying my mazuza.

colonel, visited Moyenne and on leaving the colonel said the mazuza should be
on the right pillar not the left. When I said I would move it, he shook his head
and said: 'No, it's a very good idea to let it stay where it is!
Many visitors travel after bereavements, family and personal problems, and
Lam happy that so many of differing creeds find they are able to enjoy a few
moments of peace and quiet in the little chapel in which to pray. I know that
after my father's death I gained similar relief in a temple of tinkling bells, Sam
Poh, in the hills of Malaysia.

No one who lives as close to nature as I do on Moyenne can doubt that ther

friends, try to help others. With the right will and taking the right way, we will
find God waiting at the end of the long hard road that is this present life!
I quote this letter in full for it expresses my feelings on a subject which should
be included in any book that tells the story of myself and the island.
Proprietors and pets
The chapel has a number of plaques, including one which records the history of
Moyenne and all the previous owners.
First came Julie Chiffon and Melidor Louange, who were given the island on
their marriage in 1850 and whose story I have already told. They sold Moyenne
in March 1892 for 301 rupees to Alfred d'Emmerez de Charmoy who not only
used to light his cigars with ten -upee notes enough to feed a family for many
months in those days) but had the reputation of being a ladies' man, giving a
succession of girlfriends a handful of jewels and a parcel of land as a farewell
present.
He sent his son, Adolphe, to Mauritius (where the family had settled after the
French Revolution) for his education. But the boy was wealthy enough to pay
his tutor to let him go riding with the governor's daughter instead. Father brought
Addiphe back to Seychelles, where he followed in his father's lifestyle until he
ran out of funds. The grandson, France, whom I met in 1975 on a visit to Sey-
chelles, settled in England and practised as a lawyer in Belsize Park, London.
Sadly, Alfred, who had vied with the Rassool family to see who could own
most of Seychelles, hanged himself at Baie Lazarre on Mahe, but at least we do
not have to contend with his ghost.
After Alfred, Moyenne was bought by an Englishman, J Harold Law Baty,
whose one claim to fame was the founding of the Union Lighterage Company
(ULC), which is still operating today. He paid 1,800 Seychelles rupees for the
island in March 1899 and the same month sold it to Miss Emma Wardlow Best
for 2,000 rupees — a quick, neat profit. Miss Best, who was born in India, came
to Seychelles with her army officer father who had a heart attack and died on
arrival in the islands. There was never any mention of her mother who, presum-
ably, died in India. Miss Best, a trained nurse, decided to stay and work at the
hospital rather than go on to Berkshire, England, where she was to have been
companion to her father.
It is known that she visited Moyenne for picnics during Alfred's ownership
and when she retired in 1899 bought the island as a home for herself and for
stray dogs which she found on Mahe. Miss Best was the representative of what
was then the Society of Prevention of Cruelty to Animals and she built a sub-
stantial granite and coral concrete house near Coral Cove for the dogs. These
numbered forty when, unwell, she returned to live on Mahe in 1915.
Local fishermen used to steal dogs and take them to Miss Best who gave them
A fair old con game all those years ago.
a small present and then had to buy more fish from the fishermen to feed them.
On Mahe, she went to live with the then matron of the hospital (they had most
likely been nurses together) and, having a fence built around the property at Val
Riche, San Souci, continued to collect stray dogs. When she died in 1919 she had

In 1914, Miss Best had given two 'parcels' of Moyenne to friends, one to a Mr
and Mrs E E Hoareau, the other to a Miss Alice de Lafontaine. What Alice did to
displease Miss Best I have not been able to discover but she was taken to court
and her gift was returned in 1917.

Knocks in the night
lar topics raised by visitors to the island.
Along with pirates and buried treasure, ghosts are top of the list of the most popu-
Even on my first holiday in Seychelles I was 'warned' about 'nams', as ghosts
are called. There were tales also of the bon homme de forêt and the dodociers-
pecially on islands such as little Cachée.
While the bon homme frightens anyone who is foolish enough to enter his for-

makes them vanish.

Today, if the younger generations pretend to laugh at their grandparents su-
perstitions, they still have a deep-rooted belief in gris gris and go to the loca
practitioners of the art. For a handful of rupees they seek assurances about the
fidelity of their girl or boyfriends, health and financial affairs. The film, West of
Paradise, set in the Seychelles, had gris gris as one of its major themes.
By chance, I caught a first-hand glimpse of the workings of gris gris. When
we were about to open the island to visitors and everything was clean and tidy,
I was puzzled to find, two days in succession, some black powder on the steps
to the new house and swept it away. On the third day I was caught by Madame
who was very angry. She had gone over to Mahe and into the hills to consulta
powerful lady called 'Daisy' who had given her the powder to spread on the
steps and thus assure that many, many clients would pass. Well, to be fair, many

When I took up residence in the small cottage, I was advised by Madame to
dose my door and windows firmly at night. She said that we had a persistent
ghost who knocked on windows, which meant that it was a woman as accord-
ing to island traditions male ghosts knocked on doors.
At full moon we also had the trudge-trudgers who shuffled past the cottages
which were on the path from the cemetery to the main landing. As, to my know!-
adre, we never had any slaves on Moyenne (the shuffling presumably made by
people in chains) I tended to disbelieve this story and, in fact, never heard of
encountered the trudge-trudgers myself.
Madame had planted a green plasti-like shrub, called Bois malgache, in front
of the cottages to ward off evil spirits. She was not amused when I said that if I
was one of them I'd use the back door.
The lady of the knocks was a totally different matter.
Within a few weeks of settling in to the cottage, I heard two separate and dis-
tinct knocks on my window. Human knocks was how I described them, not to
be confused with a bird or fruit bat bumping the window, or a passing bushrat.
I had been wide awake, reading a book by pressure lamp, and when the knocks
came they made me jump and the dog, IDon't Care', started to shiver, her back
hairs on end. Not imagining a ghost but a living person, I opened the door and
called out, getting no response. It was moonlight with very few clouds and I went
over to the family's cottage and banged on their door.
Sasa came out and together we checked the beach below the cottages and the
main landing and sandspit for footprints or the marks of a boat. We found noth-
took himself back to bed.
ing and Sasa, who was somewhat disgruntled and kept mumbling 'nam', finally
I returned to my cottage and locked the door. The dog was still there and
clearly frightened, which was strange as usually with any visitors around she
was away along the path or down to the beach barking her head off.
The next day, Madame explained that Sasa had been telling me it was a 'nam',
or ghost.
She also told me that Philibert, the policeman husband of her daughter, Marie
France, had been disturbed throughout the night on his first visit to Moyenne
and had never stayed overnight on the island again.
It appeared that our ghost was a lady and did not like policemen, as Superin-
tendent John Stone and his wife Margaret were also subjected to knocks on the
windows of the new house when they came one weekend.

You heard the knocks, was the reply.

"When I came to build the chapel on Morenne, I decided it would be right to

Trom that date we have had no more knocks and no more voices on Moyenne.
Visitors tell me that Miss Best must now be at rest. I believe and hope so.
Thinking back to the knocks and remembering how policemen, especially, had
a rough time, I guessed that the man who decided that Miss Best should not be
broughtover to Moyenne but buried on Mahe was the administrator who prob-
ably also held the post of police commissioner. It seems to make sense.
Three other 'ghost' stories are worth telling.
Madame had made a habit of placing flowers on the two 'pirate' graves but
told me she stopped after a while because, each time she passed the cemetery
later, she found the flowers had either disappeared or been thrown down the
hillside.
She came to the conclusion that whoever was buried there did not like flow-
ers. have planted a garden around the cemetery and the shrubs rarely flower,
alhough this may be because of the small amount of sunshine filtering through
the large trees.
One of Sasa's tasks was to collect fallen coconuts around the island but he had
been told not to touch the three nuts which I had placed between the graves as
a 'gift. I thought that perhaps coconuts would be more acceptable than flowers.
Some days later, however, Sasa returned in an agitated state, having dropped
his bag and knife. He admitted taking the nuts and said he had been 'beaten by
something which chased me out of the cemetery'. I noticed that from then on he
kept well away from the cemetery. The last story I have to tell is about a mani-
festation which, to my knowledge, I am the only person to have seen. Cutting a
path, now known as Julie's Jaunt, which runs above the main path and cemetery,
I sat down to relax for a few minutes when I saw clearly a small white dog run
along the path into the cemetery. The dog stopped by the graves for a minute or
so and then returned the way it had come towards the cottages.
At the time, September 1983, the only dog on the island was 'I Don't Care,

House guests became far more possessive about Moyenne than ourselves.I

98
which said: 'Movenne closed today'. Our German guest, whose English was not
t00 great, stopped the group by asking them if they did not understand the

Madame also was possessive, especially concerning the employment of any-
one other than a relative or close friend of the family.
on one occasion she discovered that I was to employ a young guy to help with
the bar and that he would be coming over with the brewery order in a couple of
days. As the boy was nearing Moyenne in the Amy Jane, another small and fast
boat cut through the rocks on Round Island and moored below the cottages. A
few minutes later Madame brought the newcomer over and said he was to have
the bar job. explained T had already taken on another lad who at that moment
was landing with the cartons of drinks below the house.
Madame was not to be deterred and when I asked her protege if he wanted to
work on Moyenne, he replied: Yes, very much! Realizing that whoever I took
on would have to live and eat with the family, I knew it would be necessary to
pay off my own choice and take on hers, which was what I did.
The outcome was that after the first week, Madame's boy went over to Mahe
ostensibly for some clothes and a toothbrush and never returned. It was just a
ploy on Madame's part to keep the workforce 'in the family'. She found me a
distant cousin who proved to be the laziest worker I had known but it was Mad-
ame who eventually got rid of him for me. I tell the story to give a small insight
into the Seychellois family bonds — the matriarchal influence is a strong, unify-
ing force.

Turtles, tortoise and a crocodile
Glancing through the log which I kept for the first fifteen years on Moyenne, I
see that I was hit twice by falling coconuts. The first occasion was while I was
sheltering from the rain under a Malaysian dwarf palm and the 'drop' was only
a few feet away, but it still gave me a bump' and a shock. The second could have
been far more serious. I was sitting at a beach table on St Anne, when I leaned
forward to pick up my glass of beer. At that moment I was struck hard on my
right shoulder. Many thanks, Seybrew.
The palm from which the coconut fell was extremely tall and, luckily, grow-
ing at an angle amid equally long cedars. The nut bounced off a cedar as it fell
and after hitting me, missed a friend who was lying spreadeagled on the sand.
A direct crunch would have seen me into hospital, if not the cemetery.
Surprisingly, in spite of the thousands of palms in Seychelles, there have been
few reports of injury by falling nuts. More often it is a palm frond, also a fair
an unthinking visitor.

Bertie the Carpenter produced a new bar and renovated the thatched restau-

The Wolf Cubs on their only visit to Moynne managed to elude their keepe
- unconcerned reading a book on the plateau — and doffed their shirts and
shorts, marked themselves with grease paint, stuck feathers in bands round hi
heads and frightened two elderly ladies passing through the cemetery by jump-
ing out and making 'very nasty noises', as one lady complained later. By this
time, the cubs had washed off the paint, dressed and returned looking like tit-
tle angels' for lunch. Luckily there were no heart attacks and I was able to have
a private chuckle, although I did 'speak' to their 'keeper' and they have not been
back to the island since.
I had to send two young rascals down to play on the beach when I caught
them rubbing the lines between the tortoises' armoured plates with sticks and
rocks to make them move faster. Of course, poor old Derek and the others moved,
trying to escape the pain caused by the rubbing in what is their most sensitive
area. For this is where the growth takes place, a ring a year, rather like a tree.
My anger has always been easily aroused by cruelty to animals. Youngsters
pulling crabs apart for fun or using catapults on birds are bound to send my blood
pressure soaring.
"Years ago, travelling along the Nairobi-Mombasa road in Kenya, near Athi
River, I recal dearly two youths stoning a mother giraffe and her baby. It was
senseless and wicked but obviously a game for them. The mother kept circling
the youngster to prevent stones from hitting it, taking the blows herself. Although
one me been sufficient to make its mark As I ran towards
the they jumped into theit car and made off for Nairobi. Happily, the baby
giraffe was unharmed but the mother suffered cuts from the stones.
Maybe my respect for living creatures goes too far but when I am walking
around Moyenne I step over any ant. As I am incapable of creating even such a
tiny insect what right do T have to destroy it — white ants excepted.
Mind you, I'm not bad at punishing myself. One day, while working on the
peak, I accidentally hit my head with a large cutting knife. Thereafter I suffere
dizzy spells which were very worrying until a neurologist from London, Reggie
kelly, put me through a series of strenuous tests and diagnosed BPV — benign
positional vertigo — which he said would right itself in due course. But lying
down or getting up the world span and I was happy when the effect slowly dis-
appeared.
Rene's daughter, Gina, had drunk kerosene and landed in hospital. But at no
time did she show any sign of harm and in a few days she was back running
about the island. Rene had visited a dentist on Mahe and after twelve injections
and a broken tooth to be drilled out did not go near a dental clinic for another
fifteen years. I had nine teeth extracted at one go and two days later chaired a
joint dinner of the Rotary and Round Table clubs at the Reef Hotel on Mahe. The
same week I had a farewell luncheon with the American Ambassador, John
Moran, at his residence at Sans Souci (Without Care). It was my first visit to the
house in which Archbishop Makarios spent his exile before returning to become
president of Cyprus.
John made things easy for me by sending over his launch, the Stars and Stripes
(Captain Jude), and meeting me at the Yacht Club. The main course at lunch was
an omelette. He was obviously as well informed as he was pleasant and thought-
ful. We shared one vice — good cigars.
On another visit to Mahe to see off some houseguests at the airport, I almost
killed myself. I noticed a man drinking a beer and eating samosas (small, crisp
a favourite of mine in East Africa.

Back to Piccadilly
Almost six years after I settled on Moyenne I left the island to visit my parents
in the UK. My father had undergone surgery for an ulcer but recovered quickly
enough for me to be able to wait to travel until he was back home where we could
spend time together rather than visit him in hospital.
Taking off by British Airways 747, for the first time I saw Moyenne from the
air. I also found fellow passengers from the Karanja and Kampala steamship days
on board, including Elise Broomhead, former owner of the Northolme Hoe lat
Glacis, Ruth and Eric Passmore, who put together Seychelles first radio trans
mitter, and Helen George, widow of the tax commissioner who drowned whil
sailing his yacht.
My first flight from the islands was uneventful and I arrived at Heathrow on
a cloudy but rainless evening to be surprised when a Yorkshire voice called out:
That's im, love! There, in the reintion area, were a couple who had spent a
day on the island six months before. In fact, they were meeting some other friends
... but fame at last.
i booked into Piccadilly Hotel, an old favourite for its comfort and central situ-
ation, at £34 a day — I was to pay E163 a few years later - and as usual went to
a small aé in Windmill Street for saltbeef sandwiches, pickles, poppyseed cake
and coffee.
London was noisy with the streets full of Scots, some in kilts, who were down
for a soccer international which their team lost. Two lasting impressions of that
first evening are the almost overpowering diesel fumes from the traffic and the
unpleasant smell of people on the underground.
But after a few days in London I did not notice them any more. Nonetheless,
London came as quite a shock after pollution-free Moyenne.
Although frail after the operation, my father was gardening with mother when
Tarrived at Clover Patch, Seaford. I spent most of the holiday redecorating the
house inside and out. We also went for luncheon trips, father driving his old
Rover 90.
Perhaps the most traumatic experience was when I drove a hired car through
Dartford Tunnel with huge lorries fore and aft to Essex to see the Pyalls, many
times visitors to Moyenne, and an aunt in Norwich who had passed the ninety
mark. The Swan at Lavenham, one of my favourite pubs, caused a detour through
Suffolk but the good food and Tudor-timbered room - with all mod cons -
was well worth it.
During my stay at Seaford I talked in the garden with mother and father sepa-
rately. I told them that I'd had a good life and that they should not be too sad if
anything happened to me. Mother said she would never leave England but the
old man expressed an interest in Seychelles without committing himself.
I was to spend a week in London shopping and seeing some shows — Rudolf
Nureyev in Sleeping Beauty at the Coliseum, the Rocky Horror Show, Deathtrap and
Ipi Tombe — but decided to return to Seychelles by the midweek flight, a deci-
sion I found that I had shared with neighbouring islander, Doris Calais. We not
only changed to the same plane but had adjoining seats. John Reynaud, Sey-
chelles roving ambassador at the time, was on board and I was carrying a letter
requesting diplomatic courtesies as the Seychelles High Commissioner had asked
me to take back a large box of papers to his ministry. The papers travelled in the
back row of the first class compartment. But Doris and I were in economy.
