Mercenaries and mutiny
As my return to Moyenne coincided with end-of-month accounts I was soon
immersed in island affairs. The tropical vegetation had not had a holiday an
Rene had held the fort very well.
as usual, there was much to be done on the land. Tourists were still arrivine:
increasing numbers and we had a steady flow of house guests. In my absen
By now, Seychelles had adopted a new constitution and the Seychelles P
ple's Progressive Front had replaced the SPUP as the sole party in what was now.
a one-party state. Albert Rene, without opposition, was elected president, and
again in 1984 and 1989. He had seized power at the point of a gun but at the la
election won ninety-six per cent of the roughly 40,000 votes cast. No one could
say that he was not popularly elected. They did have the choice of saying no
But affairs did not go all that smoothly for the president and the SPPE. T
incidents affected not only the country but also the tourist industry. The firs
was the abortive mercenary raid on 25 November 1981, best told as I experienced
it.
On that particular day I had gone over to Mahe to see Ogilvy Berlouis abou
my citizenship. He had asked to meet me at the Marine Charter Association -
the embarkation point for glass-bottom and charter boats — for a briefing on
the Rotary Club's annual deep-sea fishing competition. That year I was Rota
president and Ogilvy was patron of the contest. As such he was to fish himse
He wanted me to attend the skipper's meeting as a 'friendly face'
The briefing went well. It was a warm, sunny late afternoon and a Royal Swaz
Airlines plane flew overhead to land at the international airport.
About ten minutes later, Ogilvy was called to the phone and when he wet
straight to his car and drove away without saying a word I was surprised, tel
knew him to be a courteous man I asked David Pool, who was with the minister.
as usual, if he was coming back. Since the meeting was breaking up, howerer.l
told David to wish the minister well with his fishing. It was pointles staying on
and keeping Sasa waiting.
During an uneventful crossing of the bay, Sasa and I never noticed that we
were the only boat on the waters. But as we arrived in the small cove below the
cottages we saw Madame waving and shouting for us to come ashore.
We had planned to lift a trap to collect some fish for supper but changed our
minds because of Madame's obvious agitation. Then we discovered that a cur-
few had been imposed over Seychelles and that no movement whatsoever on
land and water was permitted. At that moment a mercenary raid was taking place
at the airport. We did not think we deserved to be called idiots by Madame but
we were wise enough to leave the fish traps alone and take a corned beef sup-
per instead.
The Seychelles Defence Forces did a good job. But to this day, I cannot imag:
ine how forty mercenaries ever expected to pass through customs, arms in their
hand luggage, even if it was labelled for a beer drinking festival. The sick part
for me was that toys for handicapped children were used as camouflage on the
top. With half the mercenaries through and in their minibuses the balloon went
up. Now the mercenaries outside had to fight their way back into the airport.
Eventually moet made it to an unfortunate Air India plane that they hijacked to
Durban.
The airport was a mess. A handful of mercenaries still had to be rounded up
on Mahe, the curfew continued, and a group of Zambian Rotarians coming
aboard Air India for the fishing found themselves in South Africa instead. But
the outcome was a decided victory for Seychelles and vindication of the newly-
formed Defence Forces, which acquitted themselves so well.
The second incident, a mutiny by an army company stationed in what was
the old prison my 'King George Hotel', took place nine months later on 17 Aus
gust, 1982. It caused a couple of days of confusion, for the rebel soldiers took
over the nearby radio station, threatened to blow up the petrol station in the
Tanted mad taffair sound far more serious than it really was
When loyal troops moved in and the mutiny was over some rebels remained
atlage, and we all came under curfew once again. Happily, with independence,
liberation, mercenary raid and army mutiny all within such a short time, we even-
tually managed to settle down to a more stable way of life.
Seaford and sadness
Only a few days after the mercenary raid, I heard from the British High Com-
mission that my mother was seriously ill. I went over to Mahe and telephoned
my father. There is a four-hour time difference between Seychelles time and
GMT. I phoned at 1800 hours which meant 1400 hours for father. My mother
was in hospital after a severe stroke. I told father I would be there the next morn-
UK that night.
ing as the first flight out of Seychelles following the mercenary raid was to the
It meant a mad rush back to the island to pack my bags, say an unexpected
farewell to my house guests — Barbara and Sheila — and return to Mahe and
the airport, where I got my tickets from the British Airways manager and my
travellers cheques from the Barclays Bank manager. Both were on duty for the
first plane to leave the battle-scarred airport. There were soldiers everywhere,
hardly surprising, but I had no problems with clearance.
Happily, Rene took care of the house guests, who were from Johannesburg,
and especially worried as one of the mercenaries was a woman from South Af-
rica.

a daily constitution along the main path and cards, dominoes and darts, read-

hair on Sherlock Holmes, we're only bringing the bus to you!
At the airport everything was made easy and we sat by the trolley which was

tages. Then, to my surprise and dismay, father said:
Wait here, I'm going to have a look at the duty free shop. I've heard about
them but never seen one
And with that he disappeared into a mass of people. I need not have worried
as some twenty minutes later he returned, commenting: Not bad but the only
good buys are for the drinkers and smokers. The choice of cameras and the like
are not so hot.! Which, come to think of it, is a fairly accurate assessment.
The jumbo jet, City of Westminster, provided a comfortable flight and when,
some hundred miles from Mahe, we heard the captain tell us that the descent
was about to start, father said in a loud voice heard all over that part of the air-
craft: What so soon? I'm just beginning to enjoy it all.'
Father Friday
So father arrived in Seychelles at 6 am, 31 January, 1982, and passed through a
well-guarded airport without any problems, to be met by 'Dudu Karsan
Chudasama, who was born in Zanzibar — his nickname means insect in Swahili
— and who is still my closest friend in Seychelles. He had his car waiting and on
that fine, sunny morning took us to the Yacht Club, where Rene was waiting
with the boat.
We sailed over to Moyenne on a calm sea and within two-and-a-half hours of

The good, the bad and the ugly
During the five years that father stayed on Moyenne, apart from the trip to Bird
They were good years and I was happy to find him eager to take part in all
our activities. He encouraged us in our development plans and we broke rocks
and built a fine new path from the plateau to the beach — Rene's Fifty Steps -
to make it easier for him to take his dog for walks on the sandspit. And, of course,
it was a boon for visitors also, especially some who had commented: 'God help
those with short legs, as they mounted the previously far more difficult route.
The work, including a five-foot stone and cement retaining wall to prevent
landslides, took us two years to complete. It was another job to be tackled in our
spare time when not welcoming guests and carrying out essential island main-
tenance. Rock breaking was the hardest bit. A new loop path from the house to
Lover's Leap, near Coral Cove, named Ray's Way, proved to be a popular de-
tour. Father cut the ribbon and inaugurated the walk in March, 1984.
We acquired a second, larger generator to cope with the installation of addi-
tional electrical equipment and security lighting and a floodlight for the flag at
night. And, on April 3, 1984, television and video arrived on Moyenne much to

Father was very upset when I was forced to put "Too' to sleep just before

The vet on Mahe, Bernard Moulinière and his very helpful assistant,

After Fiapi, Emma proved to be the best fishing dog, trotting out at low tide to
grab parrotfish in the coral pools and even chasing and beaching a young shark
on the sandspit.
She outlived Talk Coco and Catchy, a dog who swam ashore one day and
made his home on Moyenne. After Emma's death in June, 1994, our friendly ver
Bemadette, again provided two puppies, one of which we named after her and

the boss of the island menagerie. Ten puppies arrived — this time in the middle
of Rene's bed — of which we kept three, Bamboo, Tam-Tam and Cassie.
Our last feathered friend, Jojo, died in 1983 and when Madame and Sasa de-
cided to leave Moyenne and settle on Mahe with their daughter Eliana in 1985,
our animal kingdom' was reduced to the dogs, two cats, the tortoises and wild
birds — quite enough for such a small island. The second cat, left behind by
Madame, was named Whisky when he joined the menagerie.
In 1984 we widened the main path around the island and this prompted a
number of visitors to see how fast they could complete the one-mile circuit. An
inteligent and energetic courier, Thomas Lebon, held the record for a long time

At home alone
With father gone, I had the choice of letting the house to tourists again or co
tinuing to live there as I intended when it was first built. Without much hesitation
I decided to make it my permanent home and I now enjoy sitting on the vera.
dah in the evening, watching the sun set behind the mountains across the ba
The view is beautiful whatever the time of the day and the changing weather
and moods of the sea and islands provide a fascinating study. At night, the twink-
ling lights of Victoria and the shipping in the port are reflected in the wates and
if there is a moon and few clouds to hide the multitude of stars then the picture
is perfect.
I know no place anywhere in the world that I would rather be than on my
own balcony. The shush-shush of the sea on the shore and the wind whispering
in the palms are background music enough, although listening to a symphony
or concerto in such surroundings is truly magnificent.
Give me a good cigar and a decent bottle of wine, my old dog and cat cured
up in chairs beside me, and I am the happy hermit so many visitors expect.
But, born under Leo, I am gregarious by nature and often the extrovert, sol
really do enjoy having guests on the island and thereby the best of both words.
I moved into the main bedroom which had been father's and Rene joined me
in the house sleeping in what had been my bedroom for the past five years. Or
way of life continued much the same, Rene running the kitchen and I playing
host at the Jolly Roger. Those days when the glass-bottom boats were visiting
other islands, we worked on the land, still cutting and clearing and planting.
pied a considerable part of our time.
Maintenance of the property, from seawalls to thatched restaurants, also occur
Madame's departure left us with two empty cottages and we decided to turn
one into an island museum and use the other as a store. The museum was par-
mary welcomed by our visitors but, unfortunately, the souvenir hunters
mone them take any shiny shells or small pieces of coral. After replacing the
alished green snail shell exhibits twelve times I decided to call it a day. After
all, the visitors could buy the shells from Sanville, the curio dealer by the
Clocktower where I purchased mine. Nevertheless, the museum does provide a
doser look at the marine and plant life that can be found in the Seychelles and
serves a useful purpose for youngsters in particular.
Putting nameplates on the palms, trees and shrubs was another exercise ap-
preciated by the visitors. The early plates had been regularly renovated or
replaced and many more introduced, totalling something like 2,000 to date.
After work, Rene and I would shower and make supper before relaxing over
a game of chess or a video film. At the weekend, of course, he joined his family,
reluming on Monday morning usually with the mail from our post office box in
Victoria. This gave me time to answer any urgent letters and post them the next
day on my weekly trip to Mahe.
Ifound it pleasant to walk to a flat rock not too far from the house along Ray's
Way which had been one of father's favourite spots. At night, especially, it was
a wonderful vantage point to lie back and look at the stars, the Southern Cross
and Orion the most easily observed, the whole sky a mass of millions of twink-
ling lights.
It often reminded me of my unsuccessful attempts to see Halley's Comet as it
passed by Seychelles in April 1986. I had been assured that a good view would
be obtained from that rock at 0100 hours, so I sat with the dogs for company and
my binoculars at the ready. I waited for three hours but no comet appeared. There
were compensations, however, no less than fourteen falling stars and a spectacu-
lar meteor flashing right across the sky in front of me. My second attempt to see
the comet began at 0500 hours on the rocks to one side of Pirate Cove. Again, it
failed to appear, but the sight of a school of porpoises playing and leaping out
of the water just below me as the sun rose, all lemon and gold, made the wait
well worthwhile. As with the 'green flash', I had to ask, was there really a comet?
In the months following father's death, I had a series of nervous illnesses, in-
cluding shingles. Fortunately, Alex Mellanby, who had been a doctor on Mahe
and was now practising in the village of Dartmoor, England, came to the islands
faces. I missed father more than I realized.

Changing times and faces
With the passing years, I came to realize how much we had changed the 'face
of Moyenne.
The wide paths and nature trails enabled me to visit all parts of the island regu
larly. Where there had been wild bush, sturdy voung mangoes, mahoganies and

reef and neighbouring islands.

Moyenne had become what I intended — a wild park easily accessible to visi-
fors ofall ages and providing a glimpse of most of the vegetation to be found in
Seychelles, especially the endemic palms and trees. The coco de mers planted in
1980 and 1984 were strong and healthy.
Our 'Operation 1,000 coconuts' was starting to bear fruit and hundreds of
young coconut palms were growing all over the island where they had fallen,
no longer landing in thick bush and failing to germinate as in the past.
The forests of casuarinas along the 'saddle' were succeeding in their battle
against the hot, salty and dry climate which they faced throughout most of the
year. And the flowering shrubs and plants were slowly becoming splashes of
colour along the paths as well as on the plateau and around the museum.
Special visitors to Moyenne are now asked to plant a palm tree. Thus, in mid-
1995, Mauritian President Cassam Uteem and his wife planted coconut palms

Iexplained it was 'Moyenne calling' but that I was an island not a ship. When

Today, we take it all for granted, but I do occasionally think back to those early
days on. Moyenne when we had to fetch our water from Mahe and a smoky wick
and half a coconut shell of oil provided the light for our games of dominoes and

While we were forging ahead with developments on Moyenne, major changes
were taking place on the'mainland. The most important was in the political field
where a multi-party system was introduced. After an initial constitution had been
rejected by referendum, a second, hammered out by all parties, was accepted
and presidential and legislative elections held.
Albert Rene was a comfortable winner over his old colleague and adversary,
James Mancham, who had returned after fifteen years in exile in the UK. The
SPF took twenty-one of the twenty-two assembly seats, the odd one going to
the Democratic Party. With nominated members the opposition mustered a mere
five seats to the SPF's twenty-seven. But, with peaceful elections behind them
and pledges of unity on all sides, the islands should now enjoy a period of sta-
bility and progress, essential for a prosperous economy so dependent on tourism.
Fine new stadiums were built in time for Seychelles to host the Fourth Indian
Ocean Games when Seychellois sportsmen won a greater number (seventy-five)
of medals than ever before. Tourism topped the 120,000 mark in 1995.
Seychelles certainly appeared to be gearing itself well for the twentieth cen-
tury. Moyenne, too.

Pieces of eight
Pirates and buried treasure are subjects which have fascinated me since boyhood
— and the majority of visitors to Moyenne, if their daily stream of questions is
anything to go by.
With a legendary £30 million treasure on its peak, Moyenne surely invites
those who 'step ashore as the pirates of old' to dig for information if not the real
thing.
After all, Hodoul the privateer made his base on neighbouring St Anne and
sailed from his safe anchorage a mere half-mile from our Jolly Roger to raid ship-
ping and return there with his loot.
And, on Mahe, John Wilkins is still seeking the £120 million hoard of La Buze
— Olivier Vasseur — which eluded his father, who solved the pirates
cryptogram treasure map thirty-odd years before.
One of the regular questions put by visitors is why people think there is pi-
rate treasure in Seychelles.
The answer is logical enough. When piracy was still common in the Indian
Ocean, the Seychelles islands were uninhabited, unlike, say, the East African
coast, where, apart from the indigenous tribes, Arabs and Portuguese had been
well established.
What a wonderful haven Seychelles offered for any ship to put in for repairs
a wit to Thered aste abundance of timber, fresh water, fruits, fish
Friend Would have been even more welcoming, well away
from naval patrols and authority and no one at all to spy on them when they
buried their treasure, Indeed, there was simply no one to die up the chests of
gold and jewels after they had sailed on to plunder more merchant vessels.
it is known that pirates, such as Edward England, William Kidd, George
Taylor hAve Bowen, Tew and White as well as La Buze, were in Sevchelles
waters and that treasures, such as the Bishop of Goa's fiery cross, have yet to be
When visitors ask if believe there is a treasure on Moyenne my reply is: Why
Any of our granite islands with a good anchorage nearby must qualify as a
passible resting place for pirates and their ill-gotten gains. The islands circling
the bay of Victoria, including Moyenne, would be my first choice rather than
the larger mass of Mahe where a site might not be so easily marked. Fregate,
which is said to have the remains of a pirate stockade, is another likely spot for
hidden gold.
Ofcourse, Moyenne has had its quota of amateur treasure hunters armed with
metal detectors and pendulums.
Personally, I would advise against using detectors as the widely varying
amounts ofiron ore in the island's rocks and boulders make it a frustrating game
for the more serious-minded.
Take a metal detector along a beach and the operator will receive a steady low
note which will peak when passing over a bottle top, can or perhaps ring. The
same detector, even the most sophisticated, will emulate a police siren as it moves
along the paths and between the giant boulders. And, when it gives a prolonged
rock just below the surface.
beep at a likely treasure site it may well be reacting to an especially iron-heavy
On a map of Moyenne which we give to visitors two sites are marked Big
Dig and Small Dig'. The latter is a good example of the fallibility of metal de-
tectors.
A young guest from Tags Island in the River Thames, England, arrived with
the very latest in detectors and I gave him permission to try his luck anywhere
on Moyenne. He spent about three days searching all possible and not-so-possi-
blesites, finally bringing a map to the Jolly Roger where, over a couple of beers,
he assured me that two points marked with an X showed the exact location of
buried chests. He had marked the sites also with two sticks and they were close
to Treasure Peak, where our legendary £30 million is said to lie.
To be frank, I did not take much notice of the double 'find' but, some time
after he had returned to the UK, I had occasion to climb to the peak to plant some
young palms. Passing the spot indicated on the map and having a spade with
me, I pulled out one stick and dug down to uncover a small rock in the shape of

We did discover a large granite boulder midway between where the markers
had been. Sadly, 'X' did not mark the spot and I decided it was my final venture
with a metal detector of any kind.
The only success we did have was many years ago at what is now known.
the 'Gold Cavern' on the main path opposite Long Island. A large coconut palm
had fallen down the hillside disclosing a deep cave at the spot where the rod
had been. A visitor staying on Moyenne at the time brought along his met
nite reaction on his machine and called out: T've found gold!

coincidence but we named our cave the 'Gold Cavern'.

Incidentally, Miss Best had built a coral cement rainwater catchment for her

The big dig
The 'Big Dig' is a different story altogether. With my fishing dog, Fiai, hat
been walking for the first time in the area high above Rocky Point in the as of
the island. It was 2 February, 1975, and a bright sunny day with the prain.
wind blowing from Mahe across the bay. Sitting down in the shade between tig
giant boulders to share a picnic with Fiapi and the ever present and friendin
skinks — a type of smooth, brown-speckled lizard — I noticed two small rods
set at a right angle, which is not at all usual.
Clearing away a thick covering of dead leaves, I slowly unearthed what re-
sembled the marking for a double grave. This surprised me because there had
never been any suggestion by anyone at any time that there were graves on the
island other than those of the two 'pirates'.
Returning to the Jolly Roger, I discussed my find with Rene and we decided
it would be worth further investigation. During the next few weeks we man-
aged to dig down to a depth of roughly four feet and realized that the backil
was coming out far more easily than if it had been virgin earth. At four fet we
uncovered pick marks on one of the four 'walls'.
With visitors to welcome and island maintenance chores we could work on
the site only in our spare time and so the going was slow, but sure. As we ap
he due down further without finding a trace of anything at all, although the
ath and rocks were vielding to our spades and crowbars, spurring Rene and
myself on in the knowledgehat man had been there before us.
At corom foot we discovered five-inch-thick walls making two small rooms
inside the dig and the whole area was enclosed by man-made walls, on one side
the labs having been stim into place in what I termed Egyptian fashion.
It was necessary to demolish the walls forming the rooms if we were to dig
further but we took a full photographic record for future study. Reaching thir
nen fet we unearthed a small cave in which the cores of two rocks had been
naced side by side. The cores must have been much larger rocks broken down
to their present cannon ball size and shape. These were removed and by 1 De-
rember, 1986, we had recorded the Big Dig' as eight feet wide, fifteen feet long
and fourteen feet deep.
With father's death, the excavations were halted and it was only the arrival
in December 1991 of a professional rock blaster — with accompanying police
constable and whistle — to take off the top of a boulder for the new kitchen ex-
tensions which caused a revival in the Big Dig'. I took him along to the site and
he blasted two large boulders which had proved troublesome by blocking an
otherwise easy path to remove the tons of debris.
During our dig another reason had kept us hopeful of finding something in-
testing at the site. While at a depth of six or seven feet, I noticed that two giant
boulders above the working were becoming unstable. On examination, I discov-
ered that they had been jammed in position by small rocks and if left alone could
prove to be excellent booby traps, squashing Rene and myself in the hole. Using
acrowbar, I carefully levered out the small rocks to enable the larger boulder to
fall down the hillside and clear of the working. It fell according to plan on 2 June,
1986, to be followed by its mate a week later.
Clearing the areas where the boulders had been standing, I found they were
flat and there was no doubt that they had been put in place by man and that
fiften to twenty men would have been needed. This, in turn, required a ship.
But, what sort of ship? Perhaps, just perhaps, a pirate ship. Certainly, no previ-
ous owner of Moyenne had any reason for such an elaborate 'dig' or the necessary
labour it must have involved. For doubting Thomases we have a full photo-
graphic record, even showing the bigger boulder falling.
After the blaster, the site remained untouched, strewn with large rocks and a
formidable task before any new excavation could take place. Unfortunately, I
had to fly to Singapore for a hernia operation in April 1992 — like father like
the year.

zine of February 1987. The large boulder in the centre of the picture was one of
those blasted five years later.
With the arrival of my father on Moyenne, the Press, especially the Yorkshie
media, had a field day. Three- and four-column photographs showing the two
of us waving farewell to visitors against a background of granite rocks and co-
conut palms illustrated articles with headlines such as: Robinson Cruse and
Dad Friday', 'Man Friday, 89, on the Yorkshire treasure island', Man Friday at
89 revels in the simple life' and 'Simple life for York's castaways on sunshine
isle'.
Father thought it all great fun and it did not do our tourist business any harm
either. Visitors to Seychelles made a beeline for Moyenne, Brendon and his two
'Fridays' and the Press comments extolled Moyenne's beauty and its Creole cui-
sine. In 1988 I did a small survey of comments by leading British newspapers:

In September, 1988. The Times of London ran a series of humorous and seri-
ous letters in reply to one published from a schoolday friend, Cliff Moiser, who
had asked for suggestions as to what he should take a man who lived on an is.
land in the Indian Ocean.
Now the clerk to Portsmouth magistrates, he brought me some of the replies:
A dirty seat in a train that's late (ripped out by vandals); the ashes of a British
passport; a credit card; litter; some pollution; English food; some (or a whole)
Silton cheese; copies of various UK tax regulations to prove the wisdom of hav-
ing settled away from it all; a biography of Mr Arthur Scargill and others, for
the same reason; a tin of bath olivers; a copy of Country Life magazine. Sadly, he
did not bring the Stilton.
Incidentaly, in his book Dewsbury Boy (May, 1994), Cliff described me as an
'enigma'. As I don't always understand myself I considered it fair comment.
An amorous tortoise
Ofall the newspaper reports, the one which made me chuckle the most was by
Victoria Brittain in The Guardian. Headlined 'The Trouble with Derek, an amo-
rous encounter in an island paradise', it is worth recording as a visitor's view of
Moyenne and one of its giants.
'A lovelorn giant tortoise called Derek forced me to stand on the lunch table
and take off my skirt as he struggled to share the fragile bench I was perched on
for what had been a perfect meal of fish curry, fresh coconut, breadfruit chips,
grated mango and pawpaw. It is a mistake to wear a yellow skirt in front of a
giant tortoise with an insatiable passion for bananas and anything which stirs
his memory of them.
'Apart from the amazing Derek, Moyenne Island, a dot on the map of the In-
dian Ocean, halfway between Africa and India, is like most of the other 114
islands of Seychelles, an idyll of empty coral beaches and turquoise sea filled
with exotic fish. The fish here are as tame as the giant tortoises since Moyenne is
set in the St Anne Marine National Park where no fishing, hunting or shell col-
lecting are allowed.
'Moyenne is protected from being swamped by tourists by being visitable just
for the day in a glass-bottomed boat which makes only one trip each morning
from the main island, Mahe.
'The island and its one house belong to tortoise Derek's owner, a thoroughly
transplanted journalist from Yorkshire called Brendon Grimshaw who last year
buried his ninety-two-year-old father in the island's three-grave cemetery..'
From time to time I received articles and cuttings from overseas which I could
not understand but assumed they were complimentary. Two such pieces were
from a leading Israeli newspaper (Didi Menuci in The Last News') and the So-

Magazine features usually took a more in-depth look at the island and its res

A colleague from my earliest newspaper days, Maurice Colbeck, following

a pretty down-to-earth sort of name, vou'll agree and to hear it in such exotic
surroundings was almost startling. The only Grimshaw I'd known had been
Brendon of that ilk, chief reporter on a local newspaper in Dewsbury when 1
worked there just after the war. I was hardly likely to find him here.
'You've guessed, of course, that a moment later I turned a corner to be con-
fronted by the one and only Brendon himself. Forty years older, leaner, browner,
clad in shirt, shorts and sandals, but unmistakeably the Grimshaw I remembered
— extrovert, a "character" to his fingertips and thoroughly enjoying himself as
the Yorkshireman gone native host to a bunch of peeling tourists.
Immediately and in colourful detail, Brendon announced details of our old
acquaintanceship to the mixed bag of Dutch, English and Americans invading
his island. He bade us make free with his old Creole villa, made my wife put her
feet up and produced a bottle of wine. And then, between shouting abuse at the
giant tortoises eating the food scattered for a crowd of exotic finches he'd intro-
duced to Moyenne, he regaled everyone in sight with his recollections of what
used to be called the Heavy Woollen District.
What they made of it, or Brendon, I shall never know. He told them about
the redoubtable old editor of the Batley News series who thought nothing of
threatening to throw a young reporter out of the window if he failed to get the
nitials of all the mourners in the endless funeral reports we published. I don
think he ever did it, or there'd be scars on me to prove it. Rayner Roberts (RR as
hewas called by everyone, including himself) was a character in the grand style,
priding himself that he didn't just produce reporters but "newspapermen"
endon must have represented one of his greatest achievements, a man who
could not only find and write the news, but then set it on the old Linotype ma-

The articles on Moyenne and myself which I liked the best were At Home on
Treasure Island' by Harold Baldwin in March 1989 in the now defunct Trust
magazine, Brian Tetle's One Man's Island' in the 1992 edition of Silhouette, the
in-flight magazine for Air Seychelles, and Keeping Open House on Grimshaw's
Treasure Island' by Roderick Smith in the Lifestyle section of The Expatriate maga-
zine (1986).
All the articles were superbly illustrated.
There was a nice piece, with some excellent black and white drawings by Bette
Whitlock, published in Riyadh, Saudi Arabia. It ended with the verse: I met a
man who owns an island, twice ringed by reefs and very far away. And the only
thing that dared tread upon his land, the turtle and the tortoise and the wave of
sand.'
Since that first interview for the BBC there must have been something like an-
other thirty for radio programmes in the UK, Europe, the Americas, the Far East,
Africa, Australia and New Zealand.
Television crews have materialized regularly from all parts of the world, the
most memorable being Japanese who brought with them a remarkably beauti-
ful actress for instant translation of my interview. They were the only ones to lie
on their backs to get tremendous shots of parasol palms.
A French team came to plant a flag on the peak declaring: 'Seychelles Capitu-
lation' for a treasure hunt game with participants in studios in Paris and Rome
and the clue hunter using a helicopter to follow their instructions.
Unfortunately, a parrot bit all the fingers of the young guy who was setting
the clues and made it impossible for him to climb to the peak. To be helpful, Rene
took the flag and made sure it would not be blown away by jamming it firmly
between two rocks. I had gone up to the peak to photograph the helicopter as it
came to take away the flag and was unexpectedly bombarded by scores of coco
plums which were caught in the downdraft of the propeller.
But I witnessed also the unsuccessful battle which the helicopter hunter had

Perhaps surprisingly, Moyenne has featured in only two films.
In July, 1981, I went down to the sandspit to welcome Omar Sharif, who wa
to spend the day making a sequence for the film Return to Eden. The crew had
arrived an hour earlier and Omar was to transfer from the power boat, Rebel, is
a small fishing job for the filmed arrival on the island.
I was puzzled to find him dressed in a smart blue suit with a white i dire
and a tie, shoes and socks. His first comment was: I'm terribly overdressed, arent!
I?'
It was no overstatement and I quickly relieved him of his jacket, shoes, socks
and tie and rolled up his trousers to knee length. He unbuttoned his shit and
Now he certainly looked more like an islander.
rolled up the sleeves. I lent him my sandals, going barefoot myself in the film.
We were filmed all round the island, starting at 9 am and finishing at frei.
the afternoon. It was an exhausting experience for me, let alone Omar who had
flown into Seychelles from Paris only the day before. Every shot, opening with
a clapperboard and shouted instructions for us to talk on a particular subjet,
was taken three or four times. The first and second takes were natural, the third
somewhat strained and the fourth downright awful. I was astonished by Omar's
ability to switch on a tremendous smile within seconds of appearing to be ut-
terly dejected.
In between shots, Omar and I accompanied by Fiapi, who managed to'stea
almost every scene — chatted about the island and his life and his love of horse
racing, gambling and bridge. I felt that he would be glad to return to Deaville
as soon as possible. Just before leaving we had a couple of Camparis and tonic
on the rocks and knew we had earned them.
The following week the film crew returned and I had to go through a number
of shots again, as if I was talking to Omar, who had flown back to France For
continuity, they asked me to wear the same yellow shirt but I could do nothing
about the short haircut which I'd had during the week. On the odd occasion when
I watch Return to Eden I wonder what viewers make of the two rather different
looking men in yellow shirts who introduced Omar to Moyenne. Incidentally it
took the film crew twenty-two hours to produce a final four minutes of film.
Apart from meeting Omar Sharif, who was a thoroughly nice guy, the other
compensation was the friendship I formed with the scriptwriter, Marc Alexan-
der.
The BBC Pebble Mill at One series in June 1985 was considerably easier and
mandatin three hours, to produce a pleasant and informatwell or
all within three hs the best film coverage of an and informative fifteen-
minute feature It remains the best film coverage of any sort on Movenne.
The television interviews contentes and provide excellent publicity not only
ir Movenne but Sechelles. In the first six months of 1995 several cameramen
hare visited Moyenne from Africa Journal (Reuters), Vox Germany, Channel 7

An aerial shot of Moyenne was featured on the R1 stamp of a Seychelles issue
1o mark the fortieth anniversary of the accession of Queen Elizabeth 11 in 1992.
of course, it was the queen's head on the stamp, not mine. The island was also
shown on the reverse side of the old, red 100-rupee note. While not quite the

Albert Rene came to power, is a public holiday, and on that day in 1991 I was
relaxing with a cigar on the beach at Moyenne.
No one was expected on the island and I was surprised to see a small boat
put in with Rene and Dudu on board. They walked up the sandspit and were
dearly relieved to see me alive. Hugging and ruffling my hair they kept repeat-
ing: Thank God you are still with us!
Then, over a drink, they told me of their gruesome experience of going to the
mortuary to identity the body of 'Brendon who had drowned off Moyenne'. In
fact, it was a man from Cerf Island, whose body had been washed up on that
island and wrongly identified as me. There was similarity in colour and we both
had big noses!
Not totally sure of the identification, Rene and Dudu had hired a small boat
— not too easy on a public holiday — and come over to Moyenne. End of story.
An unpleasant morning for all except the lively corpse still smoking his cigar.

Magic moments
derful place for conjuring up memories of the past. Almost uninvited, they roll
into the mind as surely as the tide washes the shore below.
The verandah of my home on Moyenne, in the quiet of the evening, is a won-
Whispering palms and the distant boom of waves on the protecting reef cre-
ate an atmosphere just about ideal for reminiscing. Perhaps it is because of
Moyenne's isolation, a thousand miles from anywhere, that I find it easy to re-
live experiences stretched over six decades and bring into focus scenes of mv
rian steppes and endless African plains.
travels from as far apart as the Amazonian and Congo rainforests to the Sibe-
Places and people tumble over one another in my mind, the line of thinking
broken, perhaps, by the hooting of a ship or the bright twinkling lights of Sey-
chelles' tiny capital, Victoria, reflecting in the waters of the bay.
Memories spring up haphazardly, leading unsequentially from one to another.
And, if my memory dims, a stroll through my island home soon awakens new
lines of thought as I look at gemstones or brassbound Zanzibar chests and
Makonde or Bali carvings, paintings and books — all treasured mementos of a
lifetime's globe trotting.
The artefacts are as varied as their origins. I hope they will interest you as much
as they continue to renew so many magic moments for me.
The seventeen-mile stretch of mountains on Mahe dominates the view from
Moyenne with their tree-clad slopes and massive rocky outcrops glistening in
the sunlight after heavy rains.
Facing west, magnificent tropical sunsets are an added bonus varying from
blood red to turquoise and gold, silhouetting peaks and imbuing the waters with
the deepest shades of purple and indigo.
Sometimes the mountains are wreathed in clouds, spreading a cloth along the
highest ridges reminiscent of Table Mountain above Cape Town. The mood
changes as quickly as the clouds and breathtaking seconds give way to medita-
tion and the inevitable memories and a return to the momentarily forgotten glass
of wine or whisky — aptly known to old timers as sundowners'.
Conquering Kibo
Undoubtedly, the mountain which comes most readily and regularly to mind is
Kilimanjaro, at 19,340 feet the highest point in Africa, dwarfing Mahe's Morne
Seychellois, a veritable molehill at just 2,970 feet.
From the time of my arrival in Kenya in 1954, it became an ambition to reack
the top of the sugar loaf Kibo which, with its twin peak of Mawenzi, forms th
bulk of Kilimanjaro. This name and its true meaning still provoke controver
among historians and linguists. Of the differing explanations, 'mountain of gre
ness, 'mountain of caravans', 'mountain of water', 'mountain impossible

The first day we trekked ten miles through small farm holdings bursting with

My companions were two women teachers, an elderly woman missionary and
an extraordinarily fit-looking young Swede in a powder blue track suit

Emperor Haile Selassie's grandchildren and had the advantage of training in
Addis Ababa, which, like Rome, is built on seven hills and which stands at an
altitude of some 8,000 feet. I must say that my heart sank a little when I saw him.
Surely, I could cope with the girls, but I guessed this 'Olympic vision' would be
sitting on top of the mountain waiting for me, with probably a couple of hours
to spare - if I made it at all!

Kibo. Easing our sore and blistered feet in ice-cold waters, there was time to

Off
From Peter's Hut, only the guides had accompanied us, and they had carried
with them wood to light a fire and water to make tea. There was nothing hospi-
table about this part of the mountain, so, on the way down, we never made
stop, arriving at Peter's on the fourth day. It took us three days up and two down.
You will start your assault on the main peak at one o'clock in the morning!
our guides told us. Drink your tea and go to bed. We will wake you up!
We didn't mind. Already the wind was howling and the air was thin. The
sooner we started and got to the peak, if we did not fall victim to mountain sick-
ness, the better.
The graffiti on the walls inside the Top Hut had not been encouraging: Put
my head outside for third time, vomited and decided to go back; Snow and
scree are not for me!; 'The way I feel they'l have to carry me down!' And so on.
But we were dead tired after our third ten-mile stint and slept soundly until the
guides banged unceremoniously on the wooden door. It was then, clutching our
stout iron-tipped staves, that we saw what a monster of a mountain was await-
ing us.
Our Chagga guides explained why we were leaving at that time. Firstly, in
the early hours it is too dark to see ahead of you; secondly, the scree of loose
stones is frozen for a good foothold; thirdly, you get to the top as dawn breaks
over Africa.
In the event, we found, firstly, that it was brilliant moonlight; secondly, that
the scree was not frozen solid but very loose; thirdly, that when eventually dawn
broke over Africa, we were a long, long way from our goal.

Savage sunrise
There was a definite track to follow but from the start it was a slow slog. Kili.
manjaro is no mountaineer'S mountain. No gear was required, just perseverance
and willpower. Reaching Hans Meyer's Cave, much later than expected, it was
clear that dawn would beat us to the top. But, when it came, what a dawn! Deep.
deep blood red, slowly softening behind an ebony black Mawenzi. It was cruel,
savage and intensely beautiful all at the same time. Unforgettable. And, spurred
on, we moved nearer the peak one painful step at a time. Taking a sip of water
every five paces from a water bottle which froze over within seconds became an
obsession.
Pecte
going. The two girls staggered, fell, picked themselves up, repeated the process
and made it!
At the top we collapsed into a heap, congratulating one another as we shared
a flask of hot tea. We managed to find the tin box which contained the climbers'
book, signed and relaxed for five minutes... no more, as the guides called out:
'Down, down, blizzard coming!' We needed no second warning as clouds and
snow were already swirling around us as we slid down the loose scree, rolling
sideways to slow the descent. Returning was a lot easier and we were back at
the Top Hut and on the next ten miles to Peter's, all in time for 'high tea' and a
well-earned night's rest.
The fifth and final day saw the guides picking everlasting flowers and mak-
ing coronets for us to wear as we passed cheering Chagga children from farms
on the lower slopes. Our arrival at Kibo Hotel to a warm welcome from Frau
Brühl was a festive affair. Best of all was stripping off, taking a hot bath and
shaving for the first time in five days. Sitting around the log fire after dinner was
special. We had conquered the mountain, really been among the snows of Kili-
manjaro. All the others in the hotel still had the ascent ahead of them. We had
done it and were signing the hotel log, adding our comments. Strangely, I do
not recall what I wrote that night. Maybe I was in too high spirits... mountain
and bottle.
Our small group split up within a couple of days and, apart from the young
Swede, whom I can recall only as Matt, I never heard from them again. It was
some years later, in Dar es Salaam, that a friend told me Matt had died of a heart
attack in Addis. I remembered Matt with affection and respect for his courage
on the mountain, wondering whether the strenuous efforts at reaching the peal
had strained his heart too much.

I was happy in Mombasa where, in a few months, I had become part of the
local scene. Nairobi, a colder and more impersonal city, never had the same at
traction as Mombasa and the coast. Today, almost 40 years on, 1 still retain my
membership of the Mombasa Club with its fine reputation, good food and even
a men only bar and billiards room. The club has a spacious upper lounge and
'popsie' bar looking out to the ocean, fringed by tall palms, and is on nudging
terms with that ever commanding edifice, Fort Jesus.
For a young bachelor, Mombasa had just about everything in those memora-
ble days. A score of good bars and restaurants, lively music and a strong mix of
tropical heat and sex. My favourite haunts were the Carlton for oysters and beer,
the Nelson for lobster thermidor and, last of the evening, the Star Bar, where,
with mischievous malice, I would take my more sober-sided colleagues and
friends down from Nairobi.
The Star had a small bar at the back. Access was through the noisy revelry of
the main bar and a door with a wooden square panel which opened to your
knock; and, if your face was acceptable, in you went. The barman was Changi,
and I guess more business deals were clinched in his den than anywhere else in
Mombasa. When the time came to leave, Changi would let you slip out through
a door onto a large square, eminently convenient for your parked car.
Nyali Beach Hotel was magic as far as my memories of those days are con-
cerned. On Saturday a three-piece band would play for dancing on the small
open-air terrace ringed by tables with red shaded lamps. Add a moon and a star-
filled sky and it was really romantic. Two chums and myself with our girlfriends
from the nurses home used to go down to a beach shed half an hour or so before
the last waltz' and change from our black ties and cummerbunds and evening
dresses (obligatory dance gear) and swim out to the raft anchored offshore. Ly-
ing on the raft with pretty lights reflected on the water and the sound of music
literally wafting over the waves was never to be forgotten. Nor were the bacon,

Tusker in the tulips
My predecessor as features editor, Maurice Frankland, had a humorous pen, and
when he died the friend with whom he shared a house, Franz Wolf, buried him
in a grave in their garden and privately published a book of his best works en-
titled That Once I Planned. Thoughts on paper in a Malta convoy during the
Second World War - such as: 'I want to live, To do the things, That once I
planned to do; My wild, ambitious schemes, My quiet, foolish dreams' - sadly
faded with his untimely death at the age of 44.
Frankland had a reputation for occasional eccentricity. In those days, an edi-
tor was considered 'god', and it ill-behoved a young member of staff to be
anything but respectful. Frankland's playful slap on the posterior of his editors
wife earned him an immediate banishment to what was deemed the group's hot
station, Dar es Salaam. Reprieved to Mombasa, he imbibed a little too much and
Lost his lower dentures on the sea shore and then, cross with himself, threw his
upper dentures after them....
Back in Nairobi, he gave enjoyable dinner parties but, often tiring of his guest
by 11pm, he would quietly put the national anthem on the record player an
disappear to bed. Those present would stand for 'the Queen' and then be rea

duction was well-nigh perfect and received the praise it deserved.
During my time in Nairobi, I had two homes, first a small granite house with
a large picture window looking down a wild valley — so wild that, sitting in the
garden one day, I heard an elephant trumpet and saw, to my astonishment, a
young tusker munching his way towards my flower beds. I managed to 'walk'
the elephant back home... to the compound of a white hunter at the foot of the
valley.
My second home was a wooden farmhouse at Limuru, about 11 miles from
Nairobi and cold enough at night for log fires and thick blankets on the bed. In
the rainy season it was not an easy drive as the last miles were 'murram' or red
clay roads which turned quickly to deep mud, and often we slithered into ditches.
I shared the farmhouse with Ken Ridley, his wife Peggy and two children,
Michael and Gillian. We all carried a change of clothing for such emergencies.

In that first year (1957) back in Nairobi I made greater use of my old Land-
Rover before trading it in for a more practical, comfortable and fast Mercedes
There were many picnic trips to the Rift Valley with its extinct volcanoes and
series of lakes, Naivasha, Nakuru and Elmentaita. There were good pubs, lik
The Bell, The Lake and the Stag's Head to stop at and quench your thirst with a
cold Tusker or White Cap. And the road, built by Italian prisoners-ot-war (wh
left behind a delightful little chapel), was superb and always encouraged me to
top the 100 mark on the long straight stretch with only the railway crossing a
nuisance.
One spot I liked in particular was 'Hell's Gate' where, a couple of miles or so
along a sandy track, there were hot springs and the chance to collect some
obsidian.
The car had to be left in the shadow of the high cliffs, reminiscent of a minor
Grand Canyon and ideal as a background for any cowboy and Indian film. On
one occasion I set off alone along the track. I passed the springs and ten minutes
later turned a slow corner, with high grass on each side of the track, to find my-
self 30 feet from two fully-grown young male lions. They were playing, like
kittens and, presumably, the wind was blowing in my direction, for they did
not notice me and, after 'freezing on the spot', I slowly melted backwards and
out of sight, then ran like hell for those aptly named gates. I never ventured on
foot in that particular area again.
'Tag' with the rhino
Sometimes I would take a trip with friends to the dried-out lake bed at Amboseli,
where we would play 'tag' with the rhinos. Keeping the Land-Rover's engine
running and with foot poised on the clutch, we would hoot and annoy a rhino
until he charged. Then we would quickly let out the clutch and shoot forward,
making the furious beast overshoot his mark and come to a halt. We would hoot

Usually, I found space near some flat-topped fever trees, parked the car, spread

twigs. Looking up 1 was horrified to see a female rhino and her baby — and re-

was very new and very shiny and the rhino would be able to see herself in the
hubcaps.
I barely hesitated before making loud whooping noises as I scampered away
from the car and into the patch of fever trees, up one of which I clambered as
quickly as possible. The trick worked; mother rhino ignored my car and came
charging after me, stomping and snorting below. I was well and truly 'treed'.
After half an hour I climbed further up the tree and, to my total surprise, came
face to face with two giraffes feeding on the young leaves. They were just as
alarmed and took off rapidly. Very quietly I eased myself down the tree. There
was no sign of the rhino — only my undented shiny car, so I jumped in and
zoomed home. I had been lucky again.
The Ngong hills of Karen Blixen's Out of Africa fame provided a popular, if
fairly strenuous, walk for Ken Ridley and myself. His wife, Peggy, would drop
us at the far end of the hills and drive back to 'Hoppy's' bar and restaurant to
await our arrival.
'Hoppy was the Nairobi hangman, who also served afternoon tea and cakes
and, in the evening, steak, chips and beer. Though definitely a rough man with
a rougher vocabulary, he had a soft spot for youngsters and would encourage

Throughout eight years in Kenya my colleagues and I lived reasonably nor.
mal lives against the backdrop of the Mau Mau rebellion. As a constant reminder
of those days, I have a drinking horn which was stained originally with dried
blood having been used in an oath-taking ceremony. The Mau Mau groups,
bonded with their oaths, had little effect in the city, but out on the farms and in
the forests it was a different matter altogether.
Shortly after arriving in Nairobi, I was asked to stay for six weeks on a coffee
farm, Watangi at Kiambu, and act as a companion for the owner, Constance
Findlay, while the man normally resident in her guest wing was away in the UK.
The first night was something of an eye-opener as to how the 'other half lived
in more ways than one. Driving out the dozen or so miles, a third of it through
the co

e moment. The Mau Mau were active in the area, and Constance was at isk

ers: Driving along the Rift Valley road towards Lake Naivasha with Peggy Ridley

by cars and trucks as they enjoyed the retained warmth from the sun. But we
spotted one still very much alive.
The children couldn't bear to see it suffer and asked me to shoot it. I stopped
the car and walked back to the snake and, taking aim, shot it neatly in the head.
The bullet ricocheted and could have hit me or the kids. It was extremely stupid
on my part.

port of Banana. I hoped to cross Africa from east to west and advertised for a
planned route was through the Ituri Forest — home of the pygmies — of north-

Travelling north, we crossed the Equator, missing out the capital of Kampala
and entered the Congo through Uganda's Arua and Aru. It was late afternoon
when we arrived at Arua and the immaculate young customs officer asked if
we had time for a game of tennis. His chance of finding a partner was rare. Un
fortunately, we had to decline as we wanted to be across the border and into a
hotel by nightfall.
As it happened, we could have given him a game, for the road barrier at Aru
was padlocked and we had to fetch the Belgian officer from the town, where he
was doubling as postmaster. It was already dusk as we walked the mile or so,
leaving the car in 'no man's land'. I recall my surprise on being greeted by eve
rybody we passed with a friendly 'bonsoir. This then was decidedly
French-speaking territory: no 'good evening or Kenyan jambo.
Whereas there had been no formalities on the Ugandan side, we now had to
fill in long forms by kerosene lamp and it was a couple of hours before we go
final clearance. Then there was further frustration for we could find no hotel o
guesthouse and had to drive on until we reached a suitable spot to park, make
our own supper and sleep in the car. For such emergencies the boot had been
filled with cans of luncheon meat, corned beef, baked beans and other vegeta-
bles. Biscuits, chocolate, canned beer and soft drinks completed our 'shop',
ensuring that, if necessary, we could keep going for long distances. To avoid
Petrol problems we carried two spare tanks. In fact, we tired of our canned diet,
eating local food whenever we had the chance, and had much food left over at
the end of the trip.
Elephant camp
Our first destination was Gangala na Bodio, where African elephants were be-
ing trained along the lines in India. Four Indian elephants, imported to help the
project, died within a year of landing at Dar es Salaam. But King Leopold Il per-
severed with an idea not attempted since Hannibal, and a team led by a Major
Laplume trained two elephants in two years. By the time we visited the camp
the total had reached seventy.

five to six feet high from a herd, stalked it upwind, then pursued and tied it to a
tree with a strong rope. Adult elephants, particularly the mother, were fright-
ened off by rifle fire. Then trained beasts known as 'monitors' were brought up
and the calf was attached to one and taken back to the camp.
Taming and training took between ten and twelve months. The calf had to
learn to tolerate any human approach, especially from the one chosen to become
its handler. Every morning and evening a group of men surrounded the calf.
singing traditional songs and offering titbits such as sugarcane, sweet potatoes,
pineapples and bananas. It first learned to rise and lie down on command. Then
it was mounted for a few seconds and gradually for longer periods, its legs re-
maining fettered. Walks were always accompanied by the 'monitor.
The next stage was to teach the animal to work and, in particular, haul a 900lb
load. The animals worked six hours daily but only in the morning as they are

the picture.

[??  • no hat n  ??]
reputation for butting anyone who called him but failed to provide a bun or a
banana. I tested the story and found myself subjected to a series of butts from
his head and trunk, finally ending up in a muddy stream.
Driving deep in the Congo provided two surprises. The first was the well-
kept state of the dirt roads, each village being responsible for its own section.
The second was the reliability of the many ferries which, miles from anywhere,
were always manned. I recall rounding a corner and braking hard as the road
ended with two planks jutting out over a fast-flowing river flanked by a high
forest. It was almost dusk and, as I waited for the canoe-propelled ferry, the sound
of drums could be heard on the still night air. The glare of headlights and one
hoot on the horn brought the ferry over from the far bank and, with a cheerful
across. The pattern was always the same.
"bonsoir or 'bonne nuit, the car with the two of us sitting inside was paddled
Stopping overnight in the town of Paulis, we journeyed on to Stanleyville to
relax for a couple of days and enjoy our first sight of the mighty River Congo.
At this point we had to abandon any idea of trying to reach Banana and the
west coast because of heavy rains. We decided instead to return through the
southern region of the Ituri Forest and spend some time in Ruanda-Urundi -
as the now separately independent countries of Rwanda and Burundi were called
the Nile.

Though disappointed not to be able to cross Africa from east to west, the de-

============================================================
PAGE_103  (source: IMG_1453.heic  confidence: 98%  words: 526)
============================================================

tour was tremendously exciting. We took the road from Stanleyville to Mount
Hoyo where we stayed at an auberge of that name.
Mount Hoyo has been described correctly as a 'sponge' as it is riddled with
caves containing magnificent stalagmites and stalactites. The caves had been dis-
covered only thirty years before and, more recently (in the early forties),
geologists had found bat guano deposits of up to 30,000 tons forty feet deep in
some caverns.
Local tribes feared the area, telling stories of ghosts and fierce animals. How
ever, they did use the Grand Homa Cave as a refuge from raiding Arab slavers
and managed to keep its existence secret by concealing the entrances. Chimpan-
zee and okapi now frequented the caves and a band of drummers made echoing
music for visitors. The great African adventure movie King Solomon's Mines was
partly filmed at Mount Hoyo and the nearby water cascade known as 'the Steps
of Venus' proved a popular setting in one Tarzan epic.
Having driven many hours and many miles through thick forest we came
across a small roadside inn where the patron insisted on our having a beer be-
fore signing the register — it was a house tradition. Sadly, my memory has ald
to recall the name but it was an oasis of civilization with the emphasis on com-
fort and good cuisine for tired travellers.
Arrow in my back
It was while I was staying at this friendly inn that I got the chance to trek deep
into the forest along narrow tracks to a pygmy village. My companion was an
American woman who wanted to film the little people performing their crab'
and other dances. The village was no tourist attraction. Indeed, I doubt whether
it had been visited by 'big people' before we materialized. There were no huts
in the forest clearing, just shelters made by bending branches and intertwining
leaves.
Our arrival was unexpected and caused great excitement among the scant-
ily-clad villagers. Two small stools were produced and we sat, as much objects
of curiosity as the pygmies were to us.
The guide who had brought us explained to them that we would like to se
their dances and, at his suggestion, a display of their prowess with bows and
arrows. The pygmies did not use poison but plant juices which worked as an
anaesthetic, enabling them to shoot and capture monkeys, which were then kept
in cages, rather like beef on the hoof, to be killed, cooked and eaten only when
necessary.
One nimble chap 'ran' up a tree and fixed a couple of match boxes and a ciga
rette box as targets. Then the 'shooting began. They were deadly accurate and
we applauded their skill. However, while we watched, some younger villagers
became excited and began firing arrows. One struck me with considerable force.

From Nile to Orinoco
After following the Lake Tanganyika shore the road started to rise, the country-
side becoming wilder and more barren with every mile. Rough and gritty, the
surface also worsened and there were sheer drops into deep ravines. We were
the only vehicle out there and, as darkness fell, still had not reached our goal.
Our headlamps spotlighting the stark terrain made the climb more dramatic and
it was with tremendous relief when, on a small plateau at 6,724 feet, we found a
stone pyramid marking the southernmost source of the Nile. We shared a bottle
of wine and took turns to sit atop the pyramid - an appropriate shape for the
monument.
Dr Burkhart Waldecker, who saw the source in 1937, erected the pyramid on
what was an adjacent hill a year later. It bears a Latin inscription honouring all
aohs'.
who 'since most ancient times have sought the source of the Nile of the Phar-
While siting with our backs to the pyramid and looking down towards Lake
Tanganyika we were rewarded by incredible lightning and thunderstorms re-
vealing in stupendous flashes the bare mountainous country below us. The storm
continued as we began our descent to the lake, where we found a firm patch of
ground and parked for supper and a well-earned night's rest.
The first pale blush of dawn gradually turned into a glorious, sunny morn-

That 2,000-mile journey through Zaire has been matched only by my journey,
a quarter of a century later, to the Orinoco, Devil's Island, and 1,000 miles up
the Amazon.
Even in my schooldays I had dreamed of the Amazon. The word was redo-
lent with anacondas, piranhas and massive rainforests teeming with exotic
animal and bird life.
That dream was to come true in November, 1988, when I flew from Seychelles
via London to Port of Spain, the Trinidadian capital, to catch a special expedi-
tion voyage in the comfortable steamer Polaris. She took 80 passengers, seven
naturalists, led by Tom Ritchie, a crew, mainly Swedish, and a Danish chef, whose
lot pastries with Colombian coffee encouraged us to answer a 5.30 am 'Good
lorning nature lovers' call.
The first day was spent steaming along the coast of Trinidad and its sister is-
d of Tobago. A freshening wind, however, produced an unpleasant rolling
tion and the captain's welcome cocktails and dinner had to be postponed. It
with some relief that we anchored in the calm waters at the mouth of the
oco.

That 2,000-mile journey through Zaire has been matched only by my journey,
a quarter of a century later, to the Orinoco, Devil's Island, and 1,000 miles up

the Amazon.
Even in my schooldays I had dreamed of the Amazon. The word was redo-
OC
lent with anacondas, piranhas and massive rainforests teeming with exotic
O1
animal and bird life.
That dream was to come true in November, 1988, when I flew from Seychelles
via London to Port of Spain, the Trinidadian capital, to catch a special expedi-
tion voyage in the comfortable steamer Polaris. She took 80 passengers, seven
naturalists, led by Tom Ritchie, a crew, mainly Swedish, and a Danish chef, whose
hot pastries with Colombian coffee encouraged us to answer a 5.30 am 'Good
morning nature lovers' call.
The first day was spent steaming along the coast of Trinidad and its sister is-
land of Tobago. A freshening wind, however, produced an unpleasant rolling
motion and the captain's welcome cocktails and dinner had to be postponed. It
was with some relief that we anchored in the calm waters at the mouth of the
Orinoco.
We took one of the ship's Zodiac inflatables and headed up a tributary known
as Rio Arature, happy to put up with the light drizzle which dewdropped the
vild vegetation, bottle brush plants and arum lilies. But we were caught totally
nawares when the skies opened dramatically and 'drowned' us for fifteen
inutes before the sun obligingly appeared and dried us out.
Orange-winged parrots, toucans, green ibis, a great black hawk and many
er birds swooped overhead or sat motionless viewing the intruders. In all,

============================================================
PAGE_107  (source: IMG_1457.heic  confidence: 100%  words: 473)
============================================================

we saw more than 100 different species. Howler monkeys lived up to their name
as they swung from tree to tree, and there were capuchins and squirrels to keep
them company.
It was a thrilling first experience of a South American rainforest. In the mean
time, a launch came alongside the Polaris carrying officials who gave us clearance
for entry into Venezuela.
Cruising upstream, we anchored in the afternoon off Horqueto Pequenco Is-
land, where another expedition was highlighted by the sight of blue and yellow
macaws, the sun turning them a deep gold as they flew home to roost. After din-
ner, I went with a small group on a night trip up a narrow stream bordering the
town of Curiapo. Aided by powerful flashlights we saw boatbilled herons, our
the outboard, we were able to drift with the current and listen to the magical
sounds of the jungle.
The next morning we visited Curiapo (population 1,000) built entirely on stilts
with comfortable wooden 'streets' and boasting a 40-year-old power supply.
Children begged us for our highly-polished red apples and we sent for more from
the ship, taking back in return a huge wheel of manioc bread as hard as a stone.
Described as a Venezuelan Venice', Curiapo was a fascinating place, the peo-
ple waving and breaking off discussions to shout greetings to us. Stereos blared
from houses and well-stocked stores offered everything from deodorants to
blowguns. We sailed just before noon but even at dusk, several hours into the
ocean, the waters were still a muddy yellow from silt carried out by the mighty
Orinoco.
Devil's Island
After a day at sea, accompanied by swarms of flying fish and a family of dol-
phins, we arrived at the iles du Salut, a former penal colony, the best known of
gentle.
which is Devil's Island. The weather was perfect and the ship's motion had been
We landed first on Île Royale, a light and airy French island with lots of
bougainvillea and other tropical flowers. We patronized its small hotel, drink-
ing Kronenberg beer before taking a trip round Devil's Island, which was covered
with coconut palms and inhabited by a solitary pig.
The third island, St Joseph, was a different matter altogether. Walking through
the ruins of prison blocks, now heavily overgrown, there was a tangible feeling
of evil, an oppressiveness which increased with the sight of tiny cells, some un-
derground, and knowledge of the pain and suffering which prisoners endured.
The only light touches on that visit were the sighting of a hawksbill turtle
which reminded me of my Seychelles home, and a long line of leaf cutter ant
each carrying a piece of leaf larger than itself, and looking both busy and com
calat the same time. In his magnificently illustrated log of the voyage, a memb

I bought a T-shirt showing an emaciated man being attacked by mosquitoes
and bearing the words 'I survived Devil's Island'. I was happy to return to the
warmth and friendliness of the Polaris for our next lap to Belem and the start of
the Amazon expedition proper. The first intimation that we were approaching
the great river came when the turquoise waters began to change to a muddy
brown.
There was something very exciting about nearing the Amazo, so ye-
gle fever', as one passenger described her feelings. Lots of cruise ships make the
1,000 miles up the river to Manaus these days but passengers rarely disembark
between ports. We got on close terms with both river and jungle, at least twice a
day spending a minimum of five hours exploring, often leaving the Zodiacs to
collect plant and animal specimens.
We had only one casualty, our tour leader who was bitten by a snake, but a
good doctor and well-stocked surgery soon had him back on his feet. The one
decidedly hostile snake we captured was an emerald green boa constrictor, but
ike all our specimens, he was returned home on the trip down the river.
Research told me that the Amazon vies with the Nile as the world's longest
rer but it is way ahead the biggest, moving 28 billion gallons of water an hour
o the sea from 1,100 rivers merging in the basin. The Amazon's jungles spend.

Anacondas and Alberto
A city tour took in a visit to the Museo Goeldi which had just celebrated its 122nd
anniversary. More of a botanical garden and zoo than a museum, it occupies an
entire city block and provides the odd experience of being in a rainforest yet sur-
• Of
rounded by the roar of traffic. It was there I saw my only anacondas, long sluggish
monsters not at all like those in a James Bond epic. But at least I could say that I
had seen them in the Amazon.
After a Brazilian-style lunch, which was different if not delicious, we returned
to the ship prior to an evening 'on the town'. We started much too early and spent
an hour on a wild goose chase all over Belem for 'the' night club called La Plata-
forma which turned out to be in Rio! Happily, we discovered Restaurante Sabr
da Terra and enjoyed a very sexy Afro-Brazilian folk dancing show while drink-
ing far too many caipirihnas (aguardiente, sugar and limes).
Alberto, the owner, was a very friendly host, giving each of us a rose-green
stone monkey pendant and taking us along to the local hotspot, La Pena, a vast
open-air night club with terraced seating for several hundred night hawks and
toilets for men, women and transvestites! It was dominated by a huge stage with
well-built men and women dancing and miming to ear-blasting music from a
battery of loud speakers. Alberto advised caution as the many young 'women'
cruising the club who charged $10 for twenty minutes were 'not always girls'.
We danced until the early hours, drinking the local beer and relaxing during

adventures, lunch, another Zodiac trip (4.15 pm) to return for another recap ses-

One of the nicest aspects of the twice-daily Zodiac trips was their individual
style of exploration. The number of passengers was limited to eight plus a leader
We saw far more this way and it was greater fun, especially when discovering,
say, a black scorpion (which I nearly put my foot on), to know what everyone
had seen and done.
I found myself clambering on board the same Zodiac with the same small
group nicknamed the 'Silly People' because we did unusual things and were
always ready to take the odd risk.
Two of the group were Maria Gross, the representative of the International
Oceanographic Foundation, based in Miami, and Rebecca Bruns, whom I often
found scribbling away in the rain and even after dark with a pen torch. Her even-
tual 'piecé' appeared in the Detroit News (10 September, 1989) and had, among
its illustrations, a four-column photograph of me rubbing noses with a sloth to
which I became very attached. Let Rebecca explain:
When you want to understand a place you should get to know its residents.
With this in mind, our naturalists bought a three-toed sloth in the market at Belem
and brought it on board. It was love at first sight, E T in the wild. Those groping
hooked claws, that sweet, flat sleepy face held all the innocence and vulnerabil-
ity of the jungle. Unfortunately, ticks and moths ploughed through her thick
algae-coated fur which saved the creature from being smothered by our hugs.
Our touches actually caused her to let out a heart-rending pitched squeal.
'To make her feel at home, we cut down a small tree in the jungle and hoisted
it from our Zodiac onto the aft deck. During our first "Rising of the Sloth"
, little
Lead-Ass (as she was fondly dubbed in a sloth-naming contest) ended up hang-
ing out with us in the lounge.
With our very own Amazonian mascot we proudly steamed up the beauti-
ful Narrows (Breves) from Belem towards the main trunk of the Amazon. Here
was the jungle of stage and screen, so close on either bank we could pick out
buttressed roots and fat black bees tumbling from the underbrush.
Every so often our whistle pierced the steamy air and fleets of dugout canoes
chased us for miles, paddled by spindly-armed kids. They yelled requests for
clothes or offers of fish, riding the ship's waves and always smiling up curiously
as though at some rare domesticated cats in a floating kennel!

pink Dolphins
On our first early morning expedition, we were joined by a couple of bubble-
rum pink dolphins as astonishing as the brilliant and varied colours of the
Pression of macaws, parrots, kingfishers, parakeets, aracaris and innumerable
There was always something new to see or do. Landing on the left bank of the
Rio Dona Rosa, we found a pond with several six-foot-large caimans and, try-
ing to catch them, quickly lost all our bait. A couple of net casts captured some
red piranhas and other fish for the shipboard aquariums. As a bonus, a troop of
squirrel monkeys gave a swinging performance in nearby trees.
On the Rio Tapajos we landed to tour a dry forest, swam and had a splendid
tarbecue on a long river sandspit. A heavy tropical storm failed to dampen our
spirits, and a sight I will never forget was the bald head of a man showing above
ing his fiancée to marry in the small chapel on the island. Dr Howard Temison.
Jr. and his wife Melissa, now live in Raleigh, North Carolina.
I came to like the Caboclo people who lived along the river on high ground
or in huts built on stout rafts of big logs. With the tremendous rise in the Ama-
zon at the height of the rainy season (the trees had water marks 40ft up), these
floating homes were a very practical proposition. We visited one log home and
also went ashore to watch a Caboclo making a large ball of rubber from latex
brought in from trees tapped in the forest. They are a friendly but poor people,
not to be confused with the Amazonian head-hunters or painted natives, said to
number less than 100,000 today, who hide themselves deep in the jungle.
The day before the end of our voyage we saw the point at which the black
waters of the Rio Negro meet the yellow waters of the Rio Solimoes to become
the Amazon River. A distinct line can be seen for several miles before merging,
known as the Wedding of the Waters'.
Anchoring for the night at Lago do Rei, our Zodiacs took us to a dense forest
area, home to the beautiful capped herons, monkeys and, along the banks, spec-
tacled caimans.
Pelted by piranhas
After the captain's farewell dinner, a night trip to 'capture' caimans had be
planned but torrential rains sent most of the passengers to an early bed. Or
two Zodiacs, one, of course, with the silly people on board set out, and were
warded by good weather. We edged as quietly as we could into a nar
tributary and with a bright light powered by a car battery picked out the
eyes of the caiman along the two banks. It was at this point that I lost my fe

============================================================

piranhas. Startled by the light, between forty and fifty of them leapt into our boat.
We managed to pick them up with our bare hands and throw them back into
the water with only Maria receiving a small nip on one finger.
An elderly American couple had joined us and sat silently until the piranhas
started to 'rain into the Zodiac. The man's comment to his wife, 'Gee, I've beer
hit in the face by a piranha, made us all howl with laughter. The leader, Dennis
Cornejo, defused the situation by quickly opening the icebox and offering drinks.
The caiman hunt then started in earnest and I was the first to lean over the
front of the Zodiac as it inched into the bank and grab the reptile behind its eyes.
Lifting it up and watching the swinging tail, the Great White Hunte's photo-
graph was taken. Each of the others (except the Americans) captured a caiman
and we all returned to the ship in high spirits.
Looking back, the most memorable aspect of the trip was gliding silently on
the still black waters, with a slight mist just above our heads and bats fitting
away from the powerful beam. When the lamp was turned off we sat in total
blackness, the jungle noises seeming to magnify. No one spoke. It was a moment
out of time and never to be forgotten.
Manaus was totally different. Although the city took an economic slump when
it was found that rubber could be produced more efficiently in Malaysia and
Indonesia, it had recently rebounded with a free-trade zone. We visited the
market, built by Eiffel (of the Paris tower fame) and the magnificent Opera House
where Caruso once sang.
Walking down a street in search of a souvenir I heard a voice call out, 'Hey
Gringo', and realized the man meant me. That was another first. The souvenir,
a toucan made of amethyst, is on a shelf in my den on Moyenne.
I had planned to fly back to Trinidad via Caracas but because of local prob-
lems and not having a visa for Venezuela, I changed my flight, joined the other
passengers and flew via Miami. It turned out to be a big mistake.

Lost liberty
As far as I knew, only the airline and my Polaris chums were aware that I was to
transit through Miami. So you can imagine my surprise when, stepping from
the plane into the concertina corridor leading to the arrival lounge, a mystery
civilian approached and asked if I was Mr Grimshaw.
He took me to a deserted lounge, confiscated all my travel documents and
made me wait for an hour. I was not too worried as I had six hours to kill before
my onward flight to Trinidad and, with a clear conscience, I was merely curious
as to what it was all about.
He returned with a customs officer and said they would examine my luggage.
I replied that my luggage should be transiting to my next flight. As I was not
entering America, I asked for an explanation. The customs man shrugged and
turned away, whereupon the mystery man walked me to the VIP lounge and

Attending the Tobago Rotary Club, I was amused to find that the bannerette
