I flew back to Trinidad and on to Seychelles via London and a three-day stay
at the Piccadilly Hotel. The return to Seychelles was uneventful and Moyenne
had survived my absence. 'Man Friday' Rene had kept everything afloat as usual.
A small box resembling highly polished brown-veined marble was added to
my memory table. It was made of petrified wood from the Amazon. There is
also a preserved and mounted red piranha, its mouth full of small sharp teeth,
to remind me of the night of the caiman hunt and the astonishment of our Ameri-
can friend when he was struck in the face by one of those voracious little fish.
With such an epic trip behind me, I had not planned anything special for 1989.
But in mid-year news of an unscheduled Polaris trip from the Mediterranean -
where she was taking groups on Greek, Roman and Arab adventures —caught
my fancy and I booked for the voyage starting at Mallorca, visiting Gibraltar,
Tangier, Madeira, Gomera and La Palma in the Canaries, San Tiago in the Cape
Verdes and across the South Atlantic to Belem at the Amazon's mouth.
Yes, Belem again. And then, after a flight along the Amazon to Manaus, off to
the States and Miami — and whatever problems that city might bring. This time,
armed with my visa, I was to take in Disney, Epcott and MGM at Orlando, a
thanksgiving dinner with Maria and her family and a trip to Key West before
flying Concorde to London via Washington DC. On paper it looked like another
exciting holiday. It was, in more ways than one.
Dinner at 4am
The start was not auspicious. My aircraft, with 360 passengers on board, was
poised for take-off at the end of Seychelles Airport runway only to be halted by
what the pilot described as 'a fault. He was' optimistic, quoth he, that we would
be away in an hour. Two hours later he was only 'encouraged', The fault had
manifested itself around 10pm. By 4am the crew had run out of flying time, the
166
fault had not been found and it was decided to serve dinner on the plane.

and breaktast. I returned to the airport worried about onward flights from
I asked to be switched to the only other fight to the Uk for tree days and

After a good night's sleep at the airport's Hilton, I booked in two hours early

and indoor swimming pools, first-class service and superb food. I was soon sit-

joined forces for the rest of the trip, forging a firm friendship. After a day at sea,

and two fine Moroccan fossils.
We headed for Madeira in a five-foot groundswell, with blue skies and even
bluer seas. Sven Olaf Lindblad, head of Special Expeditions and son of my friend,
Sonja Lindblad, for whom I had opened Moyenne to tourists, had joined the voy-
age as far as the Canaries. He organized a meeting of all on board to discuss future
trips for the Polaris. Among the suggestions were the Falklands, Spitzbergen,
Greenland, Hudson Bay, Madagascar and, not surprisingly, Seychelles.
One of my reasons for joining the voyage was to compare Atlantic islands with
Indian Ocean ones. In Madeira I looked at the wide range of excellent products
made from woven rattan. It was fortunate for our pockets that there was a three-
year backlog of orders. A tour of the island, an embroidery factory and the
botanical gardens was enlivened by a sampling of many fine wines and a po-
tent apricot brandy. The overall impression of Madeira was that every inch of
land not occupied by homes and gardens was utilized to produce fruit, vegeta-
bles and flowers.

Atter Columbus
Another day's sting, an alted at the small island of Gomera, definitel
not on the tourist map, apop enerite or Gran Canaris. The port at which wi
docked, San Sebastian, was the same one from which Columbus had set out on
the voyage in which he discovered Hispaniola, now the Dominican sland, and
Cuba. The house where he stayed is still preserved.
After climbing the narrow highway of hairpin bends, through sparsely cov-
ered volcanic scrub, we arrived at an area of dense vegetation dominated by
juniper, giant heather and ferns. Then came the Parque National de Gaajonay,
which alone would have made the stopover on Gomera worthwhile. An exten
sive forest of cedar and laurel, it was carpeted with tiny brilliant-green plants.
The 3,000-foot altitude meant considerable cloud and moisture, which produced
a distinctly eerie feeling. It was easy to believe that the forest dated back to the
Tertiary Age and is the remnant of a plant community which once covered much
of Central Europe. Our drive continued through the centre of the island, offer-
ing breathtaking views of rugged peaks and ravines rivalling anything that the
nificent sunset.
Hawaiian Islands can offer. Arriving back at the ship we had the bonus of a mag-
We sailed at midnight and arrived at the more developed port of Santa Cruz.
on our second and last stop in the Canary Islands, the greenest of the group, La
Palma. Driving into the mountainous countryside, we found figs, bananas, al-
monds, oranges and other fruit trees everywhere. At the lower levels were
mulberry trees at one time serving as food for the silk moth caterpillars, formerly
a thriving industry, and an abundance of Spanish chestnut trees — like the figs,
obviously growing wild.
Higher up we entered forests of pine and reached the rim of the dramatic but
extinct el Cadera de Taburiente volcano, 6,560 feet deep, 5.5 miles in diameter
and 17.3 miles in circumference. On the far side of the crater, recently declared
a national park, towered el Roque de los Muchachos (7,977 ft) on which an in-
ternational observatory had been established in 1985. The observatory is
considered one of the world's most important in view of La Palma's totally un-
polluted atmosphere. Descending a twisting highway to Santa Cruz, we passed
through vineyards, avocado orchards and many white villas, their gardens bril-
liant with all sorts of flowers.
During the next two days at sea we saw two whales and, in the early morn-
ing, Jupiter, Orion and Canopus (the brightest star in the southern hemisphere)
in a cloudless sky. Each evening ended with a magnificent sunset but, as on
previous voyages, I still missed the 'green flash' at the moment the sun dipped.
Halley's Comet and the 'green flash' are my two failures in spite of ideal tropi-
cal conditions.
As we passed through the northern group of the Cape Verde Islands we real-
ized how arid and inhospitable they were with scrub and a few eucalyptus trees

In the Doldrums
We returned to the ship through arid, rubble-strewn hills where only goats
seemed to thrive. Acacia trees (a weed on Moyenne) were the predominant veg-
etation. It was another fine sunset and we sailed at midnight for Belem, the
Amazon and Brazil, five days away during which we experienced the 'doldrums'.
The days alternated between blue skies and strong sun and heavy overcast
conditions with rain showers. A Zodiac inflatable boat had been commandeered
as a swimming pool and, with water pumped from the sea, proved highly popu-
lar. Daily we watched the thousands of flying fish bursting from our bows. Two
green turtles compensated for a lack of whales. King Neptune and his 'hand-
maidens' boarded the boat amid hilarity as we crossed the Equator to initiate
first-timers to Neptune's realm.
There was reason for a double celebration as the waters changed from blue to
pale green and inevitably a muddy-brown as a sign that we were approaching
the Amazon's mouth. Belem had not changed in the year since my last landing.
But it was an arrival with a difference. This time I did not have a visa... and I
was not welcome.
On my previous visit, I had obtained a visa from the Brazilian embassy in Trini-
dad. This time I did not touch any point where a visa was obtainable. The nearest
embassy to Seychelles was 1,000 miles away in Dar es Salaam and to send a pass-
port through the post was decidedly risky. I had hoped a transit document would
be issued on arrival. I did not expect to be told I must leave on the next plane,
especially as all seats were booked for weeks ahead.
After lengthy negotiations, I was allowed to join the bus for the airport to try
my luck at Manaus. I managed to stay with our group after an interesting flight
at a fairly low altitude along the Amazon's length.
And, together with my friends, I did make the plane for Miami amid the nor-
mal hectic scramble once the departure gates were opened. Exactly how must
be left unsaid. This time there was no mystery man waiting to 'take me away'

Disney, Discoverer and Duval
The world of Disney, MGM and Epcott kept me busy for three days, during which
time I also joined a small group at Kennedy Point to watch the space probe Dis-
coverer being launched at night.
It was as spectacular as I had imagined and night became day for those few
seconds before the rocket vanished from sight. The countdown was far more dra-
matic 'on the spot. Afterwards we celebrated with impromptu barbecued
hamburgers and sausages and canned Budweiser beer.
With a week to go before catching the Concorde I decided to while the time
away in Key West. Maria's husband, Mark, drove me down to the hotel La Con-
cha on Duval Street and we spent the first night playing darts (and losing) in a
British-style 'pub'. I will never forget the swordfish steaks and Key lime pie or
the many bars with live music, such as Ernest Hemingway's old haunt, Sloppy
Joes.
I discovered a cigar factory and stocked up with some excellent but inexpen-

'souvenir' at the Mel Fisher Maritime and Heritage Museum. Having lived with
tales of pirates and treasures for so many years in Seychelles, I found irresistible
a replica of a chain from the sunken galleon Atocha containing gold from the
wreck. For my memory table I collected lead shots the size of large marbles and
silver and copper scrapings.
At last I could say that I had galleon gold on Moyenne.
Incidentally, the Nuestra Senora de Atocha, a Spanish ship, went down off the
keys in 1622. It took Mel Fisher 16 years to find her and bring up the hoard of
gold bars and 16th- and 17th-century gold artefacts.
Relaxed and ready for the last lap of my journey, I boarded the Concorde at
Miami for what so far has been the flight of my lifetime. Certainly, if I had the
money and there were enough Concordes I would fly no other way. Comfort-
able service and the thrill of supersonic flight added up to a great memory. The
advantage of arriving in Britain in December is that the natives know it's win-
ter. I have shivered in many a hotel room in 'summer' (starting promptly on May
1) with all heating off and not a glimmer of a fire in any lounge.

another crowd outside a cinema on Leicester Square and this time saw Kylie
Minogue and Jason Donovan waving wildly and blinking at the battery of flash-
lights.

It was no Karnak but something to do before dinner. There were six passengers
from the plane in the boat which ran aground and became firmly embedded on

wet.
As the shadows lengthened over the waterway, and with the vision of dinner
fading fast, we mutinied, ordered him over the side and told him to push. A cou-
ple of us then clambered onto the sandspit and, eventually, we were afloat.
Although I was to pass through Wadi Halfa on future occasions, I never ven-
tured forth in a felucca again.
The Khartoum incident was potentially more dangerous. During the flight,

the hotel and take a taxi to Omdurman. It's well worth a visit and you can get
some real bargains in the souk, he replied.
Four of us took his advice and enjoyed our excursion, drinking coffee with

No joy in Juba
The third day of the flight took us to Juba, southern Sudan, for lunch and finally
Marobi at teatime. Unfortunately, a combination of open-backed trucks and a
dusty, grity road saw us arrive, very travel-weary, for lunch at Juba Hotel. The
retum journey to the airport only added to the layers of dust on our skin, hair,
clothes, eyes, noses and throats. On one occasion workmen whitewashing the
restaurant walls and ceiling refused to postpone their activities during lunch and
we had splashes of distemper as an added bonus to our food. Juba was the worst
stopover in all my travels.
Attempts to clean ourselves up in the plane before arrival at Nairobi's Eastleigh
Airport were never satisfactory but at least we all suffered the same difficulties.
Hunting-Clan provided as good a service as possible given the long haul, even
if dancing rivets and occasional long oil splashes on the wings were disconcert-
ing to first-timers.
Hospitality from friends or the New Stanley or Norfolk hotels made the Nai-
robi stopover a comfortable one. The next stage of the journey was by either plane
or train to Mombasa.
If there was time before joining ship for Seychelles, most travellers opted for
the train. In those days it was a first-class experience.
Leaving promptly at 6pm there was time to relax in a spotlessly clean com-
partment before shaving and changing into clean slacks and a shirt for dinner
in the restaurant car. The press of a button would bring a waiter and, in no time
at all, your pre-dinner drinks.
The dining car produced food to match the excellent service and it was ex-
tremely pleasant to glance through the window as the sun set over the Athi Plains
and see giraffes, zebra and a variety of buck enjoying their supper before set-
tling down for the night.
Dinner was never hurried and, while passengers chatted over coffee and Co-
gnac, attendants were making their beds. Sleep came to the slow rhythm of train
wheels on the track and when dawn broke the air was distinctly warmer. Coco-
nut palms had taken the place of fever trees; there was no doubt that the coast
was near. A tea tray and a morning newspaper (The Mombasa Times) encouraged
you to rise, shine and go back to the dining car for breakfast. Attendants made
sure you were in your compartment (beds already tidied away) in time for ar-

rival at the masa stay depended on the arrival of the steamships karable.
The Mombasa Stay depended on the arrival of the steamships Karanja ot Kam
pala and then it was a two-and-a-half-day crossing to Port Victoria, Seychelles
The journey from London to the islands, which today takes just 12 hours, te
quired a minimum of Suify e?ks thirty years ago. Even the 'voyage' from Mahe
to Moyenne was more difficult. Before the introduction of outboard motors,
ducing the trip to ten minutes, it required a pair of oars, a small sail and a lot of
luck with the wind.
There was one exception. Arteericans from the Tracking Station on Mahe used
a flying boat from Mombasa to the slipway on the old port at Victoria. But they
rarely took passengers, only staff. Noël Coward was one of the privileged fen
when he was taken ill while staying at Government House. The fact that Mom-
basa crops up so often in my memory is not surprising as 1 had discovered could
have a wild time and get away with it.
For example, when I lived there, a reporter on The Mombasa Times George
Price, became engaged to a nurse at the hospital and invited me as their only
guest to a celebration dinner at the Nelson Restaurant. We had the house speci
ality, lobster thermidor, washed down with champagne. After several toasts,
George decided to crunch his champagne glass (rather than throw it acros the
restaurant) and I was horrified to see blood dribbling down his chin. There was
no real harm done and later I dropped his fiancée at the hospital and took George
to where he was staying.
It was the well-known Castle Hotel with a wide patio and an entrance directly
into the reception area. After such a crazy night it seemed only natural that 1
should not merely drop him off but drive my Land-Rover over the pavement
and, missing potted palms, deliver him to the reception desk. I recall a lt of
shouting and waving of arms as I backed out onto the main road, happily, with-
out harm to hotel or vehicle. I've seen it done in films since but never with as
much fun as on the night of George's engagement party.
My route home was circuitous as I weaved in and out of the lampstands in
the centre of the two-way traffic lanes. At some point I noticed in my mirror the
reflection of a pursuing car and, arriving at a roundabout, decided against my
original plan of driving the wrong way round it. But the other car did and, draw-
ing alongside me as we passed, two policemen leaned out and shouted: "Chicken!'
Then they drove away sounding their police siren. It would never happen to-
day.
Schnapps and ships
Visiting ships in the port was part of my job and when the Norwegian freighter
Thor I arrived on her maiden voyage I went on board for the inevitable party. I
recall that the daughter of the chairman of the shipping line was acting as stew-
ardess and served a steady succession of large trays of schnapps. The captain
174
tarted by proposing a toast to the ladies. This was followed by many more, the
" being swallowed in one gulp. It was a hazy 'disembarkation but I
sund my vehicle on the dockside. My next recollection was of a lot of men,
withanother crossing directly in front of me.
winging lamps and making a great fuss. I realized that I was alongside a train
When asked What the hell do you think you're doing? I replied that I'd jus

declined — amid chuckles at the other end of the line.
Royal Navy visits were popular and 1 remember the particularly friendly at-
mosphere on board HIMS Ashanti and still have the ship's plaque on the wall of
my den. It shares a corner with one given to my mother by a Polish fighter pilot
in the Second World War and another from a chum in the Metropolitan Police.
The Kenyan Navy in those days was composed of just one gunboat, the
Rosalind, and her role was taken very seriously by her commander and crew. I
recall being privileged to be allowed on board when she went out to meet the
royal yacht Britannia to escort her into port. The Britannia was picking up the
Duke of Edinburgh (who had flown to Mombasa) to take him to Australia for
the Commonwealth Games. The Britannia made an impressive sight, the white
foam setting off the immaculate deep blue of her hull.
As the royal yacht passed, our crew lined the decks of the Rosalind and we
were told by the admiral on board to 'take station astern and accompany her
into the narrow channel along which several hundred people had gathered.
Unfortunately, little Rosalind did not have the Britannia's speed and, although
the stoker worked flat out, we watched our royal visitor almost disappear from
view. There was no grand entry for the Rosalind, who arrived a very poor sec-
ond.
Later, at a reception by the governor, I met the admiral and felt that, in fair-
ness to our 'navy', I should tell him what had happened. It was unlikely that
anyone else would have the temerity to do so and I felt he should know why we
had appeared to fail in our escort duties.
The admiral, a charming man, chuckled and asked, Was I beastly to the
Rosalind? Oh dear, I'll make amends and apologize to her captain. He did and
we all enjoyed a good party.
The Duke of Edinburgh was driven from the airport on a tour of Mombasa in
a vintage car which had been spruced up for the occasion. Unlike Princess
Margaret's visit, it was quite informal. Before the Britannia sailed that night, I
and a colleague climbed one of the dockside cranes next to the yacht and when
the duke appeared on deck we called out for him to bring back some gold med-
als. He laughed and, giving us a wave, promised to 'do my best'.

But we knew of the Rosalind's limitations; she was not a swift craft and her

We guessed rightly that the Rosalind would head south where there was a mass
of creeks and mangrove swamps. Flying low along the seashore, the plane, with
another colleague and photographer, John Downes, on board, spotted a British

ferred by small boat to the frigate, John took his photographs and the world knew
just what was happening to them.
The British warship's destination was Seychelles, islands which had provided
exile to a number of other personages, including King Prempeh of Ghana, who
still has descendants on Mahe.
The archbishop liked Seychelles, staying at Sans Souci (Without Care) in a
beautiful one-storey house which today is the home of the American ambassa-
dor. He tried unsuccessfully to purchase Sans Souci but acquired two plots on
Mount Josephine, which were returned to Seychelles after his death.
Once again, Seychelles was beckoning, though I did not realize it at the time.
Mention of Archbishop Makarios takes my mind back to a three-week holi-
day I spent in Cyprus to celebrate my 21st birthday. I was stationed in Haifa,
serving in the army, from where the military ran a weekly service to the Cypriot
port of Famagusta. Having pre-booked hotels through a travel agent I boarded
the small boat for the fifteen-hour voyage.
The skipper, a friend, told me it was likely to be a rough trip and asked if I
drank spirits. When I said 'No, not at all', he grinned and gave me a tall glass
full of an equal mixture of port and brandy.
It was not easy getting it down and I was glad when he suggested I relax on
one of the two bunks in the ship's hospital. The next thing I remember was the
sound of many voices and the rumble of a chain.
We had arrived at Famagusta and I had slept solidly through the entire voy-
age. On the return trip the sea was calm and such drastic medicine was not
necessary.
I was a sergeant then, a rank which, much to my dismay, was not acceptable
at Famagusta's Savoy Hotel. 'I'm very sorry, it's officers only, I was told by the

Champagne in Cyprus
The next bus-ride brought me to the capital, Nicosia, and the Palace Hotel. No
problems were encountered over accommodation but I cut my stay in Nicosia,
finding little to do once I had visited the St Sophia Mosque, sampled delicious
small cakes and dark coffee in the many spotlessly clean cafés. I learned that it
was deemed impolite to leave a host until the coffee cups were cold, and I still
follow this custom today.
Dropping the last five hundred feet to the north coast I discovered that I had
left the best till last. Kyrenia was delightful, with the Dome Hotel providing
splendid service and rooms with spacious verandahs overlooking the sea. Far
from having any accommodation problems, the hotel had noted my birthday
on the registration form and, while taking a celebratory midnight swim below
my room, a young waiter arrived with a tray bearing a bottle of champagne and
some small sandwiches and wished me a happy 21st birthday on the manage-
ment's behalf. It was a lovely gesture and a great start to the day. In the morning
I clambered up to the Disney-like St Hilarion Castle which provides a fairytale
backdrop to the whole coastal plateau.
Built on the ruins of a monastery established by St Hilarion, a hermit and friend
of St Jerome, the castle still manages to evoke scenes from the past. I stood in
what had been a large window looking below to the former jousting field and
could visualize the knights on horseback cheered on by their ladies in the 16th
century when the castle was built by the Lusignans and later strengthened by
the Venetians. Not surprisingly, it had never been taken by assault.
In late afternoon I walked by the tiny horseshoe harbour and the well-pre-
served castle to the 14th-century Abbey of Bella Paise (Lovely Peace). A former
governor of Cyprus, Sir Donald Storrs, expressed my own feelings when he
wrote: 'There is ruin, but no feelings of desolation, in that haunt of ancient peace,

The 'lovely peace' of the abbey was to be a treasured memory for the remain-
der of my life. My birthday, although spent alone, had been very special. But.
then, if one has a little imagination, and a normal belief in God, being alone is

almost daily to visitors on Moyenne.
The remainder of my stay in Kyrenia was spent on hired bicycles discovering
small coves for swimming. Always there were friendly sellers of lime juice and
water melon and everywhere I received welcoming waves from shepherds sit-
ting in the shade of olive trees, surrounded by their herds of tinkling bells. The
troubles which erupted in Cyprus a few years later came as a complete surprise
to me.
I had joined the army a week before D-Day which gave my mother the op-
portunity to joke that they had been waiting for me. My call-up should have been
a year earlier but, in my schooldays, a cricket ball had fractured my thighbone,
causing a boney protrusion which grew to the size of an egg. When I was seven-
teen a surgeon with the apt name of Mr Pain had sawn off the 'egg' to stop it
affecting my walking and thus delayed my entry into the services.
My first choice had been the navy but 'disability' resulted in a B1 classifica-
tion, which meant the army, a decision considered strange at the time.
Taking a train from my home town of Dewsbury, I joined a batch of recruits
in a camp at Carlisle, where baked beans, a sausage, one slice of bread and marge
and a cup of tea were to last me until the next morning. Apart from not being
able to sleep on the journey, the only memory that remains is the astonishing
number of large rats swarming along the tracks in Carlisle Station where we took
a train to Stranraer.
Still in 'civvies' and carrying our small suitcases, we marched to a transit camp
where things began badly when one recruit died on arrival. I later heard that he
was classified as A1! We were served a similar meal and given mattresses but
no blankets on which to relax until we were marched down to the docks to catch
the ferry to Larne, Northern Ireland. Within 48 hours I was 'overseas.
Things were better organized at the training camp. Anyone who has been in
the forces will appreciate the sudden shock of discipline, the aches, pains and
blisters from squarebashing and rifle drill and the total lack of privacy. Squirm-
ing in thick mud under barbed wire in torrential rain was barely compensation
for my first glimpse of the Mountains of Mourne. But at least the canteen, with
its pile of king-size buns and mugs of tea, is a fonder memory.
At the end of six weeks, feeling tremendously fit, I was disappointed to find

that f and a forty-year-old man wer le le only ones to be posted to the Koyal Army

The city of Chester and the civvy billets certainly provided a soft landing, a
cushy number', as my new chums commented. Any tears 1 might have that the

There was plenty of spare time to ramble through the famous Rows', enjoy a

Moving to Shrewsbury, I found the command had a thriving theatrical group
and, after a rather heavy criticism of their Poison Pen, I was dragged into the next
one as an actor for the first and last time in my life.
The show was Philip King's comedy Without the Prince, and I played the farm-
er's boy, 'possessing a strange sense of humour the foundation of which is crude
sarcasm'. The reviews said I gave an 'excellent' and 'spirited' performance.
We took the play on tour and had a lot of fun. Our most touching and best
audience was in a convalescent centre for the military disabled at Church Stretton.
Clive of India's house was an unusual setting for the production and we in the
cast appreciated being served supper at Clive's dining table. The atmosphere-
was great.
The only excitement during the whole of my service time in the UK was be-
ing locked and sealed inside a railway wagon with a consignment of currency
for the troops in Europe.
I was armed with a sten gun for which I had absolutely no training. The trip
to London and a night in Charing Cross Hotel gave me the unwelcome oppor-
tunity to see flying bombs, hear the engines cut and wait for the explosions. It
was not pleasant.
My next visit to London was in August, 1945, to be kitted out for an unspeci-
fied overseas posting. It seemed odd to be leaving while the capital was in a high
spirited mood following VE-Day and VJ-Day. There was a lively forces club on
Piccadilly and I recall helping one of my favourite actresses at the time, Anna
Neagle, move a barrel of Coca-Cola and being rewarded with a string of half-a-
dozen drinks tickets. Most of the West End stars helped or performed regularly
at the club, but it had vanished when I returned on leave.

Return of the 'heroes'
Embarkation in the SS Strathmore at Liverpool proved a farcical affair. Within
two hours of marching up the gangplank and down to my hammock station on
Gdeck. I was marched off again (With a thousand more) and Inaded into a com
smothered with DDT. The ship was infested with insects.
The funny side to it all was that every street the trams passed by was having
its trestle table j-Day party, with flags and bunting flying, and, seeing troops
returning from the direction of the docks, we were cheered as homecoming he.
roes rather than a batch of bug-ridden rookies.
With three days to spare, I decided to take a risk and visit my parents with-
out permission. I got a chum to answer to my name at roll call and took a tram
to Lime Street Railway Station. Unfortunately, red caps (military police) were
standing by the ticket collectors at every platform with trains about to depart.
So, quietly walking down an empty platform, I got into a carriage of a station-
ary train and then climbed across to a carriage of the train going my way.
It speaks volumes for the British character that my entrance from the adjoin-
ing train and not from the platform raised not a single eyebrow. Most heads were
stuck in their newspapers as I took the one remaining seat.
The train left on time and the journey was uneventful, but I received a hot
reception at home. My initiative was not appreciated. Not only was I crazy to be
AWOL (absent without leave) but I would be using up father's precious petrol
coupons to take me immediately by car to Liverpool. At least mother made lunch
before we set off.
In fact, it was all a bit of an anticlimax. No one had missed me and father in-
vited my helpful army pal to join us for supper in the city. We drove to the
dockside to take a look at the Strathmore and in the few minutes we were there
a thief broke into the car. Overall, my escapade had been far from successful.
Eventually, we reboarded the Strathmore and sailed via Malta and Taranto to
Port Said with calm seas and glorious weather. It was my first real taste of the
sun and I suppose the beginning of a journey which slowly but surely brought
me to Moyenne.
Egypt, with its new sights, sounds and smells, was totally foreign. I loved it
for Cairo.
180
Cairo capers
The barracks brought me into contact for the first time with what might be termed
tough soldiery. We had little to do other than see our khaki shorts and shirts
tailored' to size by a dozen Egyptians working furiously at somewhat decrepit
sewing machines. Occasional o dhouse duty came round and to help keep
as wide awake there was a library of books, the mildest being Lady Chatterley's
Lover. The others were fairly hard-core pornography, including the escapade
of a flea investigating the anatomy of tWo lovers. Graffiti on the toilet walls was
another eye-opener for a small-town boy.
A large mirror enabled those with passes to adjust their dress under the care-
ful scrutiny of the guard commander. Once outside the Darracks it was a ditterent
world. The trams, with as many passengers hanging onto the extenor as squeezed
iside quickly taught us to forget the British habit of peaceful queuing. Small
boys sporting long white gowns and red fezzes were everywhere and new ar-
rivals with their white knees were fair game for a rip-off.
These rascals would offer to: Clean your shoes, mister?' It seemed ridiculous
bearing in mind the spit and polish shine on our toe caps. But a couple of their
chums would provide a shocking diversion while they threw handfuls of dirt
on our boots... and then we did need the shoeshiners. Sellers of dirty pictures
and others offering their sisters' favours made the Music Calling Club in the city
centre a welcome haven. Besides music, the club produced good food and even
hot baths. Further along the street (then named Sharia Malika Faridha after
Farouk's beautiful queen) was Groppis', internationally famous for its ice-cream
sundaes, cakes and iced coffee! 'Malish' was the popular equivalent to today's
'no problem.
A visit to the Pyramids and the Sphinx at Giza, on the outskirts of Cairo, whet-
ted my appetite for further exploration. I climbed to the top of the biggest
pyramid, took the tour inside, then had my photograph taken on a camel to send
home. After the Cairo stopover I found myself on a train heading for my first
posting in Jerusalem.
For the next four months my billet was a cell in a monastery which the army
had taken over temporarily — a fitting base for a tour of the Holy Land, then
known as Palestine and under British jurisdiction.
There was nothing sacrilegious about taking a donkey as transport to and from
Bethlehem. It was encouraged by the old YMCA in a small square not far from
the Jaffa Gate. My first adventure on the animal proved both hilarious and hor-
rific. As soon as I mounted, the wretched beast took off at a rate of knots and,
keeping to the pavement, ploughed through the neat pyramids of fruit and other
wares, leaving in our wake an enraged group of merchants waving their arms
and shouting obscenities in Arabic. My one worry now was that the donkey
might stop. I needn't have worried. He didn't slow down for half-a-mile and,
by that time, I was well clear of the city and along the rough road to Bethlehem.

I prayed at the Church of the Nativity and Visited many other shrines in both
Bethlehem and jerusalem. A frena took me through Hezekiah's Tunnel and
walked along the Via Dolorosa, the path jesus Christ took to his crucifixion.
used a donkey on a number of occasions when I climbed the Mount of Olives,
looking back to the city, dominated by the magnificent Dome of the Rock.
At the Garden of Gethsemane I was the only visitor. It was very peaceful. 1
had been warned to beware of bandits in the barren hills but only once did 1 meet
a horseman with a rifle and a bullet belt across his chest. He greeted me formally
and returned the way he had come. The old city of Jerusalem fascinated me mos
with its covered souk, busy merchants and the ever-present clink-clink of the
coffee sellers' cups. Only the smell of rotting guavas was offensive and, even
today, I cannot eat the fruit, although there are many trees on Moyenne.
Just before Christmas, 1945, I was posted to a Field Cashier's Unit, made up
of a captain, a sergeant, a driver and a truck. I was to be sergeant and, to my
great delight, we were stationed in Egypt just over the sandhills from the Pyra-
mids of Giza. We were so close, in fact, that the top of the Great Pyramid was
clearly visible from the hut, which served as both cash office and home.
Our work was fairly simple, changing a variety of currencies for military per-
sonnel into Egyptian piastres and cashing pay cheques for local officers. Seeing
their names, I was astonished by the number of titled officers.
Pyramid 'death' ride
Christmas morning was perfect, warm and sunny with a light breeze blowing
from the desert, so I decided to hitch a ride to Mena and hire a horse from the
stables of the hotel of the same name. Little did I realize it would be a ride never
to be forgotten — a ride which brought me close to death.
Certainly my mount appeared docile when trotting up the steep, curving
tarmac road from the hotel to the plateau with the pyramids and the Sphinx
overlooking the Nile Valley and Cairo.
Riding is a pleasant way to explore ruins, less dusty and easier on the feet.
After an hour, I had made a full circuit back to the Great Pyramid and a small
palace used by King Farouk. Then the horse sensed that we were on our way
back, and, as it obviously had not had its breakfast, took off at full pelt. But it
did not use the road. It chose the loose six-foot gravel edging, which dropped
sheer several hundred feet. Had he veered slightly to the right, both of us would
have careered down to a messy heap at the bottom. I hung on for dear life until,
nearing the hotel, the horse leapt back onto the tarmac and slithered on all fours
to a halt, before cantering calmly into the stables. When the stable boy asked if I
had had a nice ride, I told him angrily: Next time feed the brute first!'
Beside the stable was a small shop selling curios and I found the owner in his
flowing galabiyah, waiting for me.
'Please, effendi, come and relax with a coffee. It was quite a ride!' I gladly ac-

On Christmas night I was invited to a neighbouring company of South Afri-

warmth of the main tarmac highway on my bare feet (most of us had removed

traffic thundering past, it was a foolish thing to do and so was the game of
chicken' which developed when we decided that the last person to roll to safety
as a truck past was the winner. God does appear to look after fools and drunks.
The full pomp and circumstance of Arab royalty was seen to advantage when
King Abdel Aziz el-Saud of Saudi Arabia paid a state visit to Egypt. He was
hosted by the young monarch, King Farouk. The roads were sanded for the oc-
casion and even scent had been added to the fuel tanks of Rolls and the
motorcycles of the outriders, leaving the enthusiastic crowds giving a traditional
slow handclap in a perfumed haze rather than choking on petrol fumes. The two
kings and the people of Cairo were in a merry mood. But soon all was to change
-even the street names.
Plans to visit Luxor and Karnak, the Valley of the Kings and the temples at
Abu Simbel had to be scrapped when our unit was posted unexpectedly to Pal-
estine. Sadly, the nearest I got to those architectural wonders was through Agatha
Christie's Murder on the Nile and Ian Fleming's The Spy Who Loved Me.
Assassin at supper
While in Egypt we had to beware of shoeshine boys and pickpockets. It was a
far deadlier enemy we had to contend with in Palestine. The State of Israel was
about to emerge and 'freedom fighters' (or terrorists' depending on your stance),
were an ever-present menace. Steel wires sometimes stretched across roads re-
sulted in an untimely and unpleasant garroting for many an army motorcyclist.
Death lurked in the orange groves and in the city centres.

Because we handled a great deal of money I was not permitted to eat with the

Haifa did not have the same fascination for me as Jerusalem or Egypt but 1
still managed to have a happy year there. At night, particularly from the sea,
Mount Carmel, on which the city is built, sparkled like a diamond with its thu-
sands of lights. Already it was very built up but I enjoyed more 'travels on a
donkey'. I climbed up to the monastery and talked with a Carmelite monk and,
at the small Jewish theatre, attended a performance of Servant of Two Masters,
totally understandable, although it was in Hebrew.
I made friends with both Jewish and Arab families and discovered a Jewish
café which, surprisingly, produced some of the best pork chops I've had in my
life. Another restaurant I frequented for its superb food was very Arab and din-
ers were invited to view the spotless kitchen and choose the 'specialities'. It was
an intimate establishment with seating for twenty.
One visit I will never forget had nothing to do with the food. Just after I had
arrived and was studying the menu a distinguished-looking man in gold-em-
broidered gown and head-dress was bowed to his seat by the proprietor. His
meal arrived immediately and he ate quickly, lingering only over his small cup
of coffee. Then, with a smile and a nod to the other diners he was ushered out
into the street where, a few seconds later, he lay in a pool of his own blood,
gunned down in broad daylight. From where I was sitting I saw it all. We were
asked to carry on normally. It was explained that he was an emir from the north
who had been assassinated for selling land to the Jews. By the time I came to
leave there was no trace of a killing. The police had moved swiftly.
Beirut escapade
After a few months in Haifa I made friends with two sergeants and, over drinks,
hatched a plan to cross the border into Lebanon and visit Beirut and, if possible,
the magnificent ruins at Baalbek. We had heard that the border was about to be
closed and, with a few days to go, filled six steel boxes with outdated books and
papers, used our official red wax seals and piled them into the back of the truck.

With a few hours to spare we recrossed the border and arrived home safely.
Iwonder to this day who opened the official-looking boxes we had deposited in
the army pay office, and what they made of the contents.
Among the services provided by our cash office was a twice-monthly visit to
Tiberias on the Sea of Galilee and just over the Jordan border, to a camp at Mafrak.
The latter recalls plates of excellent eggs and chips. It was a fascinating journey
taking in Nablus, Jenin, Nazareth and many other biblical and historical points
of interest.
Once a week we made the short trip along the northern coast to Acre. It was
while I was standing in the back of the open truck, with a soft warm wind blow-
ing in my face and hair, that I suddenly realized I loved the sun and a warm
climate. It was a moment of such important realization that it influenced the rest
of my days and resulted in my safaris to Kenya and Tanzania and, eventually,
my life on Moyenne.
On another holiday in Cairo I visited the zoo, where an old man told my for-
tune for a few piastres. Stirring the sand with a stick, he said that I would live to
a reasonable age and always be comfortably off, if never rich. It sounded OK
but his last prediction made me laugh with scepticism and the comment: No
way!' With demobilization not even on the horizon there was little chance of my
'travelling across water and seeing my loved ones within three months'. The old
man was adamant, however, and, as I paid him, he assured me: It is written, it
is written.'
And so it was. When I returned to Haifa I found a Middle East Forces Order
which provided for all personnel likely to be spending another year or more in
the command to have a month's holiday with their families in the UK. The home-
ward route was by sea from Port Said to Toulon and across France by train to

Dieppe or Calais. Leave started as you stepped on English soil.
Bearing in mind my present age and financial situation and that wonderfully

The voyage tough the Mediterranean, especially off Crete, was one of the
and other luxuries to take to a much rationed family and then having to make

Charybdis, and saw the quiescent Etna and the active volcano Stromboli. And,
once through the Straits of Bonifacio, separating Corsica and Sardinia, we knew

Arriving at the troop ship in Port Said I had heard my name called by a red
beret already on the gangplank. It was Norman Richardson, son of the Dewsbury
Borough Treasurer and with whom I had gone to school. Naturally, we chummed
up and spent the trip (2,330 miles from Egypt to the UK) together.
Neither of us found Toulon particularly interesting, but everyone we knew
on board was heading for a neighbouring town, Hyeres. Like sheep, we followed.
It turned out to be a red-light district and in no time with a couple of dozen other
uniformed lads we squeezed into a lounge dominated by a red velvet-covered
platform on which the ladies' revealed all. Events became pretty raucous and,
at the point where a soldier, cheered by his chums, leapt onto the stage and a
performer, the military police arrived. Norman and myself dashed down a cor-
ridor through a small door and found ourselves in an alley to freedom. Even the
rough Beaujolais tasted great as we sat in a nearby café and watched trucks take
away most of our erstwhile companions.
History repeated itself a year later in Port Said when I escaped from an Out
of Bounds' club with an army pal by clambering on the toilet and sliding through
a window onto a pile of clanging dustbins in a service alley. This time we had
checked for an escape route and then watched from a first-floor restaurant op-
posite the club as some of our less fortunate friends were taken away by the
military police.
Stolen wine and cigars
The train ride across France was uneventful and the crossing of the English Chan-

when I arrived in Dewsbury. So I went next door and stayed with the mother of

My month in the UK was soon over and the way back followed the same route.

Orders came to pack our belongings and travel down to Egypt in the truck.
We were to spend a wonderful night under the stars in the Sinai Desert. It was
crisp, clear and never to be forgotten.
Bomb for breakfast
Like my father, I believe that fate plays a tremendous role in life. He used to tell
a story from the trenches in the First World War which convinced him of this.
With a chum he was allowed a day to relax and they found a farm-restaurant
and inadvertently ordered 12 eggs and chips each. After their huge meal they
returned to the trenches. My father said to his friend: 'See you tomorrow. My
father turned right, his chum turned left — and immediately had his head blown
off. I remembered father's story the day after I left Haifa.
Regularly, for almost a year I walked along the road to the police mess for
breakfast, arriving exactly at eight o'clock and returned to the cash office around
8.30am. But because we had a long road journey ahead this particular morning
I took my breakfast an hour earlier and we set off for Port Fouad thirty minutes
later. By eight we were too far along the coastal road to hear the massive explo
sion which shattered the police mess, killing or injuring my former breakfast
companions. We read of the outrage in the Egyptian Mail on reaching our desti-
nation. The explosive had been concealed in a barrel trundled under the first-floor
mess. Yes, I was lucky again.
My Palestine souvenir, a polished olivewood box, bearing a bronze relief map
of the country, contains the blue and green medal for services in Jerusalem and
Haifa.

Port Fouad and Port Said were good places to relax. There were sandy beaches
and an unpolluted Mediterranean stretch in which to swim, although we were
never tempted to try the so-called Sweet Water Canal.
The horses were well-behaved and riding provided a pleasant pastime, par-
ticularly along the north coast beach where many wealthy Egyptian families had
holiday properties. A score of good restaurants and bars catered for all needs
and a small theatre was privileged to be visited by such West End stars as John
Gielgud, Joan Greenwood, Kay Hammond and John Clements. The perform-
ances I remember were: The Importance of Being Earnest, Journey's End and Private
Lives.
The Transit Camp was run by a guards colonel before whom I had to appear,
having lost my AB64 (paybook). I had handed in my clothes to the soldier in
charge of the army-run beach and the paybook was missing when the clothes
were returned. I received a reprimand, the only blemish on an otherwise spot-
less military career.
Eventually, I changed my own piastres into sterling and returned by ship and
train to the UK and was demobbed with a not-very-well-fitting suit at York. My
parents drove up to that most beautiful of English cities to collect me and so
ended another chapter in my life.
The England to which I returned after three-and-a-half years in the forces had
changed considerably. Politics and the emergence of television were the main
topics rather than aspects of survival.
My home in Dewsbury was the same but felt smaller and work as a full-time

In'civvies' and cells
The newspaper office was in the same building as the Conservative Club, of
which I became a member as much for convenience's sake as for politics. Pan-

- viu cula day to stop at the club for a quickle at the bar.
on one occasion, the elderly cleaning lady, Mrs Morley, apprehended me,

remembered that my father's mother had been a staunch supporter of the Tee-

fact. My family had to live with that reputation so it made some sense to travel
a few miles to maintain it. And we were a family of some lineage and tradition.
One of the family trees shows that I am directly descended (over six genera-
tions) from Sir Robert Walpole, England's first prime minister. The name Roberts
in this tree was connected with the church of All Hallows by the Tower, which
became well known as Canon Tubby Clayton's Toc H. Church. Sadly, the re-
mains of my ancestors buried in the aisles and nave were destroyed during the
World War II blitz on London. By strange coincidence, a John Grimshaw was
church warden at All Hallows from 1628 to 1630. The other tree traced my York-
shire line back to 1583.
One fundamental aspect of newspapermen is their association with the po-
lice. My links with the law were pleasant and useful and even a couple of hours
in the cells at the Dewsbury Central Police Station (later to be occupied by the
Yorkshire Ripper) did not change matters. It was my fault; I asked to see a fox
which had been caught and 'detained', and rather foolishly admitted I had never
been in their cells. A wink from the chief constable at our daily ten o'clock brief-
ing resulted in the sergeant turning the key and letting me cool my heels
alongside the fox. The next time I was 'detained' was in the VIP lounge at Mi-
ami Airport forty years later.
It was roughly the same time gap before I heard again from a former fellow
reporter, Jimmy Nicholson. He was a likeable, eccentric bloke with a good nose
for news which led him to become one of the top crime reporters in Britain, earn-
ing the title of Prince of Darkness, an epithet due more to his habit of wearing a
black cloak than to his character.

General Sir Roy Bucher, the society's national and Yorkshire chairman, and

me to call at a chemist's and buy a tube of phospherine tablets. I had to swallow
half a dozen with a glass of Burgundy a few minutes before my address. The
trick worked then and later. Today it is not necessary as I'm told I talk too much.
Village called Brendon
During my English sojourn I pursued another pastime which has always given
me pleasure — touring by car. By the time I left for Kenya I had visited much of
my native land. I recall the familiar smell of wood smoke in the Cotswolds and
remember vividly on one occasion seeing a signpost pointing to Brendon. It was
irresistible and I drove down a country lane to discover the village whose name
I shared. It was small with an inn called The Staghunters, 'renowned for hunt-
ing, shooting and fishing'. There were Brendon Hills and a Brendon Common.
It was summer and still daylight when I was welcomed by the landlord who
told me to 'wash up' while he lashed up' a meal. I remember it well: a thick rasher
of ham, two eggs and a pile of mushrooms followed by a handsome wedge of
apple pie and Devonshire cream. I also recall the bill: just four shillings. Yes, it
was a long time ago.
After supper I moved into the bar and sat quietly in a corner viewing the scene.

Throughout my days in Kenya and Tanzania I was involved with the East Af-
rican Safari rally. It is one of the world's toughest events, with usually dry and
dusty conditions at the coast and often torrential rains and thick mud on the
mountainous stretches. Three names come to mind: Bert Shankland and Joginder
Singh, both winners, and Jimmy Feeney, who enjoyed nothing better than to
check the route. Sadly, his car ran into poles sticking from the rear of a lorry
parked without lights and he was killed instantly.
Our newspapers produced hefty supplements on the Safari within a few days
of the event. It was a mammoth task to collate the story and photographs from
all the sections of the route. But it was exciting and fun even if it meant a couple
of sleepless nights.
Goodwill 'spies'
Easily my most tiring and worrying drive was the 1,000-miles from Dar es Sa-
laam to the Zambian capital of Lusaka. It was a reciprocal goodwill public
relations visit. My companions were two young Tanzanians making their first
trip outside the country and we had with us four cases of a new gin-type drink
called Konyagi as promotional gifts.
The first small problem on the two-day drive arose at Tunduma when we
found our accommodation had been taken over by the staffs of President Nyerere
and President Kaunda, who were holding an unscheduled meeting in the bor-
der town. We finally managed to find comfortable beds for the night and took
on extra fuel before crossing the border as we were warned of shortages on the
Zambian side.
The second problem was an unaccommodating customs officer who insisted
on our paying a heavy duty on the Konyagi. His price would have taken all our
Zambian currency and we were forced to leave the gifts at the customs post. It
was not an auspicious start to a goodwill visit.
The tarmac road was smooth and almost straight for mile after mile through
flat uninteresting bush country. My new worry was not to fall asleep at the wheel

Looking back on that trip, I recall that entry into Rhodesia on a previous oc-
casion had been a less than pleasant experience. On my round-Africa voyage
aboard the Kenya Castle, I had learned, on arrival in the Mozambican port of Beira,
that we were to be delayed for five days.
The Union Castle agent had introduced me to a 'spiv' with long side-whiskers
and a pencil-thin moustache who said he could get me a visa and a rail ticket
from Beira to Salisbury (now Harare) if I let him have my passport and a fiver.
The train left at 6pm and, when at 4pm the cruise ship raised her gangplanks
to move from the bunkering berth, I got worried. But as the minutes ticked away
I was told that a man on the jetty was shouting my name. It was 'Mr Slick' and
he was waving my passport and urging me to leave the ship. The only way was
down the pilot ropes into a small boat. The captain gave me permission 'at your
own risk.
It proved tricky to clamber along the iron girders of the bunkering jetty, es-
pecially as I had gripped the pilot ropes too tightly, scorching my hands in the
process. An old black car was waiting and I was dropped off next to my railway
coach with half an hour to spare.
I thought Mr Slick had done well. Not only was there a visa in my passport
but I had a bunk in an air-conditioned compartment. He went off happily with
his tip and I settled down on my bunk — where I was rudely disturbed by an
194
elderly man who asked: What the hell are you doing in my cabin!'
He had booked the same berth and was furious. He called the train controller
and demanded that 1 be removed. It was a difficult situation, especially as the
rain was full. I had noticed that the man spoke with a Yorkshire accent and so,
in an attempt to defuse the tension, I asked him where he came from. But there
was no appeasing this arrogant tyke. T'm from Richmond, he snorted, 'and l
expect to get what I've paid for. Fellow Yorkshireman you may be, but out you
go!' I would rather have slept in the corridor than share a berth with such a man.
Luck took a hand yet again when I was hailed by two ex-passengers from the
Kenya Castle, an architect called Darrel Quelch and his wife. They came to my
rescue by arranging for me to share a friend's accommodation. We pooled our
food and drinks and celebrated with a jolly party. Events improved further when
I was invited to leave the train at Umtali and drive with my recent friends from
the Rhodesian border to Salisbury and spend the night before flying back to Beira.
Darrel even took time off to show me the sights of what is now Harare. I doubt
I would recognize much of Zimbabwe today.
Surprise Thank you'
Visitors to my island home often comment on the Zanzibar chests and the origi-
nal oil paintings, mainly of Maasai scenes. One picture of two young warriors is
outstanding and its title, 'Six flies', has everyone counting those omnipresent
pests to the tribe and their herds.
My favourite painting shows a group of Maasai women seated in the fore-
ground with the top of the picture merely shades of green to convey a feeling of
the plains where their menfolk were tending cattle or hunting. It is special for
me for personal reasons. During the stressful time in Tanzania when my news-
paper was taken over by the government, I received a phone call from the artist
LM Patel. He asked if I could visit his studio and select paintings for an exhibi-
tion. His request came at an awkward moment but I slipped away.
'First, he said, 'choose the painting you like best to be the centrepiece of my
show.' I pointed to Women awaiting the return of their menfolk'. It's yours,
said the artist. 'I want you to accept it as appreciation of the encouragement you
have given to Tanzanian artists during your editorship'
It was a wonderful gesture and I returned to the office with a happier heart.
My art collection includes several samples of LM's work. When I last heard of
him, he was in New York.
A Belgian named simply as Moto is represented by a picture of Kenya's Lake
Elmenteita and a more brooding work, 'Forest Meeting', deep in Ituri, conjures
up elephants and pygmies.
The Zanzibar chests are exceptional, having been made during and immedi-
ately after the 1964 revolution. Unlike the smooth and highly polished Omani
boxes from the Gulf, these are made from old teak doors and furnishings, deeply

Death squads
Not all my recollectons of lanata and canzibar are happy ones and preclude
what would be premature publication. I record just one sinister incident which
serves to illustrate what was a savage situation. The Zanzibari revolution was a
bloody business with the paying off of old scores, revenge, torture and killings.
The island's first prime minister for just four weeks spent the next four years
Shamte.
in prison or 'protective detention on mainland Tanzania. He was Sheikh Abdul
One of his sons, Hafiidh, joined me in Dar es Salaam on The Standard. On
hearing that he had been marked down to be kidnapped and returned to Zanzi-
bar for torture and subsequent death, we had to act quickly to effect his escape.
Hafiidh was sent by bus along the north coast to Tanga and across the border
into Kenya. In Dar it was announced that he was on assignment in northern
Tanzania.
The ruse worked and teleprinter messages from our offices in Mombasa and
Nairobi informed us of his successful escape. After some time with the East Af-
rican Standard, Hafiidh switched to a publishing firm and is now happily married
and the father of four daughters.
There was one pleasant postscript to the affair. My father received in England
a surprise gift of three fine Zanzibar stools from the imprisoned ex-prime min-
ister and when I passed through Mombasa on my way to Seychelles, Hafiidh
travelled down from Nairobi to say goodbye. He brought with him a large drum
which today is the centrepiece on my verandah and a constant reminder of a
fine family and a grim story with a happy ending.
Another tale which ended happily concerns my Seychelles friend Dudu. He
was born in Zanzibar and was a teenager at the time of the revolution. Realizing
he was in danger, having mixed with the island 'elite', Dudu swam two miles
out to sea to join the yacht on which the sultan and his family were sailing into
exile.
Dar es Salaam lived up to its Arab meaning — haven of peace — when Presi-
dent Nyerere allowed the sultan to stay temporarily in the capital after he had
been refused permission to land at Mombasa. It was ironic that, only a few weeks
before, the whole of Kenya's coastal strip had 'belonged' to the sultan who had
waived his ownership rights at Kenya's independence.
Dudu returned briefly to Zanzibar before travelling with another friend, Praful
Mulji, to a new life in Seychelles. Their benefactor had been Eric Stiven, formerly
administrator-general on Zanzibar and a High Court judge in Seychelles. To-
196
day, Dudu is a successful businessman but rarely talks of those revolutionar
As an editor in Tanzania, Lanzibar was in my parish and l visited the islands
regularly to assess the situation and meet President Abeid Karume. On one oc-
casion the president and I weredudt alone and to illustrate a point I knelt on the
floor, drawing imaginary Inow to represent closer thinking and co-operation.
emember the ADC s astonishmered at this breach of protocol. But the president
had more in common with fishing and too little education to be bothered about
such niceties. We fell out when he asked why I didn't publish The Standard in
Swahili. My response — For the same reason the party newspaper, The Nation-
alist, prints in english — was perhaps rasn, especially as tanzanian second
Vice-President Rashidi Kawawa was with us. But Karume was assassinated a
few weeks later.
My strangest night on Zanzibar followed a meeting with the younger brother
of an island minister. I had been drinking quietly at the former yacht club when
a young man came over and asked me to join him on Zanzibar's equivalent of a
British 'pub crawl'.
The 'pubs' were in tall Arab houses along the dark, narrow streets of the old
town. Hard boiled eggs, liberally sprinkled with paprika, and large bottles of
Amstel were the order of the day.
At what had once been an excellent sports club, I was introduced to the 'gen-
eral', a large man in army uniform, drinking heavily and attended by three young
bodyguards. He was sitting in a deck chair in the middle of a tennis court re-
ceiving homage from acolytes and anyone else. The atmosphere was seriously
unnerving and I was glad to escape with a handshake and a beer.
The highlight of the evening was 'The Africa Dance'. I expected flimsily-clad
maidens gyrating for the soldiery. What we got was 200 men!
'Let's dance, said my companion. There was no way out and we danced for
ten minutes before adjourning to the bar. While in the middle of the floor, two
plain clothes security men approached us. But after a brief word from my fel-
low dancer they apologized and disappeared.
The next morning, while I was packing to leave Zanzibar Hotel, my 'pub crawl'
friend appeared with a tray and two beers just for the road'. I could never make
up my mind whether the minister's young brother had been assigned to keep
an eye on me or if it was the sparking of a genuine friendship. We didn't meet
again so I never found out.
Zanzibar Hotel brings back many memories. It was a very old Arab-style build-
ing with a magnificent studded door framed by potted palms. The reception area
had fine Arab furnishings, including a highly-polished chest. Rooms were
spartan but had tremendous atmosphere.
On one visit I invited the deputy British High Commissioner to lunch. After
the usual greetings, we settled down to talk, but I realized that the man sitting
opposite within hearing distance was not turning the pages of his wide-open

'Kill' Grimshaw
Dealings with the government during those times could be difficult. While my
relations with State House were always correct I had many 'run-ins' with lower
seats of power, particularly with one regional commissioner. He had a knack of
making explosive decisions without any thought for the consequences. Take the
case of the water carriers. Most of Dares Salaam did not have piped water and
many families relied on men who made a modest living by carrying small trol-
leys filled with cans of water from public taps to their homes.
The commissioner, who lived in affluent Oyster Bay with all mod cons, de-
cided the water carriers were leeches on society, obtaining money for the sale of
free water. So he banned them. The result would have been chaos with families
having to join huge queues to be able to wash or even cook an evening meal. So
we ran a strong editorial in The Standard and the decision was reversed. But it
did not please the commissioner.
Soon after this the commissioner acted hastily again, this time to cut down on
vagrancy and crime in the city. He decided everyone must have an identity card
with a photograph and gave just forty-eight hours as the deadline before the po-
lice would start rounding up those without them. With limited printing works
available and negligible photographic facilities, the exercise was impossible.
Workers were being picked up on their way to shops and offices and it took
another editorial in The Standard to halt the idiocy. The commissioner was furi-
ous and had me rounded up by the police and arraigned in front of himself and
the regional office staff. The farcical 'court' was halted by a VIP telephone call to
the commissioner after which he offered me a Cuban cigar, dismissed the sta
and walked me to my car. We agreed that co-operation was essential and to avoid
future problems his press officer would keep in contact.
More difficult to deal with were the political hotheads, the Party Youth League
They also dreamed up some wild schemes. Perhaps the worst was the banning
of mini-skirts and bellbottom trousers. To entorce the ban, you leaders car-
ried rulers to measure girls skirts in the street. In an editorial l warned ot the
obvious dangers of such an operation — angry boyfriends and serious fights.
It gave me no satisfaction to be proved right when a girl was hurt running
away from the ruler brigade'. In a follow-up editorial I suggested that instead
of interfering with the personal liberty of fellow citizens, the Youth Leaguers
should do something more practical by getting their hands dirty and going back
to the land.
The next day the League marched on the newspaper and staged a demonstra-
tion calling for The Standard and its editor to be banned. When they arrived I
offered to go down and meet them but this was vetoed by the police who feared
my appearance might spark violence. So I invited three of the demonstrators to
come to see me. They refused and instead read out a long statement over a loud
hailer. Then they dispersed. By coincidence I recognized one of the demonstra-
tors the next day in our office looking at his photograph which we had printed
as part of our coverage of the event. He grinned at me and we shared a joke. The
day before this he had been chanting 'Chinja Grimshaw!' (kill Grimshaw!) But
there were no real ill-feelings on either side. No wonder I still like the Tanza-
nians.
'King' Leslie
While Tanzania has the most magnificient game parks — Serengeti, Ngorongoro,
Lake Manyara and the Selous, to name a few — they are all a considerable dis-
tance from Dar and, for relaxation, I found it necessary to acquire a fast boat
which I moored off the old Yacht Club in the main harbour.
Water skiing was the favourite pastime and, with friends, I recall skimming
over creek waters with a family of hippos below me. We found it great fun also
to speed out through the narrow entrance to the harbour and visit the handful
of islands about four miles offshore. It was possible to ski all the way back, al-
though at times like riding a roller coaster, and resulting in protesting leg muscles
the next day.
Realizing now how much islands have fascinated me, surprisingly I never
made it to Lamu with its Arab ambience or Mafia with its reputation for big game
fishing.

Diplomatic tourism
As a national newspaper editor, I found it vital to keep close contacts with the
diplomatic corps and this proved especially useful when I decided to extend what
began as an inaugural flight by East African Airways to Hongkong, and a week
in the colony as an official guest, by visiting Japan. The Japanese ambassador,
with whom I lunched each month, arranged for a visa and many other privi-
leges, although I was not made aware of this until I landed in Tokyo.
And, to this day, I say the Soviet ambassador was the most efficient tour op-
erator I have ever met. Looking at the world map on my office wall I had realized,
first of all, how close Japan was to Hongkong and then, secondly, that it was
only a short hop to the Soviet Union and the eastern end of the Trans-Siberian
Railway. Like the Amazon trip much later, I had always wanted to make that
week-long train journey.
Within forty-eight hours of asking if it was possible to visit the Soviet Union,
I had a berth on a Russian boat from Yokahama to Nahodka, a train ticket to
Khabarovsk, a berth on the Trans-Siberian Express, accommodation in Moscow,
a flight to Leningrad and finally a flight via Copenhagen to London. And, it all
worked perfectly. Unfortunately, timing cut my stay in Japan to only five days
but all had their memorable moments.
An old friend from Seychelles, Russ Thompson, who ran Kai Tak Airport at
Hongkong, asked me to bring some avocado pears with me on the inaugural
200
flight. Stepping into the terminal building. I was approached by a white-suited
youngster who asked politely if I had brought a parcel for Mr Thompson. Whe
I said 'yes', he took my passport and was back a few minutes afterwards to ac
company me to the waiting coach without any further formalities. Some twenty
minutes later the rest of the party emerged and one of the ministers asked what
magic whisked me through airports. I told him: avocado pears!
I dined with Russ and his wife at their flat in the Albany, but I don't recall if
avocados were on the menu. We sampled all the Hongkong sights and sounds
from floating restaurants to The Peak, which was, surprisingly, cold. A good
friend, Dick Toplis (well known for his tennis in Dar and Nairobi), and I opted
for a sauna and massage and wondered if we looked worse than we felt when
oxygen cylinders were wheeled alongside our beds! Plates of sliced oranges
placed on our chests gave the hint that it was 'all part of the service.
Slipping away from the group for a day, I took a hydrofoil to Macau and en-
gaged a friendly taxi driver to show me the town. It was a trip I was to make 20
years later with Man Friday' Rene. I bought some small jade and ivory antique
carvings from a back street shop which remain on my Moyenne memory table
today. The Street of the Sing-Song Girls was empty (apparently they had all gone
to Hongkong where the trade was better), apart from a shop which dealt in live
animals, snakes, dogs, foxes etc., for Chinese cuisine. What the driver and I ate
for lunch goodness knows but every dish was delicious. I was the only non-Chi-
nese in the restaurant, which I took as a compliment to the food.
The return trip by ferryboat provided free beer and a stage show to encour-
age trade rapidly moving away to the hydrofoils. The show was made up of
cameos, one of which I will never forget. The curtains parted to disclose a bam-
boo cage containing a middle-aged man in tiny leopard-skin briefs. He was
snarling and scratching furiously at the bamboo. Then a young Chinese girl
appeared and draped herself over the cage and he snarled even more. Finally a
small boy brought on a notice which read: Beauty and the Beast'. The curtains
closed, to much applause.
Japanese generosity
The official visit to Hongkong ended with a gala luncheon and, as champagne
toasts were raised, I collected my luggage and took a taxi to the airport, arriving
with less than half-an-hour to spare. When checking in they asked if I would
like to travel first class. 'Yes, I said, but, unfortunately, I'm in economy. They
replied: Well, we would like you to travel first class' and upgraded my ticket. It
was a good start.
The food and service were excellent and the passage through Tokyo Airport
very smooth. On reaching reception I was surprised to see a man wearing a huge
chrysanthemum badge with my name on it. A limousine was waiting to take
me to a hotel in the brightly-lit Ginza district and the chauffeur informed me

tiny mushrooms and other vegetables, passing them to our plates with his chop-
sticks. The sauces were fabulous as was the hot rice wine constantly topped up
by kimono-clad girls. It remains one of the finest eating experiences of my life.

tinged clouds. It was quite beautiful and by sighting it I could now claim to have
been a 'good boy.
Catching another fast train, I arrived in Yokahama and went to the port where

'Russia wins'
The ladies who handled my suitcases were blonde and large. I shared my cabin
with a reman retaling home er studying acupuncture in Japan. His

The ship's departure was a colourful affair with thousands of paper ribbons

viet Union was the cheapest route to Europe.
representing no less than 17 countries. The ship and flight or train across the So-
My French cabin companion and I were joined by a Yugoslav for drinks at
the bar and meals. Vodka was a must as the beer had a burnt-sugar taste which
made it far too sweet. On that first night on board the captain made his appear-
ance. A huge man, somewhat the worse for vodka, he crashed open the swing
doors and came straight towards us.
He asked the Frenchman where he lived, and on being told Paris', gave him
a tremendous bear hug and called out: 'Vive de Gaulle!' Looking decidedly dis-
concerted, my French chum pointed at me and yelled: 'Anglais, Anglais! The
resultant bear hug, to the cry of 'Winston Churchill!', was such that he lifted me
off my feet and gashed my forehead on the iron ceiling. There was quite a lot of
blood but the captain did not seem to notice. He moved on to similar greetings
with other passengers, leaving them in no doubt as to his political feelings to-
wards their countries. The Yugoslav, for example, received just a formal
handshake.
Unfortunately, it was not the last I was to see of the captain that night. He
returned to the bar and, slapping me on the back, demanded: We go play games
on deck!' What games at night in a heavy sea I could not imagine. I beckoned
the Frenchman to follow and we clambered up the companionway and arrived
at the 'tennis court', an area surrounded by thin rope netting. The captain
switched on floodlights and produced racquets and balls. The game was hilari-
ous. Every time he hit the ball in my direction he tumbled over, ending in a heap
of netting. With the roll of the ship and quite a few vodkas I did the same. We
were exhausted after ten minutes and the Frenchman wisely called to me: Let
I missed the next ball purposely and shouted: 'Russia wins!' 'Yes, yes!' cried
the captain, 'Russia wins!' and he ambled off happily to his cabin after bidding
us goodnight.
It wasn't exactly Wimbledon but it was memorable. There were two very ob-
vious KGB men on the ship the first morning at sea. I met them in the corridor
outside my cabin.
They showed a keen interest in the book I was carrying, a James Bond adven-
ture, and asked to borrow it for the day. I did not mind as in this book Bond was

It was misty with a slight drizzle as we docked at Nohodka and bought bot-
tles of vodka before boarding the train for an overnight run to Khabarovsk, where
most passengers were to take Aeroflot flights to Moscow. Only four non-Sovi-
ets were on the Trans-Siberian Express: a young Australian merchant navy officer
travelling to meet his Japanese fiancée in Helsinki and marry later in Devon; a
Japanese professor of metallurgy heading for Birmingham University, his wife
and myself.
The trip to Khabarovsk was great fun. No one slept. There were a couple of
guitarists strumming away, and singing echoed along the length of the train. The
bottles of vodka were empty on arrival.
Siberian 'quacks quacks'
Stepping from the train, I was met by a young student who was my Intourist
guide for the half-day before the express was due to leave. It was fine weather
and the foot tour of the town and parks was extensive and exhausting. Stores
had little to offer and no souvenirs. I had my first borsche, a thick beetroot soup,
and found my guide more interested in me and my travels and keen to learn all
he could of the Western way of life.
I was travelling long before the end of the Cold War and the easing of ten-
sions between East and West. In fact, I was something of a novelty all along the
route. The guide had been careful to praise everything and a good friendship
had been established when he settled me in my train. The coach was very old
and the furnishings heavily Victorian or Tsarist.
There were four bunks in the compartment with a table and a bedside lamp
under the wide window, from which I was able to see mile after mile of Dr
Zhivago country, snow, ice and silver birch trees, and later the vast muddy-col-
notony, gave a chance to stretch one's legs and provided a service to the otherwise
isolated towns and villages. Horse transport was much in evidence and the roads
looked rough and often rutted.
A samovar dispensed good tasting tea in each coach and a man passed up

My companions for the early part of the trip were two silent Russians and I
was glad to discover the young Australian only a compartment away. Together
we decided to have ainner in the restaurant car and were relleved to see the menu
was in English as well as Russian. We opted for chicken but that was as far as
we got. The elderly waitress, in a little black dress, a white apron and a frilly
Chicken'.
cap, had mislaid her spectacles and it proved useless to point to Dish No 4,
In desperation, I waved my arms and made 'cluck, cluck' and 'cock--doo-
dle-do' noises. The effect was not what I had intended. Turning to the other diners
in the packed restaurant car, the waitress let fly a flood of Russian words quite
clearly telling them what a bad man this foreigner was, making fun of an old
woman merely trying to do her job. From the sullen looks and nods of agree-
ment, I decided not to try again and we returned to our compartments.
But, Pirovskis for dinner, breakfast and lunch made us decide on another at-
