wolves. This time we decided to eat duck. Inevitably, it was the same waitress
... still without her spectacles. Even if she could not read the menu she certainly
recognized the two of us.
I decided to risk it again: 'Quack, quack, I went, waving my arms and point-
ing to the menu. Then, like dawn breaking over the Russian steppes, a big smile
appeared on the waitress' face. She also started to 'Quack quack' and flap her
arms. My Australian friend got into the act and did the same. The waitress, turn-
ing to the other diners, this time in good spirits, explained with many 'quack
quacks' and 'cock-a-doodle-dos' the previous evening's misunderstanding. To
a coach full of bird noises we were served with very large portions of roast duck.
Never let anyone say that the Russians have no sense of humour. 'Quack quack'
became the usual greeting whenever we met other passengers.
We found it unnecessary to moo for the beef as the waitress put full plates
before us proudly stating 'root boof'. It was an experience best not repeated but
worth the telling, rather like the entire journey to Moscow.
The wrong door
The compartments did not have windows opening onto the corridor so it was
inevitable during a week that I would eventually slide open the wrong door.
Accompanied by the Australian, I did just that and found myself watching the
Japanese couple in national dress performing the tea ceremony against the pass-
ing background of snow and silver birch trees. Greatly embarrassed, I went to
close the door but the couple insisted we take part. Thus I drank a perfect cup of
Japanese tea in the heart of Siberia.
Halfway along the line, my early companions left the train and I was joined
by a young Soviet soldier and his very pregnant wife. By sign language I made
it dear that they should take the two lower bunks and I would relax topside
My offer of a cigar was quickly accepted but the soldier first locked the com
partment door. Later, he produced a half bottle of ninety-six proot vodka which
was diluted with water but downed in two gulps, to 'Cheers' and 'Na zdorovie
It was the only vodka I had on the train as spirits were banned to prevent drunk
enness. both the beer and the wine on the tan were sicker oy sweet and to
cros Siberia without a tot of vodka did not seem right.
The fourth bunk was taken for one night by another silent Russian, who was
reading a large leather-bound book and did not appear to sleep at all. Just after
he had joined us I noticed the young soldier point a finger towards him and then
put it against his lips. We were not to talk and later I understood our new com-
panion was a KGB man. My second encounter.
The soldier was intrigued by my lighter and I was glad to give it to him. His
reaction, however, puzzled me. He sat on the edge of his bunk for several min-
utes with an unhappy look on his face before, suddenly, opening his suitcase
and producing a small penknife. It was his return gift — which is still with me
on Moyenne today.
On the ship and the train I found ordinary Soviets were no different from other
human beings anywhere else in the world. Even in the middle of the Cold War
they had warm hearts. On arrival in Moscow I was again met by an Intourist
guide and taken to a hotel resembling a large wedding cake across the river from
the Kremlin. It was there I encountered the only serious problem on the trip. The
large Russian receptionist flipped though her booking list and pronounced
firmly: 'No reservation, no room. I protested, but after a second check she re-
peated even more strongly: 'No reservation, no room
Iasked the Intourist manager if I was expected to sleep by the river. After all,
Ihad been met by an Intourist representative at the railway station and taken to
that hotel. There must be a reservation. If not I would fly to Copenhagen and
spend the remainder of my holiday in Denmark.
Within ten minutes I was back at reception to confront the same large lady.
This time, with a smile, she said: 'A reservation and a room!' It was a dark, heavily
furnished room but the cafeteria was conveniently situated on the same floor.
The dry atmosphere on the train and the lack of exercise had resulted in my
contracting catarrh and constipation which were difficult to explain to the young
blonde lady doctor in the hotel — but I managed. My Australian friend was di-
agnosed as having pneumonia, although it did not prevent him from
accompanying me to a superb performance of Gounod's Romeo and Juliet in the
Hall of the People inside the Kremlin. We had been given dress circle seats and
during the intervals took the escalators to the long top floor where, on rows of
white linen-covered tables, a wide range of ready-poured drinks were available
all at one rouble each.
After a spell in Moscow, I flew to Leningrad for a couple of days, taking in

Berlin Wall
My next glimpse behind the Iron Curtain' was by courtesy of the West German
Government. As a member of an official Tanzanian group, I had joined the in-
augural Lufthansa flight from the three East African capitals (Dar, Nairobi and
Kampala) to Frankfurt. It had been a memorable flight for the lavish hospitality
provided by the airline, so many of us having to decline champagne and caviar
in the early hours.
Unfortunately, fog caused the plane to divert to Stuttgart, where jugs of fresh
orange juice and champagne proved very acceptable 'uplifters' for our onward
flight to Berlin. We arrived at the hotel with an hour to change and be welcomed
with cocktails and dinner — the start of a hectic week's sightseeing and official
receptions.
It was a bitterly cold morning when we were taken to view The Wall. Look-
ing at that monument of evil I could not help feeling that it was bound to fall. I
had been on both sides and witnessed the standards of living and freedom and
lack of it. I knew also of the Germans' deep-rooted desire for unification. It was
only a matter of time. In fact, it was another two decades before hands and bull-
dozers did the job.
I was glad to be wearing the astrakhan hat which I'd bought in Moscow and
found necessary to keep my ears from freezing as we progressed through Co-
logne, Bad Godesberg, Bonn and finally Frankfurt.
Inevitably, there was a funny 'happening'. We had taken tea with Herr Baron
in his schloss overlooking the Rhine and were due to be received by a senior min-
ister at a reception in Bonn. Travelling in five Mercedes limousines with
blue-lamp-flashing Volkwagens fore and aft, I noticed that we appeared to have
passed the same red bridge, with a large poster advertising a popular brand of
petrol, a couple of times. Noting a parked bicycle and a dustbin, I was pleased
to confirm our third passage under the bridge. On the fifth circuit, I asked the
208
driver why we were going in circd's. He laughed, saying I was too observant
and we were 'killing time' as we could not arrive before the minister at the re-
ception. understood but did not think the other at sers would be pr

happy with the antics of the flashing light cavalcade. After six viewings of the

Well, they were certainly hospitable and I did acquire a taste for some of their
excellent wines which to this day I enjoy on my verandah on Moyenne.
Gun in my luggage
Having left Rene, to hold the fort during my travels, I decided it was time, in
October 1991, for him to make his first trip overseas. With friend Dudu, we flew
to Singapore, Bangkok, Hongkong and Beijing where a mutual friend, Tan
Darnbrough, was running a magnificent hotel in the centre of the Chinese capi-
tal. A hydrofoil to Macau and a coach to Malaysia made it six countries in four
weeks.
Rene quickly adjusted to glass doors opening at his approach, escalators and
underground railways, but he was decidedly unhappy on his first acquaintance
with the cable car from Singapore to Sentosa Island and the crazy tuktuks in Bang-
kok.
Apart from China, these were return visits for me but it was fun to share past
experiences with my friends. The Great Wall, five-snake soup and singing My
Way in a Beijing karaoke bar to a Chinese audience were among the highlights.
Inevitably, there was a problem. This time it was a revolver which could have
landed all three of us in a Thai prison. We had arrived early at Bangkok Airport
for our flight to Hongkong and found the check-in counter would not open for
at least an hour. The bar was at the opposite end of the main building, so we
decided to put our five bags in the left-luggage office. An hour later we collected
them, checked in the heavy ones and made for the departure lounge.
I had passed the X-ray screening machine when Rene and Dudu called me
back. There was a problem. The security officer pointed to the X-ray of my per-
sonal holdall and quite clearly it showed a revolver. It was a nasty moment.
Then I realized the bag was not mine, just similar in size and colour. Iexplained
this to the officer who opened the bag and retrieved the revolver wrapped in
cardboard and gummed paper. As further proof, a pair of trousers from the bag
were held against my leg and proved to be far too short.
By this time all the other passengers had boarded our plane and I appealed to
the officer to let us go. I assured him he would find my own holdall in the left-
luggage office with just a pair of shoes, a pullover, toilet accessories and dirty
laundry. We were very lucky for he allowed us to catch the plane.
On arrival in Hongkong, a man with a hand-held board bearing my name in-

Rene and I returned to Singapore twice for medical check-up, which pred
satisfactory and we had pleasant times eating oyster pancakes and king sou
prawns at the open-air Newton Market, Mexican food and honey base pe
ribs at Chico and Charlies and crocodile and python roasted with chile and

under the Hyatt Hotel. Photographs with orangutans, chimpanzees and a royal
python at Singapore Zoo were added later to our family album.
Majestic memories
Where next? Rene would like to try his luck at Las Vegas and compare Moyenne
with the Caribbean islands. I still hope to make the Grand Canyon and the
Galapagos Islands. Of course, Europe remains an unopened book for both of us.
I have spent many hours in Orly and Rome airports but never set foot in Paris or
the Italian capital. A one-day stopover in Athens was hardly enough, and ever
beckoning are Florence, Padua, Barcelona, Amsterdam, Vienna and the Alps. A
'roots' trip to the scenes of my youth and early reporting days has been planned
for a long time.
My memory table is almost full but the den has ample room for more souve-
nirs. What those who come after will make of them goodness knows. But the
years go by and soon only the memories will remain.
As Longfellow rightly commented in his Three Friends of Mine, 'Nothing is left
but a majestic memory. I'm sure there is also much truth in the saying: We all
forget more than we remember!
Yes, it's great to travel, to go over those reefs and far away, but it is even bet-
ter to come home and relax on my verandah and know that the world is still a
wonderful place, thank God.

Yorkshire-born Brendon Grimshaw has
spent more than half his life in Africa and the
Indian Ocean Islands, thirty years of them
on his tropical island of Moyenne in Sey-
chelles,
He retired there after an illustrious news-
paper career which began as an apprentice
on his home town newspaper and extended
to the Sheffield Star before he migrated to
Kenya where he worked as features editor
on the East African Standard.
Later he joined Reuters, the world's larg-
est news agency, working as a subeditor in
their head office in Fleet Street, London, UK,
before accepting a senior editorial appoint-
ment on the Tanganyika Standard of which
he eventually became Editor and Director.
Brendon Grimshaw oversaw the Stand-
ard's development into a fully Africanised
Tanzanian national newspaper and subse-
quently became Tanzania's first public rela-
tions consultant.
When he retired to settle on Moyenne he
took with him the good wishes of the entire
nation to which he made vital contributions
in development and understanding.
